** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALI AMINZADEM
File: AXXX XX8 004 - Oklahoma City, OK
May 20, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Arthur Campbell Cooke, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Iran, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s August 4, 2015, decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a brief in opposition to the appeal. Proceedings will be reopened and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel such that his proceedings should be reopened. The Immigration Judge acknowledged that the respondent’s previous counsel did not file the application for relief from removal - specifically, cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a) - within the time frame the Immigration Judge had set (I.J. at 1). However, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent’s own acts contributed to his counsel not filing the application, and therefore, he had not demonstrated that ineffective assistance had prejudiced the outcome of proceedings (I.J. at 2).
 
Upon our de novo review, we determine that the respondent’s proceedings merit reopening. We first note that the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed jointly with the DHS (I.J. at 1). Further, as noted above, the DHS has not filed an opposition to the respondent’s current appeal. Second, the respondent has met the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (I.J. at 2). The respondent’s previous counsel was aware of the filing deadline and did not file the cancellation application within the time frame set by the Immigration Judge or withdraw her representation of the respondent (I.J. at 1).
 
Thus, while we acknowledge the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent was afforded an opportunity to apply for relief, we do not agree that the application was waived, when the respondent affirmatively hired counsel to file the application, and counsel neglected to do so. Finally, the respondent is prima facie eligible for relief from removal and has submitted the relevant application. See Joint Motion to Reopen Proceedings filed June 15, 2015. Under the totality of the circumstances, we agree that the respondent’s proceedings merit reopening and the record should be remanded to the Immigration Judge for a hearing on the respondent’s eligibility for relief from removal under section 240A(a) of the Act. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 3226593 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 3226601 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YAN QING CAO
File: AXXX XX4 953 - New York, NY
May 19, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Chunyu Jean Wang, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 On January 14, 2016, the respondent submitted her third motion to reopen proceedings in which the Board dismissed her appeal on August 16, 2004. The motion will be denied.
 
To the extent that the respondent suggests that the Board committed error in not addressing issues relating to the Immigration Judge’s conduct of the hearing, this aspect of her motion is in the nature of one seeking reconsideration. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Whether treated as a motion to reopen or reconsider, the respondent’s motion is untimely, and as a motion to reopen it is also number-barred as it is her third such motion. Sections 240(c)(6), (7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6), (7); 8 C.F.R. § § 1003.2(b), (c)(2).
 
The respondent contends that reopening and/or reconsideration is nonetheless appropriate in light of misconduct on the part of the Immigration Judge, and as her first attorney gave her erroneous legal advice, preventing her from pursuing derivative asylee status. The respondent also asserts that other attorneys failed to raise or address the issues she now cites in her motion. As she avers that she experienced a gross miscarriage of justice and her due process rights were violated, she asks that proceedings be sua sponte reopened. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
However, the substantive basis for the respondent’s motion is that she was provided ineffective assistance by the numerous attorneys1 who have previously represented her in these proceedings. Despite this assertion, the respondent has not complied, even substantially, with any of the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against any of her various former counsels. Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133, 143 (2d Cir. 2007); Twum v. INS, 411 F.3d 54 (2d Cir. 2005); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). She correctly notes that her first attorney, who prepared her asylum application, was later disbarred (and was expelled from practicing before this Board on May 6, 2002). However, this fact alone does not exempt the respondent from any compliance with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against him or any of her other former attorneys.
 
*2 In this regard we note that her first attorney never appeared in person before the Immigration Judge on the respondent’s behalf. While an associate at his firm did appear on one occasion, that hearing was continued, and the merits of the respondent’s asylum claim were not reached (Tr. at 6-11). Thereafter the respondent appeared with other counsel, not associated with her first attorney’s law firm, with the Immigration Judge noting at the February 15, 2001, hearing that her first attorney had been arrested. Neither he nor anyone from his law firm was representing the respondent at the time of her merits hearing on April 28, 2003. Rather, the respondent had three other attorneys appear on her behalf during the remainder of her hearings (Tr. at 14, 30, 48, 58, 61). The respondent was also represented by three different attorneys during the course of her appeal and two prior motions to reopen. As such, the fact that her first attorney and/or his office was involved at the outset of the respondent’s case does not establish he was ineffective per se and does not excuse her failure to comply with any of the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against any of her subsequent former representatives. Therefore, reopening on this basis is not warranted.
 
As noted, the aspect of the respondent’s motion arguing a gross miscarriage of justice relative to the former-Immigration Judge’s handling of her case is in the nature of one seeking reconsideration. It is untimely by more than a decade. Section 240(c)(6) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). As indicated above, to the extent that the respondent avers that former counsel erred by not raising this issue, she has not complied with the requirements for reopening on such a basis. Twum v. INS, supra. The respondent has not identified any exceptional circumstance preventing her from such compliance, or which otherwise supports sua sponte reopening or reconsideration of her proceedings. Matter of G-D-, supra at 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (“As a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations.”); Matter of J-J-, supra.
 
We further do not find that this case presents a gross miscarriage of justice as alleged by the respondent. We do not condone either the tone or non-judicious comments of the Immigration Judge. However, as the Board specifically declined to join in the Immigration Judge’s frivolous finding, the respondent was not precluded from pursuing adjustment of status, contrary to her arguments otherwise.
 
This case is also distinguishable from the situation presented in Matter of Farinas, 12 I&N Dec. 467, 471-72 (BIA 1967), wherein we found a gross miscarriage of justice. In that case the respondent’s deportation order, which was valid when entered, became invalid by virtue of controlling circuit precedent issued prior to the execution of the order. We further note that another case, Matter of Malone, 11 I&N Dec. 730, 731-32 (BIA 1966), also finding gross miscarriage of justice, is distinct from the respondent’s case as that respondent’s deportation order was clearly not in accord with the law as interpreted at the time of issuance. Neither of these situations is present here. Also, unlike those cases, the respondent’s arguments are largely premised on various claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. Both the circuit where this case arises and the Board have clear precedential authority regarding the requirements for such claims, and the respondent has made no effort to comply with any of the same, nor explained why no action has been taken in this regard.
 
*3 As such, we find that the respondent’s motion is not exempt from the noted statutory bars, and the exercise of our sua sponte authority is not warranted. Therefore, we find that neither reopening nor reconsideration is warranted. Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Hope Malia Holiona
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record indicates that the respondent has had some eight different attorneys representing her during the proceedings below, on appeal, and in the course of her prior two motions (Tr. at 6, 14, 30, 61; Exh. 2 (Form I-589); Notice of Appeal; 10/5/04 Motion to Reopen; 4/2/07 Motion to Reopen).

2016 WL 3226617 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN COX-SANTIAGO
File: AXXX XX0 056 - Chicago, IL
May 18, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
AL Kola, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alexandra Kostich
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 20, 2015, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his October 14, 2014, removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
In his motion to reopen, which was not accompanied by an affidavit, the respondent alleged that his failure to appear resulted because he mistook the hearing date, believing it was on December 15, 2014. The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding that the respondent’s confusion regarding his hearing date did not constitute exceptional circumstances beyond his control that would excuse his failure to appear. See Uriostegui v. Gonzales, 415 F.3d 660 (7th Cir. 2005) (concluding that mishearing the date uttered by the interpreter was nowhere near the illustrative examples of exceptional circumstances given in the statute).
 
On appeal, the respondent now alleges lack of notice and also avers that his prior attorney never informed the respondent of his court date. The respondent did not assert lack of notice in his motion to reopen, and he has not proffered an affidavit or explained why lack of notice was not previously alleged. Counsel’s arguments are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).
 
*2 Ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an exceptional circumstance that excuses an alien’s failure to appear and allows reopening of the removal proceedings and rescission of a removal order. Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472, 473-74 (BIA 1996). However, the alien must satisfy the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) for claiming ineffectiveness of counsel. Specifically, the alien must: “(1) submit an affidavit establishing that she had an agreement with counsel to represent her and detailing its terms; (2) present evidence that she has given notice to her counsel of the ineffectiveness claim and an opportunity to respond to the allegations, and include any response she has received; and (3) if the attorney violated his ethical or legal obligations, show that she has filed a complaint with the governing disciplinary authorities or explain why she has not done so.” Lin Xing Jiang v. Holder, 639 F.3d 751, 755 (7th Cir. 2011) (citing Matter of Lozada). Satisfying the Lozada requirements “““is a necessary condition to obtaining reopening on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel.” Id.
 
Here, the respondent has not identified who his prior attorney was. Nor has he fulfilled any of the requirements necessary for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, supra. Consequently, reopening on this basis is not warranted. See, e.g., Ghaffar v. Mukasey, 551 F.3d 651, 656 (7th Cir. 2008) (“Ghaffar did not comply with any of the Lozada requirements. Consequently, the Board acted well within its rights to deny his motion for remand”); Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498, 504 (7th Cir. 2001) (“[W]e have difficulty understanding how an alien who fails to comply with the Board’s criteria can succeed in challenging its decision”).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 3226617 (BIA)
End of Document

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YEFRI BERTIN SORTO-HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX6 187 - Charlotte, NC
May 6, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jonathan R. Maldonado, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa P. Durant
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 2, 2016, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On January 16, 2015, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed and the respondent waived appeal. On February 1, 2016, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to reopen, requesting sua sponte reopening of proceedings to allow the respondent to apply for asylum, withholding of removal and protection under the Convention Against Torture (“CAT”) (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2-3). The respondent also stated that he may raise a claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel, after obtaining additional information (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 4, fn 3). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposed reopening, arguing that the respondent did not assert exceptional circumstances to justify sua sponte reopening (DHS Opposition to Motion at 4). The DHS further asserted that, to the extent that the respondent was asserting ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent’s motion should be denied for failure to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (DHS Opposition to Motion at 5-6).
 
On February 2, 2016, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion, finding that the respondent did not comply with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent appeals this determination.
 
In his motion to reopen and on appeal, the respondent claims that previous counsel rendered ineffective assistance. Along with his brief on appeal, the respondent has filed new evidence that he claims supports his assertion that his former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel and that his actions prejudiced the respondent (see Respondent’s Brief at 7-9, see also attachments A-D). Among these documents is a letter written by the respondent’s prior counsel stating that although the respondent articulated a fear of return to El Salvador, no asylum application was pursued before the Immigration Court or the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) (see attachment D).
 
*2 The respondent has shown ineffective assistance of counsel, which justifies a remand. Based on the totality of the circumstances, we determine it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge to allow the respondent to pursue an application for asylum and related relief.1 The Immigration Judge should also consider whether, in accordance with section 235(d)(7)(B) of the William Wilberforce Trafficking Victims Protection Reauthorization Act of 2008 (“TVPRA”), Pub. L. No. 110-457, 122 Stat. 5044  (codified at 8 U.S.C. § 1158(b)(3)(C)), the USCIS has initial jurisdiction over any application for asylum and withholding of removal.
 
On remand, the parties should be given the opportunity to present additional evidence and arguments. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The removal proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and entry of a new decision.
 
Margaret M O’Herron
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We emphasize that the Immigration Judge did not err in denying the respondent’s motion to reopen given the evidence before him at the time of his decision.

2016 WL 2848927 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARISTEO SANCHEZ
File: AXXX XX6 457 - Houston, TX
April 29, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rory H. Potter
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on July 9, 2015, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our April 28, 2015 affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s November 5, 2013 decision. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his 2004 removal order based on a change in the law. The respondent now filed a motion to reopen with the Board. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has opposed the respondent’s current motion. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
As the DHS correctly points out, the respondent’s motion, submitted on July 10, 2015, is both untimely and number-barred. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent acknowledges that his current motion is number-barred, but argues that sua sponte reopening is nonetheless warranted on the basis of the ineffective assistance of his attorney. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 219-20 (5th Cir. 2008) (“This court has held that a request for equitable tolling of a time- or number-barred motion to reopen on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is ‘in essence an argument that the BIA should have exercised its discretion to reopen the proceeding sua sponte based upon the doctrine of equitable tolling.”DD’ (internal citation omitted)).
 
The respondent’s attorney’s failure to meet the Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit’s filing deadline for appealing the Board’s decision does not demonstrate a sufficient basis to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings. The respondent did not meet the procedural requirements to claim ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496-98 (5th Cir. 2000) (upholding the Lozada requirements). In fact, the respondent has retained the same attorney for this motion to reopen. The respondent also does not demonstrate that he merits a favorable exercise of our discretion, after previously filing an untimely motion and failing to raise issues before the appropriate adjudicative body, and after we have twice previously declined to exercise our sua sponte authority. See generally Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 2848927 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 2848945 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZENADO RUIZ-NIEVES A.K.A. ZENAIDO RUIZ-NIEVES
File: AXXX XX3 260 - Adelanto, CA
April 27, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven R. Espinoza, Esquire
*1 A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was mailed on December 21, 2015. The appeal was accordingly due on or before January 20, 2016.1 The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was mailed after the due date and filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on March 16, 2016. We conclude that the appeal is untimely.
 
In conjunction with the appeal the respondent files a motion to accept late-filed appeal.2 Counsel states in his February 29, 2016, declaration that his staff did not calendar the deadline to file the Notice of Appeal with the Board and did not bring the Immigration Judge’s decision to his attention until on or about February 9, 2016 (Motion Exh. B). We conclude that the respondent does not show substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to properly allege ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent does not present his affidavit regarding the ineffective assistance of counsel claim. His February 16, 2016, statement that he wants counsel to file an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen leaves many questions unanswered (Motion Exh. C).3 The second requirement is excused because counsel obviously knows about the allegations of ineffective assistance.
 
The respondent did not file a complaint against counsel with California state bar authorities or explain why not. Counsel did not self-report his ineffective assistance to California state bar authorities. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 825-26 (9th Cir. 2003) (failure to file bar complaint not fatal where former counsel submitted letter of self-report to bar); section 6068(o) of the Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code (duties of attorney). When an alien retains prior counsel to pursue an argument of inadequacy of that same attorney, the reporting requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, remain meaningful. It is not for the attorney to decide issues regarding his or her prior conduct. The self-reporting requirement lets a state bar decide and puts the state bar on notice if there are further problems, particularly problems of a continuing nature.
 
*2 We conclude that the respondent does not show substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada and we thus do not consider this appeal by certification. The Immigration Judge’s decision is therefore now final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
Because we are dismissing the appeal as untimely, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See sections 240(c)(6)(A) & (B) and 240(c)(7)(A) & (C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6)(A) & (B) and (c)(7)(A) & (C); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); see Matter of J- J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
In light of the foregoing, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to accept late-filed appeal is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed as untimely.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

2016 WL 2848953 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GERARDO TORRES-HERRERA A.K.A. GERALDO TORRES A.K.A. GERARDO TORRESHERRERA A.K.A. GERARDO TORRESHERRA
File: AXXX XX9 889 - Los Angeles, California
April 26, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vera Alicia Weisz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The proceedings in this matter were last before the Board on October 22, 2015. By a decision of that date, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the decision of the Immigration Judge of April 1, 2014. The Board noted that the respondent had not provided any evidence with his appeal to establish his contention that a conviction had been vacated and did not submit a brief as had been indicated on his Notice of Appeal. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel and seeks the reopening of proceedings and relief from removal. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion.
 
In the proceedings before the Immigration Judge and in the respondent’s appeal to the Board, the respondent was represented by Myava R. Escamilla who was disbarred by the California State Bar on October 16, 2015. Given the facts presented by this case, we find that the respondent has properly asserted a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See generally Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-640 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be granted and the record will be remanded for further proceedings to allow the respondent the opportunity to apply for any relief for which he may be eligible.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 2848953 (BIA)

2016 WL 1722512 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ADRIAN ANGULO-MOTE
File: AXXX XX0 950 - New York, NY
April 4, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Genesis A. Peduto, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 6, 2015, which ordered him removed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but we review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed upon noting that no application for relief was filed and that no relief was available. We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. Although the respondent indicated on the Notice of Appeal that he intended to file a separate written brief or statement, no such submission has been received, despite counsel being granted the requested extension. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(E). In the Notice of Appeal, the respondent states only that there was ineffective assistance of counsel and that counsel failed to file for any relief on behalf of the respondent. However, the respondent does not in any way comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, he has not identified any relief for which he may be eligible. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 1722512 (BIA)

*1 The proceedings in this matter were last before the Board on May 6, 2015, at which time the respondent’s appeal of the decision of the Immigration Judge of September 25, 2013, was summarily dismissed. On August 3, 2015, the respondent filed the timely motion to reopen now before the Board alleging ineffective assistance of prior counsel. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The respondent has substantially complied with the process requisite to asserting such a claim. See generally Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-640 (BIA 1988). The respondent has provided a detailed affidavit describing the claimed ineffective assistance of counsel who is now deceased. Given the affidavit and other evidence presented, we find that reopening should be granted.

2016 WL 1722534 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RICHARD ASARE
File: AXXX XX0 905 - Baltimore, MD
March 31, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gerald Karikari, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Andrew Kowalczyk
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent filed a motion to reconsider, considered timely filed, on January 29, 2016, after we issued a final removal order on December 16, 2015. Section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The motion, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS), will be denied.
 
The Board’s December 16, 2015, order affirmed an Immigration Judge’s decision denying a further continuance of the proceedings. We also upheld the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent had abandoned any application for relief from removal, due to his failure to file the application by the filing deadline. We noted that an Immigration Judge has authority to set deadlines for the applications, and may deem an application abandoned if not filed by a deadline. We noted that the respondent had been in removal proceedings for three years, and was granted six continuances. We agreed with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s desire to further recover from his April 2014 knee surgery did not establish good cause for a continuance. Nevertheless, the respondent did not file any application for relief by the deadline.
 
The pending motion does not establish any error of fact or law in our December 16, 2015, decision, or show that an argument was overlooked. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing the requirements for motions to reconsider). While the motion suggests that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel from his prior attorneys, the assertions meet none of the procedural requirements for making such a claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent claims that his hearing was not fair, in that he was not provided a Twi interpreter. This claim was not made on appeal to the Board. Matter of Cervantes, 22 I&N Dec. 560, 561 n.1 (BIA 1999)(declining to address an issue not raised by party on appeal).
 
In any event, despite the respondent’s belated suggestion that he received ineffective assistance of counsel, or should have been provided with an interpreter, he does not show prima facie eligibility for any form of relief from removal. Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247, 251 (BIA 2007). While the respondent says that he is pursuing a visa application as a widower, no evidence of any such application is presented. Additionally, the Board in visa petition proceedings affirmed the DHS’ decision that the respondent failed to establish that his marriage to his now-deceased wife was legally valid.
 
*2 The respondent’s case also does not represent an “exceptional situation”, warranting reopening under our sua sponte authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
The pending motion will, therefore, be, denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2016 WL 1722585 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MANUEL ZAMORA-ROSAS A.K.A. JOSE MANUEL ZAMORA
File: AXXX XX9 728 - Orlando, FL
March 28, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marcela Gyires, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Richard Jamadar
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of December 4, 2014. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on January 18, 2007. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The record reflects that on April 24, 2006, an Immigration Judge terminated proceedings, finding that the respondent had shown prima facie evidence of U.S. citizenship. The DHS appealed, and on November 24, 2006, the Board sustained the DHS appeal, vacated the Immigration Judge’s decision, and remanded the case to the Immigration Court. As Mr. Pineda had entered his appearance before the Board by filing a Form EOIR-27, a copy of our decision was mailed to him.
 
The December 20, 2006, Notice of Hearing (NOH) for the January 18, 2007, hearing was properly mailed to the respondent’s counsel, Ralph Pineda, Esquire, who had previously entered his appearance in Immigration Court through the filing of a Form EOIR-28 (IJ. at 2, 4). The January 18, 2007, in absentia order was personally served on Mr. Pineda on the same date. In his September 18, 2014, motion to reopen in absentia proceedings, which was not accompanied by an affidavit from the respondent, the respondent alleges that he thought he need not appear because the Immigration Judge had previously terminated proceedings. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding that notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).
 
On appeal, the respondent again alleges he should have been served with notice. However, the respondent has not established that notice to counsel was improper, and that it did not constitute notice to the respondent; nor has the respondent alleged or established ineffective assistance of counsel. To establish ineffective assistance of counsel as “exceptional circumstances” for an alien’s failure to appear, the alien must substantially comply with the procedural requirements set forth by the Board in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005). Lozada requires the alien to: (1) support the motion with an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel and what representations counsel did or did not make to the alien in this regard; (2) inform counsel of the allegations being leveled against him or her and give counsel an opportunity to respond; and (3) state in the motion “““‘whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not.”DD’ Dakane, 399 F.3d at 1274 (quoting Lozada, supra, at 639). Here, the respondent has complied with none of the above-noted requirements, and consequently we find no basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 1722585 (BIA)
End of Document

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Case Reopened by IN RE: KEVIN ANTONIO CANALES-YANEZ, BIA, June 13, 2016
2016 WL 1722597 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KEVIN ANTONIO CANALES-YANEZ
File: AXXX XX4 560 - Atlanta, GA
March 24, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carlos A. Piovanetti, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s November 17, 2015, order denying his October 19, 2015, motion to reopen. The respondent has also submitted evidence on appeal, which we construe as a motion to remand. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
A motion to reopen must generally be filed within 90 days of the date of a final order of removal. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent does not challenge the Immigration Judge’s determination that the motion to reopen was filed outside of this 90-day window (I.J. at 2). Instead, the respondent argues that the timeliness requirement should be equitably tolled in light of ineffective assistance of counsel he claims to have received during his removal proceedings (Form EOIR-26 at 2). See Avila-Santoyo v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 713 F.3d 1357, 1362-65 (11th Cir. 2013) (en banc). Specifically, he claims that his prior attorney failed to fully consider his potential avenues for pursuing Special Immigration Juvenile (“SIJ”) status before advising the respondent to accept an order of removal.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not satisfied the procedural requirements for raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 3). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); accord Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222 (11th Cir. 2003). As the Immigration Judge properly noted, the respondent has not established that prior counsel has been informed of the present allegations or that a complaint has been filed with the relevant disciplinary authorities (Id.). See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. Thus, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not properly raised a claim for reopening on this basis, even under a lower “substantial compliance” standard. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 1222 (declining to decide whether substantial compliance with the Lozada requirements is sufficient); see also Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005) (assuming, without deciding, that substantial compliance is sufficient). The motion is therefore untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
*2 We also find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request for sua sponte reopening (I.J. at 3). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). Absent evidence that the relevant state court order has been obtained, the respondent’s potential eligibility for a yet-to-be filed application for SIJ status is too speculative to warrant reopening at this point. See also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (explaining that sua sponte authority is reserved for truly exceptional situations).
 
Finally, we construe the additional evidence the respondent has submitted on appeal as a motion to remand. See Matter of L-A-C-, 26 I&N Dec. 516, 526 (BIA 2015). The evidence reflects the respondent’s ongoing efforts to obtain the relevant state court order needed to apply for SIJ status. For similar reasons discussed above, we are not persuaded that the respondent has met his heavy burden of establishing that the result in this case would be different if a remand were granted for consideration of this evidence. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472 (BIA 1992).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Margaret M O’Herron
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 1722597 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 1357918 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARCO SIAJES-HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX5 251 - Buffalo, NY
March 22, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carlos Piovanetti, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Denise C. Hochul
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of November 13, 2014. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on August 29, 2014. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief opposing the appeal.1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).
 
The respondent moved to reopen proceedings, acknowledging that he had notice of his August 29, 2014, hearing, but alleging that a change of venue motion had been filed on August 22, 2014, and further asserting that due to the respondent’s limited economic resources and his misplaced belief his presence would be excused, he failed to appear at his hearing.
 
Contrary to the respondent’s assertion, the record does not reflect that the change of venue motion was filed prior to the August 29, 2014, hearing but instead it was only included in the motion to reopen filed on October 6, 2014 (I.J. at 3). Regardless, even had the change of venue motion been filed before his hearing, the “mere submission of a motion for change of venue does not excuse an alien’s failure to appear.” Romero-Morales v. INS, 25 F.3d 125, 129 (2d Cir. 1994). In addition, as noted by the Immigration Judge, although the motion included an affidavit from the respondent, it pertained to the enclosed motion to change venue, and does not address why he failed to appear at his hearing on August 29, 2014.
 
To the extent the respondent implied in his motion that he failed to appear because of ineffective assistance by counsel for failure to file the change of venue motion, he has not substantially complied with the procedural requirements for bringing an ineffective assistance claim. The requirements include submitting (1) an affidavit describing in detail the agreement with counsel, (2) proof that counsel was informed of the allegations and had an opportunity to respond, and (3) a statement regarding whether a complaint was filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and, if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988); Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2d Cir. 2005) (holding that an “alien who has failed to comply substantially with the Lozada requirements ... forfeits her ineffective assistance of counsel claim.”).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent now alleges that he was unable to attend his hearing because he took his niece to a medical appointment on the date of his hearing, and he proffers a letter from a physician. We will construe the submission as a motion to remand and will deny the request. See Matter of L-A-C-, 26 I&N Dec. 516, 526 (BIA 2015) (“A motion to remand for the purpose of presenting additional evidence must conform to the same standards as a motion to reopen and will only be granted if the evidence was previously unavailable and would likely change the result in the case”).
 
We first note that the respondent has not adequately explained why this was not raised in the motion to reopen. The respondent’s counsel alleges that this evidence was not available because the respondent’s counsel “had not heard from respondent” when the motion was filed. However, the respondent’s failure to maintain contact with his counsel does not establish that the evidence was previously unavailable and could have been submitted or summarized with his original motion to reopen. See Li Yong Cao v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, All F.3d 149, 156 (2d Cir. 2005) (explaining that the BIA may decline to remand where the movant has failed to “comply with the requirement of 8 C.F.R. § 3.2(c)(1) [now found at § 1003.2(c)(1)] that his motion articulate material, previously unavailable evidence”).
 
Moreover, the respondent did not submit medical records or other corroboration to support his apparent claim that he was unable to attend his hearing because his niece suffered from a serious medical condition. See Alrefae v. Chertoff, 471 F.3d 353, 357-358 (2d Cir. 2006) (noting that “““[t]he BIA’s standard for exceptional circumstances appears fairly stringent, both in terms of the required severity of the circumstances and the proof required to establish a claim”); see also Matter of B-A-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 57, 58-59 (BIA 1998) (“Where an alien argues that his failure to appear resulted from a ‘serious illness,’ we normally would expect specific, detailed medical evidence to corroborate the alien’s claim.”). Because the doctor’s note did not clarify the severity of the respondent’s niece’s condition, the respondent has not established that his niece’s condition prevented the respondent from attending his hearing.
 
The Board has stated that “[n]otifying the Immigration Court of the respondent’s unavailability is a minimal and logical step that, if not taken, is a factor which tends to undermine a claim of exceptional circumstances.” Matter of B-A-S-, supra, at 57. Here, there is no indication that the respondent sent the doctor’s note to the Immigration Judge or notified the Immigration Court of his inability to be present prior to the hearing. Consequently, the respondent has not established that the proffered evidence would change the outcome of proceedings and remand is not warranted.
 
*3 The respondent has not established any exceptional circumstances to support sua sponte reopening. See generally Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (Board’s discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (Board and Immigration Judges retain authority to sua sponte reopen or reconsider proceedings in exceptional situations; sua sponte authority is not meant to cure filing defects or otherwise circumvent the regulations).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003. 1(e)(5).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

The record reflects that two briefing schedules were mailed to the respondent’s counsel at the address he provided. One was issued on January 27, 2015, providing a due date of February 17, 2015, and then a second one was issued on January 28, 2015, providing a due date of February 18, 2015. There is no indication that either notice was returned by the postal service, and therefore no new briefing schedule will be issued.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Case Reopened by IN RE: LARRY EDWAR IRIGOYEN BURBANO A.K.A. LARRY BURBANO LEMA, BIA, November 4, 2016
2016 WL 1357941 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LARRY EDWAR IRIGOYEN BURBANO A.K.A. LARRY BURBANO LEMA
File: AXXX XX4 417 - Arlington, VA
March 17, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul Saul Haar, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)] - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status (conceded)
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - Nonimmigrant visa revoked under section 221(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1201(i)] - revocation of visas or documents (conceded)
 
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia and a citizen of Venezuela,1 appeals from the Immigration Judge’s January 13, 2015, decision pretermitting the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1225(a), based on an approved Alien Relative Petition (Form I-130) filed by the respondent’s United States citizen wife. A motion to reopen filed with the Immigration Judge prior to the respondent’s filing of his appeal has been forwarded with the record for our consideration. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).2 The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a response to the appeal or the motion. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). The appeal will be dismissed; and the motion, denied.
 
We review findings of fact for clear error, including credibility findings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The following relevant facts are not in dispute. On or about March 21, 2001, the respondent entered the United States and remained until he departed in March 2006 (Respondent’s Brief at 1; I.J. at 3-4; Exh. 3 at 12; DHS Motion to Pretermit at 4). From 2001 to 2006, the respondent had no legal status in the United States (Respondent’s Brief at 1; I.J. at 3-4; DHS Motion to Pretermit at 4). The respondent re-entered the United States on May 11, 2007, and remained until his departure in June 2011 (Respondent’s Brief at 1-2; I.J. at 3-4; Exh. 3 at 12; DHS Motion to Pretermit at 4). On July 12, 2011, the respondent re-entered the United States as a nonimmigrant employee of an international organization (Respondent’s Brief at 2; I.J. at 4; Exh. 3 at 13).
 
*2 On May 13, 2013, United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status because, among other things, USCIS determined that the respondent’s entries and departures render him inadmissible pursuant to section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(C)(i)(I), for being an alien unlawfully present after previous immigration violations (I.J. at 2; Exh. 3 at 1-10).
 
The respondent’s nonimmigrant status as the employee of an international organization was revoked on December 4, 2013, pursuant to section 221(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1201(i) (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 4; Exh. 1). On December 5, 2013, the DHS initiated removal proceedings against the respondent, charging him as being subject to removal pursuant to sections 237(a)(1)(B) and (C)(i)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(1)(B) and (C)(i)(I), because his nonimmigrant visa was revoked and because he failed to maintain or comply with the conditions of his nonimmigrant status (I.J. at 2, Tr. at 4; Exh. 1). On June 17, 2014, the respondent conceded removability and renewed his application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act before the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 4-5).
 
On January 3, 2015, the Immigration Judge found the respondent statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status under 245(a) of the Act because he is not admissible (I.J. at 5). The Immigration Judge determined, inter alia, that the respondent was inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(I) of the Act because he had been unlawfully present in the United States for an aggregate period of more than 1 year between 2001 and 2006, departed, and then subsequently entered the United States without being admitted or paroled in 2007 (I.J. at 2-3, 5-6). The Immigration Judge further noted that no waiver is available to address the respondent’s inadmissibility under section 212(a)(9)(C)(i) of the Act (I.J. at 5).
 
The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Court on January 30, 2015 (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen). The respondent attached to his motion (1) his affidavit regarding his May 11, 2007, entry to the United States; (2) the results of a polygraph test; (3) a series of photographs which the respondent indicates were taken in Spring 2007; (4) a January 3, 2008, report regarding traveler inspections at United States ports of entry; and (5) a draft application for a waiver of grounds of inadmissibility (Form I-601) (Attachments to Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 1-70). However, the respondent perfected a timely appeal by filing a Notice of Appeal with this Board on February 12, 2015, prior to the Immigration Judge issuing a decision on the pending motion (Respondent’s Notice of Appeal). Accordingly, jurisdiction vested with the Board and the Immigration Judge was divested of jurisdiction over the motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of H-, 20 I&N Dec. 611, 612 n.1 (BIA 1992). That motion is now before us as a motion to remand, and the arguments are incorporated into the appeal. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4); BIA Practice Manual Rule 4.2(a)(ii).
 
*3 The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in concluding that he is statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(a) of the Act (Respondent’s Brief at 3-11; I.J. at 2-5). Specifically, he maintains that he is not inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(I) of the Act because his application for adjustment of status is not an application for admission (Notice of Appeal). The respondent also argues that new evidence, submitted with his motion to reopen, establishes that he is not otherwise inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(I) of the Act because he was inspected and admitted when he entered the United States on May 11, 2007 (Respondent’s Brief at 8-11).
 
We will deny the respondent’s motion to remand. A motion to remand for the purpose of presenting additional evidence must conform to the same standards as a motion to reopen. Matter of L-A-C-, 26 I&N Dec. 516, 526 (BIA 2015). A motion to reopen will not be granted unless it appears to the Board that the evidence offered in support of the motion is material and was not available or could not have been discovered or presented previously. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988).
 
The respondent has not established that the evidence submitted with his motion is new or previously unavailable evidence that could not have been discovered or presented at the hearing before the Immigration Judge. The respondent’s counsel claims that he was unable to submit the evidence previously because he learned of the circumstances surrounding the respondent’s May 11, 2007, entry into the United States after the Immigration Judge issued his January 13, 2015, decision (Respondent’s Brief at 3; Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 1, 3). However, the statements of counsel, which are not evidence, do not explain why the information was not previously discovered (Respondent’s Brief; Respondent’s Motion to Reopen). See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980) (uncorroborated declarations of counsel are not evidence).
 
As a result, the respondent’s motion is unsupported by new, previously unavailable evidence material to his claim. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent has made no allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel and he has not presented evidence to meet the procedural requirements for such a claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Therefore, we conclude that the respondent has not established an adequate basis for remanding the record with respect to the facts and circumstances surrounding the respondent’s May 11, 2007, entry into the United States.
 
*4 The respondent’s argument that his application for adjustment of status is not an application for “admission” as defined by the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit is inapposite because the ground of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(I) of the Act does not refer to an alien who seeks “admission,” but instead applies when any alien “enters or attempts to enter” the United States (Respondent’s Brief at 4-6, 8). Moreover, to be eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, an alien must be “admissible” without regard to whether the alien is an applicant for “admission” as the term is used in the cases cited by the respondent (Respondent’s Brief at 5-6). See Leiba v. Holder, 699 F.3d 346, 352-56 (4th Cir. 2012) (construing the phrase “lawfully admitted for permanent residence” in section 212(h) of the Act); Bracamontes v. Holder, 675 F.3d 380, 385-87 (4th Cir. 2012) (same); Aremeu v. Department of Homeland Security, 450 F.3d 578, 581-83 (4th Cir. 2006) (construing the phrase “the date of admission” in section 237(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act).
 
As the respondent does not otherwise contest the Immigration Judge’s finding of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(I) of the Act nor does he claim eligibility for a waiver of this ground of inadmissibility, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(I) of the Act and, consequently, statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act (Respondent’s Brief; I.J. at 4-5).
 
We therefore decline to address the Immigration Judge’s alternative finding that the respondent is inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act. See Matter of L-A-C-, supra, at 526 n.7 (declining to reach alternative issues on appeal regarding ineligibility for relief where the applicant is statutorily ineligible for relief on other grounds). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

At a master calendar hearing held on June 17, 2014, the respondent claimed to be a citizen and national of both Colombia and Venezuela (Tr. at 4). As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent’s May 22, 2013, sworn statement indicates that he renounced his Venezuelan citizenship (I.J. at 1, n.2; Exh. 3 at 11).

2016 WL 1357958 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ESMERALDA CARBALLO RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX1 792 - San Diego, CA
March 16, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carlos R. Juelle, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Monica Mubaraki
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)] - Alien smuggler
 
 
APPLICATION: Suppression; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 1, 2014. In her decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to terminate removal proceedings, sustained the charge of removability, and ordered the respondent removed from the United States to Mexico. During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent filed a brief that is styled as an appeal brief, but, substantively, it is a motion to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) filed a motion for summary affirmance. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed, and her request to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel will be denied.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii). Since the respondent’s case arises subsequent to May 11, 2005, it is governed by the provisions of the REAL ID Act. See Matter of S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 42 (BIA 2006).
 
This matter arises from a November 3, 2012, border stop at the Otay Mesa, California, port of entry (Exhs. 1, 3). As a result of events that occurred at the border station while the respondent was attempting to gain admission to the United States, the respondent was detained, and, based upon answers elicited by United State Customs and Border Protection (CBP) officers, served with a Notice to Appear (Form I-862) and placed in removal proceedings (Exhs. 1, 3). On November 18, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to terminate the removal proceedings asserting that her acquiescence to smuggling her relatives into the United States was not an affirmative act; thus, she was not removable under section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act for knowingly encouraging, inducing, assisting, abetting, or aiding any other alien to enter or try to enter the United States (Exh. 4). The DHS opposed the motion (Exh. 5). On January 7, 2014, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to terminate (Exh. 6). On April 29, 2014, the respondent filed a motion to subpoena the four CBP officers who encountered and interviewed her on or about November 3, 2012, when she applied for admission at the Otay Mesa, California, port of entry; the DHS opposed the respondent’s motion (Exhs. 11, 12). The Immigration Judge issued an interim decision in which she denied the respondent’s motion on May 28, 2014 (Exh. 14).
 
*2 On October 1, 2014, the Immigration Judge issued an oral decision that sustained the charge of removability under section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Act and ordered the respondent removed from the United States to Mexico (Exh. 1). The respondent timely appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent’s brief is styled as an appeal brief, but, substantively, it is a motion to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel and a request to suppress the testimony she gave on October 1, 2014. Specifically, the respondent asserts her former counsel’s performance was so deficient that the respondent’s due process rights were violated when her former counsel failed to present objections to the DHS’s evidence in support of the Notice to Appear, failed to submit a motion to suppress said evidence, filed defective motions to terminate and for subpoena, and failed to advise the respondent of her right to remain silent and, instead, encouraged her to testify (Resp.’s br. at 7-17; Exhs. 1, 3, 4, 11). She further requests her October 1, 2014, testimony be stricken from the record. It is these arguments we now address.
 
The respondent did not comply with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (Resp.’s br. at 9). See Al Ramahi v. Holder, 725 F.3d 1133, 1138-39 (9th Cir. 2013) (compliance with Lozada ensures that the Board has an objective basis for assessing the substantial number of claims of ineffective assistance of counsel that come before it); see also Tamang v. Holder, 598 F.3d 1083, 1090-91 (9th Cir. 2010) (failure to satisfy Lozada was fatal to ineffective assistance of counsel claim where ineffectiveness was not plain on face of record and petitioner failed to provide any information regarding his purported former counsel). Although compliance with Lozada is not required when the error is plain on the face of the record, there is no plain error in the present case. Tamang v. Holder, supra.
 
Moreover, we disagree with the respondent’s assertion that former counsel violated her due process rights by not objecting to the DHS’s evidence (two Records of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien, Form I-213s) for the respondent and the smuggled alien, a report from the CBP officer at primary inspection, and the Record of Sworn Statement in Proceedings of the smuggled alien (Form I-867A)) and by not filing a motion to suppress such evidence (Exh. 3 at Tabs A-D; Resp.’s br. at 9-11).1 As to the aforementioned evidence, the respondent has not presented a sufficient argument to support the conclusion that, had prior counsel objected to DHS’s evidence, such an objection would have been sustained or the outcome of the proceedings may have been affected (Resp.’s br. at 9-10). See Martinez-Hernandez v. Holder, 778 F.3d 1086, 1088 (9th Cir. 2015) (in an immigration proceeding, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires a showing of inadequate performance and prejudice); see also Torres-Chavez v. Holder, 567 F.3d 1096, 1100 (9th Cir. 2009).
 
*3 The Ninth Circuit has repeatedly held that, in immigration hearings, the sole test for admission of evidence is whether the evidence is probative and its admission is fundamentally fair; it has further held the admission of government documents like the Form I-213 is fair absent evidence of coercion or that the statements are not those of the alien. See Sanchez v. Holder, 704 F.3d 1107, 1109 (9th Cir. 2012); see also Espinoza v. INS, 45 F.3d 308, 310-11 (9th Cir. 1995) (rejecting argument that a Form I-213 is inadmissible as hearsay because “[t]he sole test for admission of evidence [in a deportation proceeding] is whether the evidence is probative and its admission is fundamentally fair”). The respondent presents insufficient argument on appeal that the DHS improperly coerced her or that she or the relative she smuggled did not make the statements contained in the evidence; thus, we conclude the DHS’s evidence was properly admitted (Resp.’s br. at 9-11; Exh. 3 at Tabs A, C, D). Based on the foregoing, we cannot conclude the respondent’s prior counsel prejudiced the respondent’s case when she did not object to the government’s evidence or move to suppress it. See Martinez-Hernandez v. Holder, supra; see also U.S. v. Lopez-Chavez, 757 F.3d 1033, 1041, 1043 (9th Cir. 2014) (to establish ineffective assistance of counsel in immigration proceedings in violation of the right to due process, a petitioner must show: (1)that the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case, and (2) prejudice).
 
Likewise, we disagree with the respondent’s additional assertion that former counsel erred by filing a defective motion to terminate that did not contain a declaration regarding her detention and an accompanying motion to suppress evidence (Resp.’s br. at 11-13). Contrary to the respondent’s argument on appeal, we do not consider the evidence obtained during her detention to be in violation of 8 C.F.R. § 287.8(c)(2)(vii) (Resp.’s br. at 12; Tr. at 37). Id. (prohibiting immigration officers from using threats, coercion, or physical abuse to induce a suspect to waive his or her rights to make a statement). The respondent cites no case law or other legal precedence to support the conclusion that, given the exigent circumstances of suspected alien smuggling, her length of detention, brief interrogations, and the CBP officers’ conduct was anything more than standard operating procedure (Resp.’s br. at 12, 15). We also note the presumption of regularity which presumes that public officers, in the absence of clear evidence to the contrary, have properly discharged their duties, including placing information on forms; no such “clear evidence to the contrary” exists here. U.S. v. Armstrong, 517 U.S. 456, 464 (1996); see also Matter of Rodriguez, 13 I&N Dec. 746, 748 (BIA 1971) (discussing presumption of official regularity which attaches to the consular officer’s issuance of the visa in question). Based on the foregoing, we cannot conclude the respondent’s prior counsel prejudiced the respondent’s case by not including the respondent’s declaration with the motion to terminate and an accompanying motion to suppress. See Martinez-Hernandez v. Holder, supra; see also U.S. v. Lopez-Chavez, supra.
 
*4 We recognize the respondent’s prior counsel’s motion to subpoena the four CBP officers who encountered and interviewed the respondent on or about November 3, 2012, may not have fully comported with the requirements set forth in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.35(b)(2) (Resp.’s br. at 13-14; Exhs. 11, 14 at 3-4). However, the respondent’s case was not prejudiced because she did not cross-examine the CBP officers who prepared her Form I-213 and other relevant immigration documentation (Resp.’s br. at 14-15; Exh. 3 at Tab A). As previously noted, the test for admission of evidence is whether the evidence is probative and its admission is fundamentally fair and that the admission of government documents is fair absent evidence of coercion or that the statements are not those of the alien. Sanchez v. Holder, supra (determining that the Immigration Judge did not abuse his discretion by admitting Form I-213, even though the petitioner was not given an opportunity to cross-examine the preparer); see also Haile v. Holder, 658 F.3d 1122, 1128 (9th Cir. 2011) (“The sole test for admission of evidence is whether the evidence is probative and its admission is fundamentally fair.”); Espinoza v. INS, supra, at 309-10; see also Matter of Barcenas, 19 I&N Dec. 609, 611 (BIA 1988) (documentary evidence is admissible if it is probative and its use is fundamentally fair; absent evidence that a Form I-213 contains information that is incorrect or that was obtained by coercion or duress, the document is inherently trustworthy and admissible to prove removability). As previously noted, the respondent has not cited any case law or other legal precedence to support the conclusion that, given the exigent circumstances of suspected alien smuggling, the CBP officers’ conduct was anything more than standard operating procedure (Resp.’s br. at 14-15).2 Since we cannot conclude the respondent’s admissions of alien smuggling were made under coercion, and we see no other objection, the Immigration Judge’s admission of the Form I-213 was fundamentally fair and, thus, proper (Exhs. 3, 7). See Sanchez v. Holder, supra; see also Haile v. Holder, supra, see also Espinoza v. INS, supra. As such, even if we accepted the respondent’s allegation that her prior counsel filed a defective motion to subpoena the CBP officers who prepared the Form I-213, we cannot conclude it prejudiced her case. See Martinez-Hernandez v. Holder, supra; see also U.S. v. Lopez-Chavez, supra.
 
*5 Turning to the respondent’s argument that former counsel failed to advise her of her right to remain silent, the respondent’s brief does not contain a statement or affidavit to support this assertion, and the respondent’s counsel’s statements in the brief, without more, are not evidence (Resp.’s br. at 16). See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); see also Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Hence, we deny the respondent’s request to strike her testimony from the record, and decline to address this issue further.
 
In sum, we conclude the respondent’s numerous due process claims related to her former counsel’s performance are without merit. They collectively fail because the respondent has not shown her former counsel’s performance prejudiced the outcome of her proceedings. See Martinez-Hernandez v. Holder, supra; see also U.S. v. Lopez-Chavez, supra.
 
Finally, we conclude the Immigration Judge properly evaluated the issue of removability and found the respondent removable. The respondent does not seek any relief from removal either before the Immigration Judge or on appeal. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request to strike testimony from the record is denied.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note the DVD of the respondent’s video recorded sworn statement was withdrawn by the DHS and will, therefore, not be addressed (Exhs. 3 at Tab E, 14 at 3-4).

2

As an aside, we further disagree with the respondent’s unsupported assertion that the Form I-213s were not properly authenticated after the DHS withdrew the DVD from the record; each one is signed by an authorized designee (Resp.’s br. at 15; Exh. 3 at Tabs A, C). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 287.6(a) and 1287.6(a) (an official record may be evidenced “by a copy attested by the official having legal custody of the record or by an authorized deputy”).
