2016 WL 1357957 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JENNY MARGARITA ACUNA-IBARRA
File: AXXX XX2 458 - Harlingen, TX
March 16, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alary E. Piibe, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Caroline Clark
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s April 9, 2015, decision denying her motion to reopen. The respondent was previously ordered removed in absentia on July 19, 1999. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
An in absentia removal order may be rescinded only if the alien files a motion to reopen: (1) within 180 days after the date of the removal order in which she establishes that her failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances; or (2) at any time in which she establishes that she did not have proper notice of the hearing in which the removal order was entered. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); see also section 240(e)(1) of the Act (defining the term “““exceptional circumstances”).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s March 13, 2015, motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances, finding the motion was untimely, and the respondent had not demonstrated due diligence in pursuing her claim. We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
On appeal and in her motion to reopen, the respondent asserts that (1) she hired a person whom she believed to be an attorney and legal specialist to help with her immigration case, (2) this person advised her not to appear for her scheduled hearing, and (3) this ineffective assistance prejudiced her case. To the extent the respondent argues that she established exceptional circumstances based on ineffective assistance, such a claim is precluded as untimely, as the in absentia order was entered in July 1999, nearly 16 years prior to the filing of the motion to reopen. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
We note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this cases arises, has not yet determined if equitable tolling of statutory deadlines for ineffective assistance of counsel applies in immigration cases. The Fifth Circuit has previously stated that an alien’s request for equitable tolling on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is construed as an invitation for the Board to exercise its discretion to reopen proceedings sua sponte. See Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 220 (5th Cir. 2008), abrogated by Mata v. Lynch, 135 S. Ct. 2150, 2155-56 (2015). In this case, the Immigration Judge made direct findings on the factors that would or would not permit equitable tolling in this case, separate from the sua sponte question. Specifically, he found that the respondent did not support her claim of ineffective assistance with sufficient evidence of compliance with the factors listed in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), as she did not provide details regarding the name of her purported representative, the name of his firm or office, or the location of their meeting (I.J. at 2). We therefore agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established that reopening is warranted under section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
*2 In addition, we find no basis for exercising our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings, in light of the respondent’s nearly 16-year delay in filing the instant motion (see also I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations). The respondent contends on appeal that a late filing, even one delayed by several years, does not necessarily demonstrate a lack of due diligence (Resp.’s Brief at 10). However, the respondent also acknowledges that she was represented in immigration proceedings in pursuit of adjustment of status as early as 2013, and her attorney learned of the 1999 in absentia order of removal in July 2013 (Resp.’s Brief at 4-5). As such, we find unavailing her argument that she could not have investigated her removal order and pursued reopening prior to the filing of the instant motion in March 2015.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 1357957 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABDOULAYE DOUCOURE
File: AXXX XX7 510 - Buffalo, NY
March 16, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Osaruwense Igunbor, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Denise C. Hochul
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native of Niger and a citizen of Mali, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on June 1, 2007, after not appearing at a hearing. He appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 10, 2015, denying his June 9, 2015, motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that his non-appearance at the hearing was caused by “exceptional circumstances.” See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) (providing that a motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal). The respondent’s motion was filed more than 8 years after the hearing, and it is untimely.
 
In addition, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny equitable tolling of the 180-day period to file a motion to reopen. The respondent has not complied with any of the procedural requirements for establishing that ineffective assistance of counsel caused his failure to appear, and he did not file his motion with due diligence. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We also do not consider evidence first provided on appeal. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984) (holding that the Board does not consider evidence first provided on appeal). Moreover, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny sua sponte reopening of these proceedings, inasmuch as an exceptional situation was not established. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s and the Immigration Judge’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). In light of the foregoing, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 1357954 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 1084466 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN BELTRAN ORTIZ
File: AXXX XX1 425 - Houston, TX
March 8, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Olivieri Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s November 16, 2015, decision denying his motion to reopen the removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent is the subject of an administratively final order of removal issued by an Immigration Judge on September 25, 2000. The respondent was physically removed to Mexico pursuant to that order on November 15, 2000.
 
On October 21, 2015, the respondent filed a “motion to reopen sua sponte” with the Immigration Court in which he requested that his removal proceedings be reopened. The Immigration Judge denied the motion pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), which provides in relevant part that “[a] motion to reopen . . . shall not be made by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of removal, deportation, or exclusion proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States.” We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, which is in accordance with controlling precedent in this jurisdiction. See Ovalles v. Holder, 577 F.3d 288, 292-93 (5th Cir. 2009) (recognizing that the departure bar precludes sua sponte reopening of removal proceedings); Salgado v. Lynch, 609 F. App’x 233, 234 (5th Cir. 2015); cf. Garcia-Carias v. Holder, 697 F.3d 257, 265 (5th Cir. 2012) (noting that “Ovalles resolved the issue of the applicability of the departure regulation to the Board’s regulatory power to reopen or reconsider sua sponte.”)
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the record should be remanded because the attorney who filed his motion to reopen provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to request equitable tolling of the 90-day statutory deadline for such motions. The respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim is deficient on its face, however, because the respondent has neither filed a bar disciplinary complaint against former counsel nor adequately explained why not, as required by Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). We deem compliance with the bar complaint requirement to be essential, for several reasons: it serves the long-term interest of policing the immigration bar; it deters meritless claims and increases our confidence in the particular claim’s validity; and it “serves to protect against collusion between alien and counsel in which ‘ineffective’ assistance is tolerated, and goes unchallenged by an alien before disciplinary authorities, because it results in a benefit to the alien in that delay can be a desired end, in itself, in immigration proceedings.” Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 604 (BIA 1996); see also Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-98 (5th Cir. 2000). “We consider the filing of such a complaint, or reasonably explaining why such was not done, to be a relatively small inconvenience for an alien who asks that he or she be given a new hearing in a system that is already stretched in terms of its adjudicatory resources.” Id. at 605. This last consideration is particularly weighty here, given that the respondent is seeking the reopening of removal proceedings that became final more than 15 years ago.
 
*2 In his appeal brief, the respondent claims that he did not file a bar complaint because former counsel “has acknowledged his ineffectiveness and immediately took steps to remedy the situation by referring Respondent to his present Counsel. . . .” (Resp. Brief at 15). This explanation is wholly unsubstantiated, however; a mere statement in the brief is not evidence. INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188 n.6 (1984).
 
Finally, the respondent has made no offer of proof on appeal regarding the evidence he believes former counsel should have presented in support of his equitable tolling claim. As a rule, an equitable tolling claim requires proof that the moving party “has been pursuing his rights diligently” and “that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way and prevented timely filing.” See Menominee Indian Tribe of Wisconsin v. United States, 136 S. Ct. 750, 755 (2016) (quoting Holland v. Florida, 560 U.S. 631, 649 (2010)). The respondent’s brief and supporting affidavit contain no indication of his diligence or of any extraordinary impediment to his filing of a timely motion to reopen.
 
In conclusion, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the removal proceedings. The following order will be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 1084466 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2016 WL 1084474 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ENRIQUE RANGEL-NUNEZ A.K.A. ENRIQUE RANGEL-NUNEZ
File: AXXX XX8 208 - El Paso, TX
March 4, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nina Bonyak, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Barbara Cigarroa
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative decision on November 24, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
Applicable regulations provide that a motion to reopen “shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Moreover, a movant must satisfy the “heavy burden” of establishing that if the proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). To prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must show that competent counsel would have acted otherwise and that the alien was prejudiced. See Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5 Cir. 1994); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent has not established prejudice such that reopening is warranted. First, the respondent has not submitted any evidence along with his motion, including any applications for relief. More importantly, the respondent still has not established statutory eligibility for the relief. He was previously precluded from eligibility for adjustment of status based on his 1998 controlled substance conviction. He subsequently filed a motion for post-conviction relief which was denied. The respondent has not submitted any evidence with this motion to establish, nor does he argue, that his controlled substance conviction has since been overturned or is no longer valid for immigration purposes. Thus, the respondent has not demonstrated prejudice from counsel’s actions or omissions such that he has established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. As such, the respondent’s motion is insufficient to meet his “heavy burden” of showing that it is likely that the result would change if the proceedings were reopened. See generally Matter of Coelho, supra. Therefore, we do not find that he has met his burden of showing that reopening of these proceedings is warranted. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 1084474 (BIA)

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CECILIA D. GUERRERO A.K.A. CECILIA DE LAS MERCEDES SANCHEZ DE GUERRERO A.K.A. CECILIA SANCHEZ
File: AXXX XX6 565 - New York, NY
February 10, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sin Yen Ling, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael McFarland
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(B)
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(U)
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
AMENDED ORDER
 
This matter was previously before the Board on August 17, 2015, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s April 21, 2015, order of removal. The respondent now moves to reopen proceedings alleging ineffective assistance by her prior counsel. The Department of Homeland Security opposes reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
The record reflects that the respondent was convicted on January 30, 2014, of conspiracy in the fifth degree, in violation of New York Penal Law § 105.05, for which she received a sentence of one year (Exh. 2, Tab B). The record further reflects that on January 30, 2014, the respondent was convicted of attempted criminal possession of a controlled substance in the third degree, in violation of New York Penal Law § 110-220.16(1), for which she received a sentence of 18 months (Exh. 2, Tab B). On April 21, 2015, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States based on her concession, through counsel, to the charges listed in the Notice to Appear, and her failure to apply for any relief from removal. The Board summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision on August 17, 2015, after the respondent did not raise any challenges to the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
The respondent now moves to reopen proceedings alleging that her former attorney rendered ineffective assistance in failing to challenge the removal charges or apply for any relief from removal. As an initial matter, we note that the respondent has complied with the evidentiary requirements for asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).1 However, the respondent has not established that she was prejudiced by her former immigration counsel’s actions. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556-57 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 Although the respondent states that she would be eligible for relief from removal if her conviction was determined to not be not an aggravated felony, she provides no specific authority or argument that her conviction under New York Penal Law § 110-220.16(1), which involved possession with intent to sell narcotics, is not an aggravated felony. We also find that the respondent has not presented a prima facie case for relief to support a reopening of proceedings. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (the Board may deny a motion to reopen based upon a failure to establish a prima facie case for the relief sought).
 
As an aggravated felon, the respondent is ineligible for asylum or cancellation of removal. See sections 208(b)(2)(A)(ii), 208(b)(2)(B)(i), 240A(a)(3) of the Act. An aggravated felony involving unlawful trafficking in controlled substances is presumptively a particularly serious crime rendering an alien ineligible for withholding of removal. See section 241(b)(3)(B)(ii) of the Act, Matter of Y-L-, A-G-, R-S-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 270, 273-77 (A.G. 2002) (aggravated felonies involving unlawful trafficking in controlled substances are presumptively particularly serious crimes). The respondent has not put forth any argument to rebut application of the presumption in this case.
 
As for protection under the Convention Against Torture (CAT), we do not find that the respondent has established a prima facie case for protection to support her motion to reopen. The respondent indicates that she fears two men who were involved in the trafficking of drugs with her and her husband. However, she makes no claim that these men have ties to the government in Ecuador, nor has she provided any evidence or argument to establish that the Ecuadorian government would acquiesce with or be willfully blind to any torture perpetrated against the respondent. 8 C.F.R. § 1208.18(a)(1) (torture under the CAT must be “inflicted by or at the instigation of or with the consent or acquiescence of a public official or other person acting in an official capacity”).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TENISINI TAUFALELE A.K.A. HALATOA FAKATOUMAFI A.K.A. HALATOA MOANA FAKUTOUMAFI
File: AXXX XX3 398 - West Valley City, UT
February 5, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kimberly J. Trupiano, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law (conceded)
 
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; voluntary departure; remand
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s December 16, 2014, decision, denying his application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), for failure to establish prima facie eligibility, and denying his alternative application for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b), based on insufficient evidence of good moral character. During the pendency of this appeal, the respondent filed a motion, styled as a motion to reopen, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel and requesting remand of the record. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded. The record will be remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issued de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The following facts are not in dispute. The respondent, a native and citizen of Tonga, entered the United States as a nonimmigrant visitor and remained longer than authorized (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 4; Exh. 1). He is the beneficiary of an approved Alien Relative Petition (Form I-130) filed on his behalf by his United States citizen wife (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 4). At a hearing held in 2013, the respondent indicated his intent to apply for adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(a) of the Act based on the approved visa petition (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 11). The Immigration Judge scheduled a final hearing on the respondent’s adjustment application for September 15, 2014 (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 14-15, 17).
 
At the respondent’s September 15, 2014, hearing, his then-counsel requested a continuance because the adjustment application was incomplete: the fingerprints and medical examination had expired, and the affidavit of support for the sponsor and joint sponsor were deficient (I.J. at 3: Tr. at 17-20). See sections 212(a)(1), (2), and (4) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1152(a)(1), (2), (4) (outlining health-related, criminal, and public charge grounds of inadmissibility). Over the DHS’s objection, the Immigration Judge continued the respondent’s case until December 16, 2014 (I.J. at 4; Tr. 19, 21). However, at the December 2014 hearing, problems persisted with the sufficiency of the affidavits of support (I.J. at 4; Tr. at 23-27). The Immigration Judge declined to further continue proceedings and instead concluded that the respondent had not established his statutory eligibility for adjustment of status (I.J. at 4; Tr. at 27). The Immigration Judge also denied the respondent’s alternate request for voluntary departure on good moral character grounds (I.J. at 8). See section 240B(b)(l)(B) of the Act; see also section 101(f) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(f) (defining good moral character).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent alleges that his inability to demonstrate prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status and voluntary departure was due to the ineffective assistance of his prior attorney (Resp. Brief at 2; Resp. Motion to Reopen at 2-5). This is essentially a request for remand, with requirements similar to those for reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-73 (BIA 1992). Generally, such a motion will not be granted unless it states new and material facts, is supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material, and establishes a prima facie case of statutory and discretionary eligibility for the underlying relief sought. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3); see also INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); Maatougui v. Holder, 738 F.3d 1230, 1240-41 (10th Cir. 2013) (confirming that a motion to reopen should be supported by new evidence establishing a prima facie claim to relief); Osei v. INS, 305 F.3d 1205, 1208-09 (10th Cir. 2002); Matter of Coelho, supra.
 
In support of remand, the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements articulated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Osei v. INS, supra, at 1209 n.2. The respondent filed a state bar complaint against his former attorney, advised the former attorney of the allegations against him, and submitted a motion supported by an affidavit detailing how the attorney’s performance was deficient and impacted the respondent’s case.
 
Specifically, the respondent alleges that his former counsel did not prepare him for the hearing, did not request that his wife file her taxes, improperly recorded information on the affidavit of support, and did not ensure that the joint sponsor’s affidavit was duly executed or supported by proof of his United States citizenship, resulting in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent could not overcome the public charge ground in qualifying for adjustment of status (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 7-8, Tabs 41-46; Tr. at 17-18, 20, 24-27). See section 212(a)(4) of the Act; section 213A of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1183a (outlining the statutory requirements for the affidavit of support); 8 C.F.R. §§ 213a.2(a)(l)(i)(A), (ii), (v), (b)(1), (c)(l)(i)(C), (c)(2), (ii) (for the various requirements related to proper execution of the affidavit of support, who may file the affidavit of support, and the evidence required to demonstrate sufficient income in support of the affidavit). With his motion, the respondent also included a letter from an accountant stating that the accountant is working with the respondent’s wife to file her back taxes (Resp. Motion to Reopen at Tab 33). The respondent also alleges he was prejudiced when his attorney did not prepare him for questioning about his 2010 arrest, and that numerous family members would have testified on his behalf as to his good moral character if they had been asked to do so-- factors relevant to the Immigration Judge’s denial of voluntary departure for lack of good moral character (Resp. Motion to Reopen at Tabs 5, 6, 7, 10, 12, 14, 16, 18, 20, 22; I.J. at 6-8; Tr. at 27-51). See section 240B(b)(l)(B) of the Act.
 
*3 We find that remand is warranted in the interests of justice. The respondent has demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s lack of preparation and communication with the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.102(o), (r) (requiring an attorney before the Immigration Court to adequately prepare for all proceedings and maintain communication with the respondent); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640; see also Ochieng v. Mukasey, 520 F.3d 1110, 1115 (10th Cir. 2008) (requiring the respondent to demonstrate prejudice in order to establish ineffective assistance of counsel warranting remand). Here, for instance, the respondent’s prior counsel: (1) did not adequately inform him of the documents required to present a sufficient affidavit of support even after certain deficiencies were identified by the Immigration Judge; (2) did not prepare him for questions he might be asked about his criminal history in qualifying for voluntary departure; and (3) openly acknowledged the affidavit of support was deficient (Tr. at 17-21, 25-27; Resp. Motion to Reopen at 31-46). Moreover, prior counsel proffered no persuasive evidence to rebut the ineffective assistance claim, even though the attorney was notified of the respondent’s allegations. Many of the identified errors in prior counsel’s performance have been rectified by current counsel. Notwithstanding the foregoing, we express no opinion as to the ultimate merits of the respondent’s applications for relief. In light of our disposition of this matter, we decline to address the respondent’s assertions on appeal that the record, as it was constituted before the Immigration Judge, supported a grant of adjustment of status or voluntary departure.
 
Upon remand, the parties will have an opportunity to supplement the record with any documentation or testimony they may choose to present relevant to the respondent’s applications for relief. However, we remind the respondent and his counsel that he is responsible for presenting a complete application for relief supported by the requisite documentary evidence. Failure to follow this instruction and any directives issued by the Immigration Judge may result in a finding that he is statutorily ineligible for any requested forms of relief. See, e.g., Matter of Interiano-Rosa, 25 I&N Dec. 264, 266 (BIA 2010).
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER; The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision and for the entry of a new decision.
 
*4 Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 807202 (BIA)

2016 WL 807204 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LIVINGSTON TONEY
File: AXXX XX5 212 - Los Angeles, CA
February 4, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaime Jasso, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination; remand
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s June 27, 2014, decision ordering him removed from the United States. The respondent requests that the matter be remanded to the Immigration Judge to allow the record to be supplemented with missing transcripts. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge found that the respondent, a native and citizen of Trinidad and Tobago, who became a lawful permanent resident of the United States in 1981, was properly charged as an arriving alien because he departed the United States in 2005, and did not return until 2008, thus abandoning his residency (I.J. at 2-3; Exh. 7). Accordingly, the Immigration Judge sustained the charged of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), and ordered the respondent removed to Trinidad and Tobago. The Immigration Judge did not make a determination as the respondent’s inadmissibility under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act (I.J. at 3).
 
The respondent does not challenge the finding of removability on appeal, but rather argues that he has been deprived of due process in his immigration proceedings because the transcripts of record are missing hearings from the first 2 years of his proceedings (Resp. Br. at 1-4). He argues that, as a result, he has been prevented from fully challenging his order of removal, and establishing his ineffective assistance of counsel claim (Resp. Br. at 1-4, 6-8, 11-12).
 
As the respondent correctly points out on appeal, missing transcripts are not a per se violation of due process (Resp. Br. at 9-10). United States v. Medina, 236 F.3d 1028, 1031-32 (9th Cir. 2001) (explaining, in an illegal reentry case, that missing immigration transcripts did not violate due process in immigration proceedings). In order to establish that a violation of due process has occurred, the respondent must still establish that the missing transcripts rendered his proceedings fundamentally unfair and that he was prejudiced as a result of that unfairness. Id.; see also Dent v. Holder, 627 F.3d 365, 373 (9th Cir. 2010); Colmenar v. INS, 210 F.3d 967, 971 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 We are not persuaded that the respondent has been prevented from reasonably presenting his case on appeal in such a way that rendered his proceedings fundamentally unfair. While the absence of a transcript of proceedings surely affects the nature of appellate review, it does not completely deprive the alien of all means of challenging the errors below. United States v. Medina, supra, at 1032. The respondent could have explained the presence of errors below through his memory, other evidence in the record, or witness testimony. See id. The respondent has not attempted to do so here, and thus he has not shown that he was prevented from fully presenting his case. See id.
 
Furthermore, even if he had established a fundamental unfairness in the proceedings, he must still establish that he suffered prejudice as a result. In the context of missing transcripts, an alien must do more than offer a “vague assertion that, if a tape recording was available, he might be able to locate some defect in the proceeding.” Id. Here, rather than asserting that ineffective assistance of counsel had occurred, the respondent asserts that he believes he may have suffered ineffective assistance of counsel, but that he needs the missing transcripts to determine the nature of his claim (Notice of Appeal, Resp. Br. at 11-12). Without more of an assertion from the respondent about how he believes he was deficiently represented, we conclude that his claims on appeal amount to no more than speculation as to his plausible grounds for relief, which is insufficient to show prejudice. Id.; see also United States v. Corrales-Beltran, 192 F.3d 1311, 1318-19 (9th Cir. 1999); United States v. Alvarado-Delgado, 98 F.3d 492, 493 (9th Cir. 1996).
 
This is particularly true where, contrary to the respondent’s assertions on appeal, the respondent need not strictly comply with the filing and reporting requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), if ineffective assistance of counsel is plain on the face of the record (Resp. Br. at 11-12). See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 597-98 (9th Cir. 2004). The respondent does not assert in any meaningful way that ineffective assistance was plain on the face of the record; nor does he allege what he believes the missing transcripts would prove, if they were produced. Accordingly, the respondent has not established that the missing transcripts prevented him from establishing his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
We also disagree with the respondent that he was prejudiced because the record contains no evidence of the charges against him or his plea to those charges. The Notice to Appear is contained in the record at Exh. 1. Further, while it appears that the respondent did enter earlier pleadings through his attorney, that were contained in the missing transcripts, he also submitted subsequent written pleadings (Exh. 16). The Immigration Judge acknowledged and relied on those written pleadings in rendering his decision (Exh. 16; Tr. at 17; IJ at 2). The respondent does not allege any error of his prior counsel in submitting these pleadings or explain why these are insufficient to establish the charges and his pleadings. Accordingly, the respondent has not established a due process violation that requires remand of this matter. The following orders will be entered.
  
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
*3 FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 807204 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 807232 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUILLERMO LOPEZ A.K.A. GUILLERMO LOPEZ SANCHEZ A.K.A. MEMO LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX6 343 - Dallas, TX
January 29, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jered Dobbs, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 In a decision rendered March 9, 2015, the Immigration Judge denied a joint motion to reopen and terminate proceedings without prejudice filed by the respondent and the Department of Homeland Security (the DHS) on August 8, 2014. The respondent timely appealed that decision. The appeal will be sustained and the parties’ joint motion to terminate will be granted.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, under the “““clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgement, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, entered the United States as a nonimmigrant visitor in 2001 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). The respondent married a United States citizen and adjusted his status to that of a conditional permanent resident in 2005 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). The respondent did not timely apply to remove the conditions on his residency; his joint petition to remove the conditions was filed approximately two years after the relevant deadline (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1; Joint Motion, Tab C). United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) denied the petition because it determined that there was not good cause for the late filing, and the DHS commenced these removal proceedings through issuance of a Notice to Appear, charging the respondent as removable because his conditional permanent resident status had been terminated (Exh. 1). Before an Immigration Judge, the respondent conceded his removability and sought to renew his joint petition to remove the conditions on his residency (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge set a date for the respondent to file the petition with the Immigration Court, accompanied by proof that the requisite filing fee had been paid (I.J. at 2). He did not file the petition; on February 15, 2012, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed (I.J. at 2; Joint Motion, Tab F).
 
In August 2014, the respondent and the DHS filed a joint motion to reopen and terminate proceedings before the Immigration Court, so the respondent could pursue adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255, before USCIS (I.J. at 2). Along with the motion, the respondent submitted: (1) evidence that his wife, through whom he originally obtained his conditional resident status, had filed a new visa petition on his behalf; (2) voluminous documentation of their eight year marriage; (3) a note from his former lawyer suggesting the prior attorney was responsible for the failure to file the petition to remove conditions before the Immigration Judge; and (4) his statement (Joint Motion, Tabs A, G, I, J).
 
*2 The Immigration Judge, observing the motion was not time or number barred because it was jointly filed, denied the motion because the respondent had not submitted proof that the visa petition had been approved, and thus the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent could not establish prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act (I.J. at 3-4). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iv). To the extent that the respondent’s former lawyer was at fault, the Immigration Judge found the respondent had not complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), in making a formal claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 4).
 
We disagree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that reopening and termination without prejudice is not warranted in this case (I.J. at 3-4). Notably, an agreement between the respondent and the DHS on an issue regarding the proper course of action “should, in most instances, be determinative.” See Matter of Yewondwosen, 21 I&N Dec. 1025, 1026 (BIA 1997). Here, the Immigration Judge concluded that the joint motion of the parties could not overcome the respondent’s failure to demonstrate prima facie adjustment eligibility because he did not have an approved visa petition (I.J. at 4 n.1). However, the standard for reopening is met when “the evidence reveals a reasonable likelihood that the statutory requirements for relief have been satisfied. . . . We have not required a conclusive showing that eligibility for relief has been established.” Matter of S-V-, 22 I&N Dec. 1306 (BIA 2000) (citing Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413, 419 (BIA 1996) (emphasis added). In addition, contrary to the Immigration Judge’s assertion that an approved visa petition is a necessary prerequisite for reopening, we have specifically found reopening to be appropriate where a pending visa petition is supported by evidence establishing a sufficient likelihood that the visa petition will be approved. See, e.g., Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253, 256 (BIA 2002). In this case, there is a reasonable likelihood of the visa petition being approved, given the respondent remains married to the same spouse through whom he obtained his conditional permanent residence nearly 10 years ago. Matter of S-V-, supra.
 
Therefore, given the joint motion and the respondent’s evidence that the visa petition is likely to be granted, we will reopen these proceedings and terminate them without prejudice pursuant to the parties’ request. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
*3 FURTHER ORDER: The parties’ joint motion to reopen is granted, and the instant proceedings are reopened and terminated without prejudice at the joint request of the parties.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 807232 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 807250 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EVERLINE GESARE NYABWARI
File: AXXX XX3 208 - Dallas, TX
January 22, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Kenya. The Board dismissed his appeal on September 26, 2013, and thereafter denied several motions. On November 13, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel of former counsel. The motion is denied.
 
The pending motion is untimely filed, as well as number-barred, and no exception to these restrictions placed by law on motions to reopen removal proceedings has been shown to be applicable here. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Further, we find no basis to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
We decline to equitably toll the time and number bar because the respondent has not demonstrated prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). He argues that former counsel failed to continue to object to the admissibility of a Form I-9, and that this was ineffective assistance. We disagree. It is settled that a Form I-9 is admissible in immigration proceedings to support charges of removability against an alien and to determine his or her eligibility for relief from removal. Matter of Bett, 26 I. & N. Dec. 437 (BIA 2014).
 
Furthermore, it has been over 2 years since the appeal was dismissed, and the respondent has not adequately explained why he delayed in raising his current claims. See Rodriquez-Manzano, 666 F.3d 948, 955 n.7 (5th Cir. 2012) (regarding ineffective assistance, “current regulations impose what is effectively a due diligence requirement on post-1992 cases...”). There is, therefore, no basis for abrogating the motions deadline. Accordingly, the motion is denied. The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 807250 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AMADO ALFONSO SANDOVAL-LINARES
File: AXXX XX5 059 - San Diego, CA
December 15, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alary E. Piibe, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(C), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(C)] - Controlled substance trafficker
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated May 8, 2014, denying his motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against him on May 12, 1999. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including any determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent received proper notice of his removal hearing because he was personally served with the Notice to Appear along with notice of the time, date, and location of the hearing. Section 239(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a). With respect to his claim of exceptional circumstances, the respondent’s motion is untimely because he did not file his motion within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s decision. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge properly declined to equitably toll the applicable filing requirements based on the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The doctrine of equitable tolling applies when “despite all due diligence, [the party invoking equitable tolling] is unable to obtain vital information bearing on the existence of the claim.” Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001). Here, the respondent had been put on notice that he was in removal proceedings and was scheduled to appear for a hearing on May 12, 1999, when he was personally served with the Notice to Appear, along with notice of the time and date of the hearing, on April 25, 1999. The respondent’s claim that he spoke with an attorney on the telephone after he returned to the United States in 2000 (which would have been more than 180 days after the Immigration Judge’s May 12, 1999, decision) does not establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent bears the burden of establishing a basis for rescinding and reopening, and he has not provided sufficient details to make his claim persuasive. Nor did the respondent show substantial compliance with the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Finally, the respondent did not show that he acted with the due diligence required for equitable tolling where his motion was filed 14 years after the filing deadline had passed. It was unreasonable for the respondent to take no steps to reopen his case for well over a decade based on the statement of an attorney who apparently declined to represent him. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir 2003) (person seeking to toll period for filing motion to reopen must show he has acted with due diligence).
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has not established an “exceptional” situation sufficient to warrant sua sponte reopening of the proceedings. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The respondent acknowledges in his motion that a Form I-213 (Exh. 2), detailing the basis for the removal charge, had been admitted into evidence at the 1999 hearing (Respondent’s motion at 4-5; I.J. at 2). Moreover, the respondent’s arguments regarding the sufficiency of the Immigration Judge’s decision could have been made years ago. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed
 
Joan B Geller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 9809849 (BIA)

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BENITO ESTEBAN MEZA-DIVENI A.K.A. BENITO ESTEDAN MEZA JR.
File: AXXX XX9 360 - Eloy, AZ
December 9, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stacy Tolchin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Elly Laff
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was before the Board on September 29, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent filed the present motion to reconsider our decision and to reopen proceedings on October 22, 2015, and filed supplemental materials that the Board received on November 4, 2015. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
In this matter, the Immigration Judge entered the removal order in 2006 and the respondent moved to reopen in the Immigration Court in 2015, approximately 9 years later. The Board affirmed the denial of reopening on grounds that the motion was untimely and that the respondent had not demonstrated an exceptional situation to warrant sua sponte reopening. In seeking reconsideration, the respondent argues that since there is no regulatory time limit for reopening sua sponte, his motion was denied for failure to meet a non-existent regulatory deadline. This is incorrect; sua sponte reopening was denied not for timeliness but for lack of an exceptional situation. The present motion does not identify any error of law or fact in our decision or argument that was overlooked. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); see also Matter of 0-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 58 (BIA 2006). The motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
The respondent also seeks reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board held in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), that due process rights may be violated, and the time and numerical limits for filing a motion to reopen may be tolled, when ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from reasonably presenting his case. An alien seeking reopening for ineffective assistance must show that he acted with due diligence. See Iturribarria v. I.N.S., 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003); Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090, 1096-97 (9th Cir. 2007) (holding that alien failed to act with due diligence in waiting 6 months after adverse Board decision before consulting with another attorney). Equitable tolling of a filing deadline is a form of relief that is extended “only sparingly” even in situations where a claimant has “actively pursued” his remedies. Irwin v. Dep’t of Veterans Affairs, 498 U.S. 89, 96 (1990). Even if counsel’s performance was deficient (a question we do not decide), the respondent has not shown that he actively pursued his remedies; in particular, there is no satisfactory explanation for the delay of more than 1 year in moving to reopen after the respondent obtained post-conviction relief in a state criminal matter. The motion to reopen will therefore be denied as barred by the applicable statutory and regulatory time and number limits.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 9809823 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MAURICIO CORTEZ A.K.A. JOSE M CORTEZ
File: AXXX XX0 869 - Arlington, VA
December 7, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey A. Devore, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Temporary Protected Status
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the May 22, 2013, decision of the Immigration Judge denying and deeming abandoned his application for Temporary Protected Status (TPS) and ordering him removed from the United States. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge for a new hearing. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
The record before the Board indicates that U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) denied the respondent’s application for TPS in 2004 for failure to document his eligibility. See Tr. at 20; Motion to Remand, Tabs A, B. The respondent through counsel filed a new TPS application with USCIS in 2012, even though he was then in removal proceedings. See Motion, Tabs O - Q. In the Immigration Court, the respondent submitted only the application form, without any supporting evidence or documentation. The Immigration Judge issued an order of removal, but also advised the respondent that he would grant a timely-filed motion to reopen with an application for TPS or cancellation of removal. See Tr. at 31-33. The respondent did not file a motion to reopen.
 
The respondent’s appeal brief and present motion to remand claim ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board held in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), that due process rights may be violated when ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from reasonably presenting his case and prejudice results. In Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), we held that an alien must show prejudice resulting from the ineffective assistance and will not establish prejudice if he does not document that he is eligible for the relief sought. See also Figeroa v. I.N.S., 886 F.2d 76 (4th Cir. 1989) (holding that even in a clear case of ineffective assistance, the alien must establish prejudice by providing objective evidence that he is entitled to relief).
 
The brief and motion do not document that the respondent is eligible for TPS. The appeal brief makes a conclusory claim of eligibility and the motion addresses counsel’s performance,1 but nowhere does the respondent point to documents establishing his eligibility. Prejudice from ineffective assistance is not shown.
 
The respondent cites Matter of Interiano-Rosa, 25 I&N Dec. 264 (BIA 2010), in arguing that the TPS application should not have been denied or deemed abandoned for failure to file supporting evidence. In that case, the alien timely filed his application for relief and timely filed numerous supporting documents, although he failed to file some documents as directed by the Immigration Judge. The Board held on these facts that the application should be adjudicated on the basis of the testimony and the timely-filed documents. In the present matter, the respondent filed no supporting documents at all (I.J. at 1). The Immigration Judge was correct in holding that the respondent’s testimony alone could not have established his eligibility for TPS (id.), as TPS requires documentation of eligibility in all cases. Therefore, the Immigration Judge properly pretermitted the respondent’s unsupported application.
 
*2 For the above reasons, the Board will affirm the removal order and deny the motion to remand.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Counsel is faulted, inter alia, for failing to file the respondent’s tax returns as ordered by the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 1; Tr. at 16), but the returns are not submitted with the present motion.

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
  Vacated by IN RE: GUSTAVO ADRIAN-CABALLERO, BIA, April 11, 2016
2015 WL 8561138 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUSTAVO ADRIAN-CABALLERO
File: AXXX XX6 437 - Houston, TX
November 27, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; rescission
 
*1 On January 26, 2015, the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s October 29, 2014, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal entered on August 27, 2002. On April 22, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and rescind with the Board. On July 21, 2015, the Board denied the motion, and on September 14, 2015 the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen and reconsider with the Board. The motion will be denied.
 
In his prior motion before the Board, the respondent offered an affidavit in which he stated that he never received any notices from any government office for his removal hearing. The Board found the respondent’s affidavit to be inconsistent with his affidavit submitted in support of his motion before the Immigration Judge in which he stated that he did not have any problems with the mail at his address of record.
 
In his prior motion before the Board, the respondent also set forth an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), against the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge and on appeal before the Board, Martha E. Garza, Esquire. The respondent asserted that he did not tell Attorney Garza that he did not have problems with the mail at his address of record; Attorney Garza prepared the affidavit in English; and Attorney Garza had him sign the affidavit without having read it to him in Spanish. In response to the allegations against her, Attorney Garza stated that in accordance with her office’s routine practice, the affidavit was read to the respondent in Spanish for corrections and signature. Attorney Garza also reminded the respondent that he met and consulted with a former associate of the firm, Raed Gonzalez.
 
Raed Gonzalez is the respondent’s current attorney. With the instant motion, Attorney Gonzalez has offered a notarized affidavit in which he states that he was not personally involved with the respondent’s case when he was employed in the office of Attorney Garza, and he attests that “it was never (Attorney Garza’s) routine policy nor routine practice at the office to consistently read affidavits back to non-English speaking clients in their native language.” Attorney Gonzalez further states that “[f]requently attorneys and legal assistants in (Attorney Garza’s) office simply would type the declaration and take the signature of the client without reading it back to them.”
 
Attorney Gonzalez implies that Attorney Garza did not respond truthfully regarding her handling of the respondent’s case. Although Attorney Gonzalez’s affidavit impugns Attorney Garza’s integrity, the respondent has not offered evidence that Attorney Garza has been notified of, and given a chance to respond to, the allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in the instant motion, or that a proper complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. See Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 648 (5th Cir. 2014) (counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed to respond before allegations of ineffective assistance are presented to the Board); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639 (setting forth the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board). We conclude, therefore, that equitable tolling of the filing deadline based on the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim is not warranted in this case. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). Further, it has not been shown that an exceptional situation is present in this case to warrant reopening sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 8561138 (BIA)

IN RE: OMAR OSORIO-BATALLA
File: AXXX XX3 104 - Seattle, WA
November 27, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sandy Restrepo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ryan A. Kahler
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 19, 2014. The respondent has also moved for a remand. The motion will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under a clearly erroneous standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon our de novo review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion for a continuance to pursue a “U” visa as a crime victim. See section 101(a)(15)(U)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(15)(U)(i). While the Immigration Judge did not specifically reference our precedent decision in Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012), concerning the adjudication of such motions, we find that he nonetheless gave adequate consideration to relevant factors and properly ruled on the respondent’s motion.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.6; see also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). Relevant factors for consideration where a continuance is based upon a U visa outlined in Matter of Sanchez Sosa include whether there is government opposition to the motion, evidence of an underlying crime that may serve as a basis for a U visa, and evidence that the alien is prima facie eligible for a U visa. In this instance, the motion was opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and the respondent presented no evidence to show that the relevant visa petition (Form I-918) had been filed and was being processed, or that the respondent had obtained the necessary law enforcement certification to show that he qualified for the visa (Tr. at 9). See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14. Thus, the respondent failed to demonstrate prima facie eligibility for a U visa. Under the circumstances, we find that the respondent did not establish good cause for a continuance and we conclude that the Immigration Judge did not abuse his discretion in denying the continuance.
 
*2 The respondent’s motion to remand appears to claim that he received ineffective assistance from prior counsel. The motion is accompanied by a copy of a U visa petition, an unsigned law enforcement certification in support of the visa petition, and an affidavit in support the visa petition detailing an immigration fraud allegedly committed against the respondent. The motion is defective as a claim of ineffective assistance because the respondent has failed to substantially comply with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective representation outlined in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (I.J. at 4). Given the vague nature of the affidavit underlying the Form I-918 concerning the alleged fraud and the equally vague descriptions of both prior and current counsel regarding the actions taken in connection with the filing of the Form I-918, we do not find that deficient representation is sufficiently plain on the record to excuse compliance with Matter of Lozada. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir.2000). Inasmuch as the respondent has not shown that he prima facie eligible for a U visa, we also find that he has failed to demonstrate the prejudice required to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance because he did not establish that he is prima facie eligible for the relief sought. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (an alien asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must demonstrate prejudice); see also Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793-94 (9th Cir. 2005) (stating that an alien must demonstrate that prior counsel’s performance may have affected the outcome of the proceedings).
 
Effective January 20, 2009, an Immigration Judge who grants an alien voluntary departure must advise the alien that proof of posting of a bond with the DHS must be submitted to the Board within 30 days of filing an appeal, and that the Board will not reinstate a period of voluntary departure in its final order unless the alien has timely submitted sufficient proof that the required bond has been posted. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3). See Matter of Gamero, 25 I&N Dec. 164 (BIA 2010). The Immigration Judge provided the respondent with the required advisals and granted the respondent a 60-day voluntary departure period, conditioned upon the posting of a $500 bond. The record before the Board, however, does not reflect that the respondent submitted timely proof of having paid the bond. Therefore, the voluntary departure period will not be reinstated, and the respondent will be ordered removed from the United States to Mexico pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s alternate order.
 
*3 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 8561133 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO CRUZ MONTER A.K.A. SERGIO CRUZ MONTES
File: AXXX XX2 222 - Buffalo, NY
November 24, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew C. Hagen, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michele Henriques
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated March 13, 2014, finding him removable from the United States, as charged, and denying his motion to suppress evidence.1 See Motion to Suppress (December 2, 2013). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge for the reasons stated therein. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). The sole issue on appeal is the respondent’s request that we reopen these proceedings based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
A motion to reopen may be denied if the respondent: (1) fails to establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought; (2) fails to introduce previously unavailable, material evidence; or (3) is found not to be entitled to a discretionary grant of the relief sought even if the first two requirements are met. INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992). With respect to the second ground, the regulations state that a “motion to reopen proceedings shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
A motion to remand must conform to the same standards as a motion to reopen where the respondent seeks to present new evidence, but will only be granted if the new evidence “would likely change the result in the case.” See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992). Here, the respondent seeks a remand based on a claim that prior counsel was ineffective for various reasons. See Respondent’s Brief at 8-11.
 
The respondent has not fulfilled the prerequisites for allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Debeatham v. Holder, 602 F.3d 481 484-85 (2d Cir. 2010). On appeal, the respondent makes general allegations concerning what actions former counsel should have taken, but in lieu of setting forth the agreement with former counsel concerning what action would be taken and what counsel represented to the respondent, the respondent provides a statement in which he identifies several issues he claims that prior counsel did not appropriately address (Respondent’s Brief at Exhibit B). Debeatham v. Holder, supra, at 484-85. The record does not contain proof that former counsel has been notified of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond. Respondent’s current counsel indicates he sent prior counsel a letter and a copy of the respondent’s brief on appeal. The record does not contain proof that a complaint has been filed with the disciplinary authority, and we are not persuaded by the respondent’s statement claiming that he did not file a grievance with the New York State Bar Association because he was unaware of that option.
 
*2 However, even if the respondent had complied with the Lozada requirements, he has not shown prejudice resulting from counsel’s alleged deficiencies. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638. The respondent has not identified any relief for which he may be eligible, or submitted the appropriate application for relief and supporting documents, pursuant to 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(c). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed on December 19, 2013, but reopened proceedings, sua sponte, on December 20, 2013.


2015 WL 8562302 (BIA)

IN RE: RUFINA ANTONIA SAMAYOA-GONZALEZ

APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent is a female native and citizen of Guatemala. On December 16, 2005, an in absentia order of removal was entered against her pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent filed a motion to reopen on August 5, 2014. On August 22, 2014, an Immigration Judge denied the motion. The respondent now appeals from that decision. The respondent is not challenging that she received proper notice of her scheduled hearing on December 16, 2005. Rather, she contends that an unnamed notario gave her bad advice not to attend her hearing and that her motion is thus subject to equitable tolling. We disagree. The Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent has not shown that she received ineffective assistance of counsel, Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), to excuse her failure to appear at her hearing. Contrary to her claims, the record also reflects that when the Notice to Appear (NTA) was personally served on her, the respondent was advised in her native language, Spanish, of the consequences for failing to appear. See also Exhibit 2, Form I-213, at 3 (respondent indicated that she understood her obligation to report for her hearing). Lastly, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not shown that she is prima facie eligible for relief, such as withholding of removal or voluntary departure, to warrant reopening (I.J. at 2; Motion at 4).1 The appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD

1

An exercise of prosecutorial discretion, including a stay of removal, lies within the jurisdiction of the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). 8 C.F.R. § 241.6.

2015 WL 7074118 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FERNANDO SANCHEZ-MALDONADO
File: AXXX XX3 279 - Lumpkin, GA
October 30, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Johanna Cochran, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Diane Dodd
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals a May 21, 2015, decision by the Immigration Judge denying reopening of an April 2, 2015, decision, which ordered the respondent removed to Mexico. The Department of Homeland Security has filed a brief in opposition to the appeal. The record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge, under a clearly erroneous standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen was a 21-page document alleging ineffective assistance of counsel against the attorney who represented him in his removal proceedings. He made specific arguments against said attorney, i.e., that his attorney erroneously told him that he did not have any other options other to accept his order of removal (Motion at 13-14). He also contends that his attorney did not inform him or his father that he had filed a stay of removal (Motion at 9). Furthermore, he contends that he is eligible for relief from removal (Motion at 15-20). He complied with the procedural requirements delineated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (Motion at 9-10).
 
The Immigration Judge did not discuss the respondent’s specific arguments in his motion, only issuing a one-page handwritten order, stating that the respondent twice confirmed that he agreed to the order of removal, waived appeal, and was satisfied with the representation he received from his counsel. The Immigration Judge’s conclusion does not sufficiently address the issues raised by the respondent’s motion. See Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994) (when a motion to reopen deportation proceedings is denied, the Immigration Judge must identify and fully explain the reasons for the decision; otherwise, the parties are deprived of a fair opportunity to contest the decision on appeal, and the Board is unable to exercise meaningfully its responsibility of reviewing the decision in light of the arguments advanced on appeal); see also Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468 (BIA 1999) (holding that because the Board’s fact-finding ability is limited, it is essential for Immigration Judges to include in their decisions clear and complete findings of fact that are supported by the record and are in compliance with controlling law). The respondent has argued that his agreement to removal and waiver of appeal were based on erroneous advice rather than a tactical decision.
 
*2 Accordingly, we find it necessary to remand the record for the entry of a new decision that more fully addresses, in light of the evidence in the record, the respondent’s arguments and the reasons for rejecting them.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the aforementioned decision.
 
Joan B. Geller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 7074118 (BIA)
End of Document
