CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)] - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i)
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)] - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of South Korea, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s June 24, 2014, decision, which denied the respondent’s request for a continuance. The respondent was granted the privilege of voluntary departure. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent’s removability is not disputed. Indeed, the record confirms that the respondent, through his attorney, conceded his removability under both sections 237(a)(1)(A) and 237(a)(1)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(1)(A) and 1227(a)(1)(C)(i) as charged in the Notice to Appear (I.J. at 1; Tr. at 28; Exh. 1). However, we do acknowledge the respondent’s statement in the Notice of Appeal apparently challenging the charge under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Act. In this regard, we have held that, absent egregious circumstances, a tactical decision by an attorney (in this case admitting to the allegations and conceding to the corresponding charges lodged in the Notice to Appear) is binding on the client. See Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938, 942 (BIA 1995); Matter of Valasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986). The respondent has not alleged any egregious circumstances; nor do we find any such circumstances on this record. Indeed, the respondent has not alleged that his attorney was ineffective or complied with the procedural requirements, as set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Regardless, the respondent does not dispute on appeal his removability under section 237(a)(1)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
The remaining issue raised on appeal by the respondent is whether the Immigration Judge should have continued the removal proceedings for him to further pursue an administrative appeal of the denial of his U-visa application (Form I-918) by the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”). However, the respondent has failed to specify any legal or factual error in the Immigration Judge’s decision, and we are satisfied that the Immigration Judge applied the appropriate factors in denying the continuance. See Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).

2015 WL 5180628 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA DE LOS ANGELES LOWE A.K.A. MARIA LOWE-ALARCON A.K.A. MARIA ALARCON-CERVANTES
File: AXXX XX7 912 - El Paso, TX
August 7, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Charles Spector, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brenda J. Thomas
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. She has filed a timely motion to reopen, alleging ineffective assistance of prior counsel. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the granting of the motion. The motion is denied.
 
The respondent contends that former counsel erroneously conceded her removability under section 237(a)(1)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(E)(i). The record reflects, however, that former counsel denied removability, and contested the charge (Exh. 1; I.J. at 1). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s adjustment of status was an admission and since she was admitted within 5 years of her conviction for smuggling, she was removable as charged (I.J. at 2-3).
 
The respondent now argues she is not removable because her adjustment of status was not an admission, citing to Martinez v. Mukasey, 519 F.3d 532 (5th Cir. 2008)(section 212(h) waiver applies to aliens who adjust their status after entering the United States, but not to those who enter the United States as lawful permanent residents); Matter of J-H-J-, 26 I&N Dec. 563 (BIA 2015). However, Martinez did not hold that adjustment of status was not an admission. Further, we have consistently held that adjustment of status is considered an admission. See e.g. Matter of Bouchra Agour, 26 I&N Dec. 566, 573 (BIA 2015). Accordingly, we do not find that former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel to the respondent such that proceedings should be reopened and termination. Nor has the respondent shown the requisite prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 5180628 (BIA)

2015 WL 5180605 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE OMAR MONROY SALAZAR
File: AXXX XX4 452 - New York, NY
August 3, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pablo Fernandez-Herrera, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 7, 2014, denying his motion for a continuance. On appeal, the respondent also moves for a remand based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but we review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals de novo. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
We find that the Immigration Judge acted properly in concluding the proceedings and entering an order of removal. The pursuit of deferred action before the Department of Homeland Security does not constitute good cause for a continuance because the exercise of prosecutorial discretion is a matter over which the Immigration Court has no jurisdiction and the possibility that the respondent would be granted such relief was speculative. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987) (the decision to grant or deny a continuance for good cause is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge and will not be overturned on appeal unless it is shown that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing); Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348 (BIA 1982) (Immigration Judge did not err in declining to adjourn hearing to allow alien to pursue deferred action); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6.
 
We find no merit in the respondent’s argument that the decision below requires reversal because the Immigration Judge made prejudicial remarks concerning his potential eligibility for deferred action. Under the circumstances, we find that the Immigration Judge merely gave a fair summary of the posture of the respondent’s potential application for deferred action. In any event, the respondent must demonstrate substantial prejudice affecting the outcome of the proceedings to establish a due process violation warranting reversal of the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Miller v. Mukasey, 539 F.3d 159, 164 (2d Cir. 2008). We are not persuaded that the respondent has done so. Absent a showing of good cause or readily available relief, the Immigration Judge acted properly in ordering the respondent’s removal. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 505 (BIA 1980) (citations omitted) (once removal proceedings are instituted, an Immigration Judge must order an alien deported if the evidence supports the charge). There is no merit in the respondent’s assertion that the Immigration Judge’s order of removal deprived him of the opportunity to persuade DHS counsel that he was a suitable candidate for deferred action on his removal since nothing prevents him from applying to the DHS to favorably exercise its prosecutorial discretion even after an order of removal is entered.
 
*2 We will deny the respondent’s motion to remand based upon ineffective assistance. As an initial matter, the claim fails because the respondent has not complied with any of the procedural requirements outlined in the Board’s decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The claim also fails because the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by the actions or inaction of prior counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (an alien asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must demonstrate prejudice). The substance of the respondent’s claim is that prior counsel failed to make a persuasive case for deferred action on his removal (Brief at 8). However, an application for deferred action is a collateral matter involving the DHS’ independent exercise of prosecutorial discretion, and outside the jurisdiction of the Immigration Court. Therefore, any deficiencies in representation concerning that application are insufficient to demonstrate ineffective assistance in the proceedings before the Immigration Court.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied and the appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 5180605 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: ENOC HUMBERTO ABURTO-RAMIREZ A.K.A. HUMBERTO HUMBERTO ABURTO-RAMIREZ

ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Candice Kasperson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Nicaragua, has appealed the May 20, 2014, decision of the Immigration Judge denying a motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board may review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
As an initial matter, we note that the respondent has not submitted a brief in support of this appeal, despite indicating on his Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) that he intends to file a separate brief or statement. Hence, we will consider the limited statements contained on the. respondent’s Notice of Appeal.
 
The arguments contained on the respondent’s Notice of Appeal do not specifically challenge the Immigration Judge’s reasons for denying the motion to reopen. The respondent simply states: “The Immigration Judge erred in applying the facts and case law. The IJ did not address the merits of the motion to re-open. The IJ abused her discretion and did not meaningfully address the motion and reasons why Respondent did not seek relief earlier.” Inasmuch as the respondent has not specifically challenged the bases for the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen, we find that the respondent’s arguments on appeal do not establish error in the Immigration Judge’s decision. See, e.g., Matter of Cervantes, 22 I&N Dec. 560, 561 n.1 (BIA 1999) (expressly declining to address an issue not raised by party on appeal); Matter of Gutierrez, 19 I&N Dec. 562, 565 n.3 (BIA 1988) (same).1
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSCAR MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX2 630 - Boston, MA
July 9, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stephen A. Lagana, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 19, 2015, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. We review the Immigration Judge’s factual findings for clear error and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). The respondent’s appeal fee waiver request is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent based his motion to reopen on an apparent contention that his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance because he only communicated with the respondent through his girlfriend when the respondent was detained. See Respondent’s Brief at 3. On appeal, the respondent argues that, inter alia, the Immigration Judge erred by finding that the motion to reopen was not timely filed. See Respondent’s Brief at 3-4. As the Immigration Judge alternatively found, however, even if the motion had been timely, the respondent did not satisfy the requirements for reopening (I.J. at 4, 5).
 
“A motion to reopen will not be granted unless the Immigration Judge is satisfied that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). In addition, to obtain reopening, the respondent must make a prima facie showing that he is statutorily eligible for the relief sought and that he warrants relief in the exercise of discretion. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 323 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 106 (1988). Prima facie eligibility is shown where the evidence reveals a reasonable likelihood that the statutory requirements for relief have been satisfied and that there is a reasonable likelihood that relief will be granted in the exercise of discretion. See Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413, 419 (BIA 1996).
 
In this case, the respondent requested that his proceedings be reopened but he did not submit an application for relief or supporting documentation. See Motion to Reopen; see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3) (motion to reopen to apply for relief must include the application for relief and supporting evidence). In his motion, the respondent stated only that he “was denied the opportunity to apply for political asylum.” Motion to Reopen at 3. He did not submit an asylum application or present material evidence that was unavailable at the former hearing. Therefore, because the respondent has not submitted an application or demonstrated prima facie eligibility for relief, he has not met the requirements for reopening.
 
*2 The respondent also argues that, when he waived his right to appeal on April 16, 2014, his waiver was not knowing and voluntary (Tr. at 8-10). See Respondent’s Brief at 5-6. The respondent did not raise this argument in his motion, however. See Motion to Reopen; Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260, 261 n.1 (BIA 2007) (matters not raised before the Immigration Judge are waived on appeal); see also Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629, 638 (BIA 2003); Matter of Edwards, 20 I&N Dec. 191, 196-97 n.4 (BIA 1990). Also, although the respondent argues that, because he waived his right to appeal, he lost his opportunity to apply for asylum, he again has not submitted an asylum application or demonstrated prima facie eligibility for asylum. Therefore, he has not shown that he was prejudiced by the error he alleges. See Toribio-Chavez v. Holder, 611 F.3d 57, 66 (1st Cir. 2010) (due process claim cannot succeed without a showing of prejudice).1
 
Further, as the Immigration Judge found, the respondent did not comply with at least some of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (to succeed on a motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent should submit an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts including the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken; former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed to respond; the motion should reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities; the respondent must show that he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel); see also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (I.J. at 5). Additionally, the respondent did not demonstrate that he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
For these reasons, we uphold the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD

1

On July 18, 2014, this Board dismissed the respondent’s prior appeal and found that the respondent, through counsel, conceded removability, acknowledged ineligibility for relief from removal, and waived his right to appeal. See Board’s July 18, 2014, decision. We noted that the respondent had not argued that his waiver was not knowing and intelligent.

2015 WL 4873235 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DAMION IROD MICHAEL REID
File: AXXX XX9 240 - New York, NY
July 2, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fitzmore Harris, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 23, 2014, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his February 11, 2010, removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a reply to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent does not dispute that the Notice to Appear (NTA)(which contained the hearing notice for the February 11, 2010, hearing) was delivered to the correct address, but states that his spouse, with whom he resides, failed to give him the notice. The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. When a hearing notice is delivered by mail but “does not reach the alien through some failure in the internal workings of the household, the alien can be charged with receiving proper notice.” Matter of M-D-, 23 I&N Dec. 540, 547 (BIA 2002); Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181, 189 (BIA 2001). Due process was satisfied here because the method of service was reasonably calculated to ensure that notice reached the alien. See Fuentes-Argueta v. INS, 101 F.3d 867 (2d Cir. 1996).
 
The respondent alleges that his former attorney also told him he did not have to appear for his hearing because a continuation request had been filed (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, page 3). Regarding exceptional circumstances for failing to appear, the Immigration Judge correctly found the motion to reopen was untimely (I.J. at 2). The respondent filed the motion almost four years after his in absentia order of removal became final, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), (4)(ii), and he has not established the due diligence required for equitable tolling of the filing deadline (I.J. at 2-3). See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 131 (2d Cir. 2008).
 
Moreover, the respondent failed to substantially comply with the procedural requirements for bringing an ineffective assistance claim (I.J. at 3). The requirements include submitting (1) an affidavit describing in detail the agreement with counsel, (2) proof that counsel was informed of the allegations and had an opportunity to respond, and (3) a statement regarding whether a complaint was filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and, if not, why not. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). See also Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2d Cir. 2005) (holding that an “alien who has failed to comply substantially with the Lozada requirements ... forfeits her ineffective assistance of counsel claim.”). Here, the respondent did not submit any evidence that he informed counsel of the allegations against him and provide him with an opportunity to respond to the allegations.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent also argues that reopening was denied in error because he has new relief insofar as his U.S. citizen spouse has filed a visa petition on his behalf. Even if the respondent had not been given oral notice in his native language, in order to pursue an application for new relief, the respondent must comply with the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3). Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). Under these standards, the respondent’s May 21, 2014, motion to reopen is untimely, inasmuch as it was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s February 11, 2010, decision, and he has not alleged that the motion would fall within any statutory or regulatory exception to the time limits. Hence, the respondent is also precluded from having his motion considered under the general time limitations for a motion to reopen.
 
The respondent has failed to establish that reopening is warranted pursuant to sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The respondent’s motion does not establish an “exceptional” situation sufficient to warrant reopening of his removal proceedings (I.J. at 3). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 4873235 (BIA)
End of Document

2015 WL 4873226 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANTOS CHICAS MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX9 049 - Charlotte, NC
June 25, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mark J. Devine, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scott D. Criss
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 8, 2014, denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reconsider. The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge did not consider the request for nunc pro tunc relief discussed in his motion to reopen. The respondent explains that the evidence relating to ineffective assistance of counsel was included in the motion to reopen to provide factual support for the equitable remedy he seeks.
 
The record reflects that the Immigration Judge issued a decision on January 16, 2014, granting the respondent pre-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The respondent filed a motion to reopen on March 21, 2014, seeking reopening on the basis of a nunc pro tunc equitable remedy. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, noting that the respondent was represented by counsel during his removal proceedings, and the respondent never expressed a fear of returning to El Salvador before the court. The Immigration Judge found that absent egregious circumstances, which are not present in this case, the respondent is bound by the tactical decisions and actions made by his attorney when such attorney is acting in her professional capacity. Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938 (BIA 1995). See I.J. Dec. dated March 24, 2014.
 
The respondent claims that he was denied the opportunity to file an application for protection under the Convention Against Torture. However, the respondent was represented by counsel when he accepted a pre-conclusion voluntary departure order, and he waived appeal. On appeal and in his motion to reopen, the respondent suggests that his former counsel may have provided ineffective assistance, but he has not complied with any of the requirements discussed in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, the respondent did not establish any prejudice as a result of his prior attorney’s representation which would warrant reopening as an equitable remedy. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 561-62 (BIA 2003) (requiring prejudice stemming from prior counsel’s actions in addition to compliance with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra). The purpose of establishing ineffective assistance of counsel pursuant to Lozada would be rendered superfluous if the proceedings were reopened on the basis of a nunc pro tunc equitable remedy under the circumstances present in this case.1 The respondent did not file an application or other documents which establish a prima facie case for protection under the Convention Against Torture. The Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider as he did not identify any factual or legal errors in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2).
 
*2 We further find no basis for exercising our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

“A nunc pro tunc order is an order that has retroactive legal effect.” Patel v. Gonzales, 432 F.3d 685, 693 (6th Cir. 2005).

2015 WL 4510727 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUZ GUADALUPE TORRES-AREVALO
File: AXXX XX8 223 - Dallas, TX
June 24, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose Zamudio Marin, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(B)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(B)(i)(I)] - Nonimmigrant - no valid passport
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated March 09, 2015. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s February 17, 2015, motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on December 3, 2014, pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A) (I.J. at 1-3). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5). We write separately to supplement the Immigration Judge’s decision with a few additional points, in light of the respondent’s appeal. First, the respondent acknowledges that she had actual notice for the December 3, 2014, hearing (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s Br. at 9). Second, the respondent claims in her declaration that she relied on the advice of a lawyer, whom she characterized as “my lawyer” (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab C), not to appear for the December 3, 2014, hearing. She claims that the lawyer advised her “agent,” who then advised her, that the December 3, 2014, hearing would be “nullified” as a result of her release from custody on November 25, 2014 (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab C).
 
The lawyer tells a different story, however. According to the lawyer’s declaration, he was not retained to represent the respondent at the time the respondent’s agent contacted him about the December 3, 2014, hearing (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab E). Rather, the lawyer’s declaration reflects that he was only retained to represent the respondent in connection with her credible fear interview on October 31, 2014 (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab E). This statement, we note, is corroborated by the fact that the lawyer did not enter an appearance on the respondent’s behalf at the November 24, 2014, master calendar hearing (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tabs C, E).
 
While we have recognized that a lawyer’s affirmative direction not to appear for a scheduled hearing can constitute exceptional circumstances justifying the rescission of an in absentia removal order, such a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must comply with the requirements delineated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
*2 In this case, the respondent has not set forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with her former counsel, and in fact, the record shows that representation in these proceedings was not within the scope of the attorney-client agreement. Nor has a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities against the respondent’s former counsel; the respondent, in fact, has not even acknowledged the existence of this requirement (Respondent’s M.T.R. at 2-3). See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496 (5th Cir. 2000) (recognizing that Lozada’s “requirement regarding disciplinary allegations served both to deter meritless claims and to aid in policing the immigration bar” (citing Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639)).
 
Insofar as the respondent has not established a viable claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, she has not established exceptional circumstances warranting rescission of the in absentia removal order. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 4510727 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2015 WL 4510729 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO DELOYA-LEON
File: AXXX XX9 852 - Atlanta, GA
June 24, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Grace R. Kennedy, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider the Board’s March 26, 2015, decision, in which we denied his untimely and number-barred motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
In the motion, the respondent argues that the motion to reopen time period should be equitably tolled in his case, and that he exercised due diligence since the Board’s December 16, 2013, decision dismissing his appeal until he filed his motion to reopen on December 17, 2014. See Ruiz-Turcios v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 717 F.3d 847, 851 (11th Cir. 2013) (stating that equitable tolling generally requires a litigant to show “(1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way”) (citing Pace v. DiGuglielmo, 544 U.S. 408 (2005)). However, the respondent’s prior motion did not argue that the motion to reopen time period should be equitably tolled, or that the respondent has exercised diligence since the Board’s December 16, 2013, decision. A motion to reconsider based on arguments that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 58 (BIA 2006). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider will be denied.1
 
To the extent the respondent now seeks to present the equitable tolling argument and has presented new documents, his motion constitutes a motion to reopen in substance. See Matter of 0-S-G-, supra, 57-58 (a motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based on the previous factual record, while a motion to reopen seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence). As a motion to reopen, the respondent’s motion is untimely and number-barred. Section 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000); Matter of L-V-K-, 22 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1999). Therefore, it will be denied as untimely and number-barred.
 
Furthermore, the arguments and evidence submitted do not show that equitable tolling of the motion time or number limitations is warranted. The respondent argues that, after he received the Board’s December 16, 2013, decision dismissing his appeal, he retained another lawyer and requested the DHS to file a joint motion to reopen, but the DHS denied this request on June 24, 2014; that he attempted to obtain an affidavit from his prior counsel, Joseph Azar, but attorney Azar merely kept promising and did not actually provide an affidavit; and that he eventually filed a bar complaint against attorney Azar on December 8, 2014, and filed his motion to reopen on December 17, 2014 (Motion, at 5-7; Motion Exh. A-C).
 
*2 The motion and the respondent’s affidavit submitted with the motion do not identify the new lawyer who represented him in June 2014, but the DHS’s June 24, 2014, letter declining to join in a motion to reopen was addressed to his current counsel, indicating that the respondent was represented by his current counsel at that time (Motion Exh. C). Therefore, the letter shows that the respondent was no longer being represented by attorney Azar but was being represented by his current counsel in June 2014.2 Nevertheless, the motion to reopen was not filed until December 2014, about 6 months later. Moreover, requesting the DHS to join in a motion to reopen does not necessarily show diligence in pursuing the ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and the respondent offers no explanation why he could not file a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel while requesting the DHS’s help. Removable aliens should not delay matters by pursuing multiple avenues of relief seriatim when they could have been pursued simultaneously. See, e.g., Galvez Pineda v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 833, 839 (10th Cir. 2005).
 
The respondent also argues that his motion to reopen was further delayed because he was trying to obtain an affidavit from attorney Azar. However, the respondent does not argue that obtaining and submitting such an affidavit is one of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (see Motion, at 9). The respondent fails to explain why such an affidavit was necessary before he could file a bar complaint against the attorney or a motion to reopen based on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. In any event, the respondent eventually filed a bar complaint and the motion to reopen without having to obtain an affidavit from the prior attorney. Based on the above, the respondent did not show that he has been pursuing his ineffective assistance of counsel claim diligently, or that he was prevented by “some extraordinary circumstance” from filing a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See Ruiz-Turcios v. U.S. Atty. Gen., supra. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent reiterates his request for us to sua sponte reopen his proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) and to administratively close his proceedings to allow him to seek consular processing of his visa petition. We do not find that the respondent’s case presents an exceptional situation that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
*3 Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In the prior decision, the Board also noted that the respondent provided radically different accounts on why he did not timely appear at his December 14, 2011, hearing, in his sworn statement submitted with his March 19, 2012, motion to reopen filed with the Immigration Judge (the “first statement”) and in his statement submitted with the December 17, 2014, motion to reopen (the “second statement”), and that he has not reconciled these divergent accounts (Dec. 17, 2014, Motion Exh. C; Mar. 19, 2012 I.J. Motion Affidavit). The respondent now asserts in his statement submitted with the current motion (the “third statement”) that the first statement was prepared by attorney Azar and he signed it without reading it or knowing its contents (Motion Exh. A, at 6-7). However, the respondent fails to explain why this explanation could not be presented with his prior motion to reopen.
In any event, the respondent’s accounts provided in his second and third statements are not supported by the record in this case. The respondent stated in his second statement that at a hearing on September 13, 2011, the Immigration Judge gave his attorney the notice of the December 14, 2011, hearing (Dec. 17, 2014, Motion Exh. C, at 5). In his third statement, the respondent states that the Immigration Judge was not present at the September 13, 2011, hearing and the clerk of the Immigration Court gave his attorney the notice of hearing (Motion Exh. A, at 5). However, as noted by the Immigration Judge, the notice of hearing was issued to the respondent personally, not to the attorney (I.J. May 22, 2012, Dec. at 1). Thus, the respondent fails to explain why he would rely on an unnamed “assistant” at attorney Azar’s office for the hearing date and time.
The respondent asserts that “we” (i.e., he and attorney Azar) went to about 3 Immigration Court hearings and the attorney’s unnamed “assistant” would call him the day before the hearing, and that this was their “normal procedure”; thus he suggests that it would have been reasonable for him to rely on the “““assistant” (Motion Exh. A, at 5-6; Dec. 17, 2014, Motion Exh. C, at 5). However, the record shows that the respondent was presented by a different attorney, not attorney Azar, at his prior hearings, and the notices of hearing were issued to the other attorney.

2

The respondent’s motion does not explain exactly when the respondent retained his current counsel. Although the respondent’s affidavit states that, after the Board’s decision dismissing his appeal, “[w]e found a lawyer right before the deadline for the appeal” (Motion Exh. A, at 7, ¶ 25), but the meaning of this phrase is not clear.


2015 WL 4510729 (BIA)

2015 WL 4510746 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AUGUSTIN CALDERON-LUGO
File: AXXX XX2 999 - San Antonio, TX
June 18, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul A. Esquivel, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 10, 2014, denying his motion to reopen to apply for asylum under section 208 of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1158, withholding of removal under section 241(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(3), and protection under the Convention Against Torture (CAT), 8 C.F.R. § 1208.16. The appeal will be dismissed.1
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003(d)(3).
 
The record reflects that the respondent was granted post-conclusion voluntary departure on May 7, 2013, to depart on or before September 4, 2013. On December 16, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reopen to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, finding that the respondent did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel and he did not establish prima facie eligibility for asylum, withholding of removal, or for protection under the Convention Against Torture (I.J. at 2).
 
On appeal, counsel for the respondent states that he has attempted to obtain the necessary information from the respondent’s two previous attorneys to determine whether he can proceed with a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, but he has not been provided the file from either of the attorneys. The respondent indicates that he is attempting to compile the necessary evidence for a viable non-frivolous complaint, and that he will submit supplements, including a completed complaint with the proper agencies upon accumulation of evidence. To date, the respondent has not submitted any additional evidence in support of his claim that he would be pursuing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim and he has not complied with the procedural requirements pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). The respondent has not submitted evidence that he has notified his former attorney of his ineffective assistance allegations; and there is no evidence that a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authority. Matter of Lozada, supra. Consequently, the respondent has not established that reopening is warranted on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent seeks reopening due to his fear of returning to Mexico, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established changed circumstances or changed country conditions to warrant reopening of proceedings. The respondent was represented by counsel when he requested and was granted voluntary departure, after being fully advised of the consequences if he failed to depart. The alleged failure of former counsel to inform the respondent of the availability of asylum, withholding of removal, and of protection under the Convention Against Torture does not create “changed country conditions” or “previously unavailable evidence” when the basis for the asylum claim arose before the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 7, 2013. The respondent’s fear of harm could have been presented at his previous hearing. There also has been no prima facie showing that upon return to Mexico the respondent would face torture by or with the consent or acquiescence of government officials (to include the concept of willful blindness) for the purposes of the CAT. In light of the foregoing, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established that reopening is warranted to consider his applications for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the CAT. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16(c)(2), 1208.18(a)(1). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Despite the respondent’s claim that he has been unable to obtain an audio recording of his underlying removal proceedings, we decline to order a transcript of these proceedings to be prepared or further extend the briefing schedule in this matter. A transcript is not ordinarily prepared where a motion to reopen is filed, since the motion will be decided on the basis of its merits rather than on the basis of a review of the record. See Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996) (noting transcript ordinarily not present when motion to reopen filed).

2015 WL 4510769 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MANDEEP SINGH
File: AXXX XX6 739 - Harlingen, TX
June 12, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eileen Collins Bretz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On November 8, 2011, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent’s removal in absentia, and on January 17, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(4)(ii), 1003.26. The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision on May 9, 2013. This case was last before the Board on July 26, 2013, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our decision. The respondent has now filed another motion to reopen the removal proceedings to rescind the in absentia order of removal to enable him to apply for asylum and withholding of removal. The motion will be denied.
 
It is undisputed that the respondent failed to appear at his scheduled removal hearing. The respondent, however, now raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Norma Sepulveda, Esquire, who represented him before the Immigration Judge. He also raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Laura Perez, Esquire, who filed his motion to reopen and rescind with the Immigration Judge, and his appeal and motion to reconsider with the Board, for failing to raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Attorney Sepulveda.
 
The respondent has attempted to comply with the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). He has not, however, shown that prior counsel rendered deficient performance resulting in prejudice. Id. See also Miranda-Lores v. I.N.S., 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994) (“To prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel at a deportation proceeding, an alien must show (1) ineffective representation and (2) substantial prejudice, which occurred as a result of the ineffective representation”); Ogbemudia v. I.N.S., 988 F.2d 595, 598 (5th Cir. 1993).
 
The respondent alleges that Attorney Sepulveda did not properly inform him of the consequences of the failure to appear, and did not provide him with notice of the removal hearing. The Notice to Appear, however, reveals that on June 15, 2011, the respondent was provided oral notice in the Punjabi language of the consequences of the failure to appear. See Exhibit 1. The record further reveals that notice of the November 8, 2011, hearing, was properly served on the respondent’s counsel, Attorney Sepulveda, who in response to the allegations against her, claims that she attempted to contact the respondent to no avail at his address of record, i.e., the address provided by his bond obligor. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (recognizing that notice to the attorney of record constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5 (notice of hearing sent to an alien’s attorney of record is sufficient notice). Indeed, the respondent states in the instant motion that “[a]lthough (he) had contact with his bond obligor, his bond obligor did not notify him of his hearing until he received notice from ICE requesting that he deliver (the respondent) at a set time and date.” See Respondent’s Motion at 5. Given this record, it has not been shown that the respondent’s failure to appear at his removal hearing was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of Attorney Sepulveda. As the respondent has not shown ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of Attorney Sepulveda, his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Attorney Perez lacks merit and will not be considered further.
 
*2 In sum, we are not persuaded to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order based on the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claims. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Further, as previously found, the respondent has not shown that his failure to appear at the November 8, 2011, removal hearing was the result of “exceptional circumstances” as defined at section 240(e)(1) of the Act. We also are not persuaded to reopen these proceedings to enable the respondent to apply for asylum and related relief as he has not set forth a claim of materially changed circumstances or conditions in his native country, India. See Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). Finally, the respondent has not set forth a case of exceptional circumstances warranting reopening in the exercise of our sua sponte authority. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 4510769 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN ISAC RIVERA-LOPES A.K.A. LUIS IDROVO URGILES A.K.A. MAURICIO TRELLES
File: AXXX XX8 905 - New York, NY
June 12, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brenna Torres, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s June 9, 2014, decision that denied his motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The record before us does not contain a brief from the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that he did not receive proper notice for the October 6, 2000, hearing. We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge.1 The respondent does not submit a sworn affidavit or an unsworn declaration under penalty of perjury, or satisfactorily explain why not. Leaving aside the “false name” issue, the respondent was obligated to provide to the Immigration Court an address at which he may be contacted regarding removal proceedings, and also promptly provide any change of address (Notice to Appear (Form I-862)). See sections 239(a)(1) and (2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229(a)(1) and (2). The respondent’s 2000 Federal income tax return (Motion Exh. G, at 73-75) was filed listing a Southampton, New York, address, and the W-2 lists an East Hampton, New York, address. He does not provide any evidence to show that he was living at the New York, New York, address [provided in his motion to change venue and provided for the Notice to EOIR: Alien Address (Form I-830)] during June of 2000 when the notice of hearing was sent.
 
To the extent that the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of former counsel (Gary Ortega), he does not satisfy any of the three requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) to properly make a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent requested reopening and administrative closure of his case. We look to the factors discussed in Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012). The reasons administrative closure is sought are not persuasive. The respondent can apply to the DHS for prosecutorial discretion without these proceedings being administratively closed, and voluntary departure is not a new form of relief. The DHS opposed the motion to reopen on the merits. Even if the respondent pursues prosecutorial discretion, he has not shown a likelihood of success. The delay from October of 2000 until March of 2014 is due to the respondent. He does not show that there would be a different ultimate outcome when the case is recalendared before the Immigration Judge or the appeal is reinstated before the Board. The factors in Matter of Avetisyan, supra, weigh against administrative closure.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

However, the DHS did not submit any evidence of the arrest, and he now submits a certificate of disposition [which does not warrant a remand] which shows that the criminal record was sealed.

2015 WL 4510768 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAFAEL MARTINEZ A.K.A. CARLOS ANTONIO TOBON-LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX5 380 - Hartford, CT
June 12, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ramiro Alcazar, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; termination of proceedings
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 10, 2014. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and ordered him removed. The respondent challenges denial of a continuance and challenges removability on appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was placed in proceedings on April 5, 2012. His first hearing was on May 14, 2012. He was granted a continuance to find an attorney (Tr. at 13). His second hearing was on September 10, 2012. He was granted a continuance for attorney preparation (Tr. at 15). On December 3, 2012, the respondent (through counsel) said he is not “Rafael Martinez,” denied all the factual allegations in the Notice to Appear (NTA), denied the charges of removability, and indicated that he intended to file a motion to suppress evidence and terminate proceedings. The Immigration Judge granted him a continuance to file that motion (Tr. at 19-21; Exhs. 2, 4-5).
 
On February 14, 2013, the respondent (through counsel) withdrew the motion to suppress evidence and terminate proceedings and withdrew his objection to consideration of the Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien (Form I-213), excepting the name of the respondent (Tr. at 48-50, 58-60, 63-66, 80, 94; I.J. at 2-3). On March 7, 2013, after the NTA was amended to add the respondent’s true name, the respondent admitted proper service of the NTA, admitted the factual allegations, conceded removability, designated Mexico for removal, and requested voluntary departure (Tr. at 58-60, 63-66; Exh. 6; I.J. at 2-3). The respondent was then granted continuances to pursue Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (DACA) and prosecutorial discretion (Tr. at 67-69; I.J. at 2; Exhs. 7-8). On March 10, 2014, the respondent indicated that DACA and prosecutorial discretion had been denied (Tr. at 73-74, 79, 92, 96-97; I.J. at 2).
 
The respondent (through counsel) then indicated that he was not seeking voluntary departure or other relief, indicated that he was amending the pleadings again to deny that proper service of the NTA was made and to deny the charges of removability, and he requested another continuance (Tr. at 97-98, 100-101; I.J. at 2-3). The Immigration Judge denied that request (I.J. at 3). The Immigration Judge found removability proved, regardless of the pleadings, based on the Form I-213 (Exh. 3) and ordered the respondent removed (I.J. at 3-4).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent1 challenges denial of the continuance. An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We agree that the respondent did not show good cause for another continuance.
 
Also on appeal, the respondent disputes removability. The respondent attempts to raise on appeal the issues raised in the motion to suppress evidence and terminate proceedings that he voluntarily withdrew before the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 48-50, 58-60, 63-66, 80, 94; I.J. at 2-3). The respondent argues that, despite the withdrawal of the motion, despite his withdrawal of objection to the Form I-213, despite his admission of the factual allegations and despite his concession of removability, he is not, in fact, removable. We find, however, that the prior withdrawal of the motion, withdrawal of objection to the Form I-213, admissions of fact, and concessions of removability are binding on the respondent. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal concession made by counsel is binding on the alien). Cf. Hoodho v. Holder, 558 F.3d 184, 191 (2d Cir. 2009) (holding that facts admitted by a party are “judicial admissions” that bind that party throughout the litigation). Moreover, we note that the respondent does not assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

We note that the same counsel that represented the respondent below represents the respondent on appeal.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DUY A. NGUYEN
File: AXXX XX4 732 - Atlanta, GA
June 10, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Charles H. Kuck, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sirce E. Owen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on March 12, 2015, when we denied the respondent’s untimely and number-barred motion to reopen. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider our decision denying his motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion does not convince us that reconsideration of our previous decision is warranted. A party seeking reconsideration requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of alleged legal or factual errors, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). Here, the respondent largely raises the same or similar arguments in the motion to reconsider as were raised in his prior motion to reopen. We considered those arguments and found them unpersuasive when viewed within the record as a whole before we determined that the respondent had not established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and denied his motion to reopen. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (stating that “a motion to reconsider is not a process by which a party may submit, in essence, the same brief presented on appeal...”). Disagreement with the result is not sufficient.
 
To the extent that the respondent is attempting to raise new arguments related to his compliance with the requirements laid out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec, 637 (BIA 1988), these arguments are not properly raised for the first time in a motion to reconsider. See O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (stating that “[a] motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied” and “arguments for consideration on appeal should all be submitted at one time, rather than in piecemeal fashion.”) (citation omitted). A motion to reconsider is neither a vehicle for advancing supplemental legal arguments that could have been previously raised nor a mechanism for submitting a late-filed brief. Moreove, the respondent did not file his bar complaint until after the Board issued its March 12, 2015, decision, finding that the respondent did not substantially comply with Matter of Lozada, supra. Regardless, none of the arguments or evidence presented, including the response from the respondent’s prior counsel, Mr. Fogel, convinces us of error in our ultimate determination that the respondent has not established prejudice by any actions or inactions of his former counsel (Motion at Tab I). Therefore, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 4537075 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2015 WL 4537102 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN GOMEZ-ALONZO
File: AXXX XX9 408 - Chicago, IL
June 4, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Royal F. Berg, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anastasie M. Senat
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s April 7, 2014, denial of his motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed. The respondent’s requests for oral argument, and en banc or 3-member panel review, are denied.
 
The Board reviews Immigration Judges’ findings of fact, including credibility determinations, for clear error, and we review all other matters on appeal de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
“A motion to reopen will not be granted unless the Immigration Judge is satisfied that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). Here, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as the respondent did not satisfy the requirements of reopening. See Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
There is no question that the evidence was previously available. The respondent could have presented the evidence at the former hearing, but did not do so because he expressed no concerns about returning to Guatemala. Since the respondent did not comply with the requirements of reopening, the Immigration Judge properly denied the motion.
 
In addition, as noted by the Immigration Judge, the DHS considered the respondent’s request for prosecutorial discretion and denied the request. Neither the Immigration Judge, nor this Board, has authority to exercise prosecutorial discretion in the respondent’s case. See Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281, 284 (BIA 1998). A request for prosecutorial discretion is a collateral matter, and the Immigration Judge did not need to continue the proceedings to allow the respondent to make such a request to the DHS. See generally Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348 (BIA 1982) (explaining that prosecutorial discretion is a function of the DHS; and that it was not error for the Immigration Judge to decline to continue the hearing for the alien to apply for a form of prosecutorial discretion for his or her case). Moreover, the respondent has not shown he was prejudiced by the continuance denial, as he has not shown he has been granted prosecutorial discretion by DHS; nor has he shown that he is eligible for any forms of relief from removal. See Bathula v. Holder, 723 F.3d 889, 903 n. 34 (7th Cir. 2013); Rapheal v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 521, 533 (7th Cir. 2008).
 
*2 The respondent contends that his due process rights were violated because, inter alia, he may not have been adequately advised regarding his avenues of relief. We disagree. In order to establish a denial of the right to due process, the respondent must show that he was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s actions. See Hasanaj v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 780, 785 (7th Cir. 2004); Wigglesworth v. INS, 319 F.3d 951, 960 (7th Cir. 2003). Here, the respondent was represented by counsel at his last two hearings and a Spanish interpreter was provided at all hearings. The motion was properly denied because while the respondent provided his statement and his Form I-589 application, the respondent did not establish that the alleged facts were new and previously unavailable. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3).
 
We also find without merit the respondent’s contention that he should have been able to withdraw pleadings. See generally Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (a respondent is bound by the tactical decisions made by his counsel). The respondent has not alleged ineffective assistance of counsel; nor has he complied with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent requests a transcription of the proceedings. The request is denied. Normally, a transcription of the hearing is not ordered from the appeal of a denial of a motion to reopen. If a review of the record indicates that transcription is important to adjudicate the appeal, the transcript will be ordered. Here, we are not persuaded that a transcript is necessary to resolve this appeal, because the digital audio recording of the hearing is available. Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 4537102 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2015 WL 4537109 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WAYNE LEWIS
File: AXXX XX9 875 - New York, NY
June 3, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Audrey A. Thomas, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Trinidad and Tobago, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s September 9, 2014, decision which denied his motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.1
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgment and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of her own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). In determining whether exceptional circumstances exist to excuse an alien’s failure to appear, the “totality of circumstances” pertaining to the alien’s case must be considered. Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, 509 (BIA 1996).
 
The respondent received personal service of the Notice to Appear on October 28, 2008 (Exh. 1). On November 6, 2008, the respondent appeared, conceded removability as charged in the Notice to Appear, and indicated his intention to apply for adjustment of status. On September 23, 2009, he failed to appear and was ordered removed in absentia. On November 2, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, which was granted. On September 15, 2010, he appeared with counsel who requested a continuance which was granted, and the matter was reset until September 29, 2010. He failed to appear on September 29, 2010, reportedly because he experienced a panic attack after seeing immigration officers and departed the building. The Immigration Judge adjourned the matter until October 1, 2010.
 
*2 Following a hearing conducted in absentia on October 1, 2010, at which the respondent failed to appear and the Department of Homeland Security (DBS) presented evidence, an Immigration Judge found him subject to removal as charged in the Notice to Appear, and ordered him removed from the United States.2 See generally section 240 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a. His counsel appeared and indicated that she did not know the respondent’s location. She requested to withdraw as counsel in February 2011.
 
On April 29, 2014, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. He alleged that he did not appear on October 1, 2010, because his attorney did not inform him of the October 1, 2010, hearing date, that he was experiencing financial difficulties, and essentially was homeless at the time. The respondent indicated that he did not attempt to contact his previous attorney because he owed her legal fees and believed that she would not speak to him.
 
The DHS filed a response in opposition to the motion to reopen, noting that the motion failed to address why the respondent did not appear on September 29, 2010, and did not offer any medical evidence to excuse his failure to appear. In addition, the DHS observed that notice to the respondent’s attorney for the hearings on September 29, and October 1, 2010, constituted effective notice to him, and that he had not filed an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his prior attorney.
 
On September 9, 2014, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to rescind the in absentia removal order because the respondent had failed to explain why he did not appear on September 29, 2010. She noted that his prior counsel had informed her on September 29, 2010, that the respondent did not appear because he had a panic attack after seeing immigration officers at the Immigration Court and had visited an emergency clinic. However, the respondent had not submitted any medical evidence to support this contention with his motion to reopen (I.J. at 3). In addition, the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) to establish an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and had failed to establish due diligence by waiting almost 4 years to file a motion to reopen with any explanation for the delay (Id.). Finally, the Immigration Judge determined that notice to the respondent’s counsel for the October 1, 2010, hearing constituted effective notice to him, and that he had not established exceptional circumstances to excuse his failure to appear (I.J. at 4).
 
The respondent submitted a Notice of Appeal which contained a short statement of reasons which alleged without elaboration that the Immigration Judge erred in her decision. His brief claimed that he was sick on September 29, 2010, and was vomiting, dizzy and had diarrhea. However, the brief does not address why he did not appear on October 1, 2010, and it does not reiterate the contention made in the motion to reopen that he failed to appear because his attorney did not inform him of the hearing date. Although he filed a brief, he requested and received an extension of the filing deadline to submit a supplemental brief with medical evidence. His request for a second extension was denied. He has not submitted any medical evidence to date.
 
*3 The respondent does not contest that he is subject to removal as charged, and he does not allege any error in the Immigration Judge’s decision that he failed to establish that his in absentia deportation order should be rescinded. In addition, his brief on appeal indicates that he did not appear because he was sick, which contradicts the claim in his motion that he did not appear because his prior attorney did not inform him of the hearing date.
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. Service of the Notice of Hearing on the alien’s representative is proper. Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (notice to alien’s counsel constitutes notice to alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). The respondent has not established that his absence at the scheduled hearing was the result of a lack of notice because his prior attorney received proper notice of the hearing. Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996) (alien seeking to reopen in absentia proceedings based upon her unsuccessful communications with attorney about hearing date did not establish exceptional circumstances where she failed to satisfy all of requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
To the extent that the respondent claimed that his prior attorney (who represents him on appeal) purportedly did not tell him he had a hearing on October 1, 2010, this allegation, without more, does not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel, especially in light of the respondent’s concession that he did not remain in contact with his attorney. In addition, the respondent could have contacted the Immigration Court directly. Furthermore, he has not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) to establish ineffective assistance of counsel because he has not filed a state bar complaint.
 
Moreover, for the reasons stated in the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent did not establish that his absence at the scheduled hearing was the result of exceptional circumstances, as required by section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. See section 240(e)(1) of the Act. See also Matter of B-A-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 1998) (“twisted foot” does not constitute exceptional circumstance); Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998) (strong headache did not constitute exceptional circumstance). See also Cabrera v. Gonzales, 229 Fed. Appx. 483 (9th Cir. 2007) (stomach infection, diarrhea and abdominal pain did not constitute exceptional circumstances or serious illness sufficient to merit reopening of in absentia order). To the extent that the respondent alleges that his panic attack on September 29, 2010, constituted an exceptional circumstance to excuse his failure to appear, he has not provided any medical evidence to substantiate his claim, or alleged that a panic attack on September 29, 2010, also was the reason he failed to appear on October 1, 2010. In addition, he failed to address or explain, either in his motion or on appeal, why he waited almost 4 years to file his motion to reopen.
 
*4 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has filed a request for a stay of removal. Given our disposition in this order the stay of removal is moot.

2

On April 7, 2010, the DHS filed lodged charges under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), alleging that the respondent had been convicted of two crimes involving moral turpitude not arising out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct. On September 9, 2014, the Immigration Judge did not sustain this charge because the DHS had not submitted evidence of the record of conviction for one of the convictions (I.J. at 2).
