2015 WL 3896291 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARMANDO RAMIREZ-CASTANEDA1
File: AXXX XX9 596 - York, PA
May 28, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elizabeth M. Trinidad, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William E. Lore
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the April 25, 2013, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings.2 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) requests summary affirmance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge did not consider Leslie v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 611 F.3d 171 (3d Cir. 2010), or certain defects in the Notice to Appeal. We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. The Immigration Judge’s findings of fact and analysis support his conclusion that the respondent did not satisfy his burden of proof as it relates to his motion to reopen (I.J. at 2-3).
 
Citing Leslie v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge committed reversible error, as there is no evidence that the Immigration Judge gave him a pro bono list of legal service providers (Resp. Br. at 5, 7-10). We disagree. The respondent does not deny that he was personally served with the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) (Form I-862) or that the NTA bears his signature. While the respondent argues that he may have been in deportation proceedings, the Immigration Judge found that “the original and only charging document in this case was a Notice to Appear, Form I-862, and dated February 17, 1998” (I.J. at 2). Further, the Immigration Judge reasonably noted that the NTA’s Certificate of Service indicates that the “respondent was provided the pro bono list” (I.J. at 2, n.2).
 
A motion to reopen may not be granted, unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4); Matter of Ige, 20 I&N Dec. 880, 884 (BIA 1994). The United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit (“Third Circuit”), in whose jurisdiction this case arises, has found that “the prima facie case standard for a motion to reopen . . . requires the applicant to produce objective evidence showing a ‘reasonable likelihood’ that he or she can establish” entitlement to relief. Guo v. Ashcroft, 386 F.3d 556, 563 (3d Cir. 2004) (citation omitted). Moreover, the Third Circuit has explained that “[p]rima facie scrutiny entails consideration of ‘the evidence that accompanies the motion as well as relevant evidence that may exist in the record of the prior hearing, in light of the applicable statutory requirements for relief.”DD’ Id. at 563 n.7 (citation omitted).
 
*2 The respondent has not established that these proceedings should be reopened on account of lack of proper notice. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent argues on appeal that the NTA may not have been signed by someone authorized to do so (Resp. Br. at 6, 12-13). However, as the respondent has not shown that any statute or regulation prohibits another immigration officer from signing on behalf of the issuing officer listed on the Notice to Appear, it follows that he has not shown that it constitutes a violation of a regulation. In addition, there is a presumption that public officers, including DHS employees, properly discharge their duties. See U. S. v. Chemical Foundation, Inc., 272 U.S. 1, 14-15 (1926); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27, 37 (BIA 1995) (citations omitted).
 
On this record, the respondent’s argument is insufficient to rebut the presumption of regularity. There is also no evidence that the respondent was prejudiced by the alleged defect in the Notice to Appear. The Notice to Appear included the allegations and charge against him and the alleged defect in the Notice to Appear did not prejudice the respondent because it did not interfere with his understanding of the charge against him or his ability to respond to that charge. See e.g., Kohli v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 1061 (9th Cir. 2007) (finding the Board complied with the regulations that vest jurisdiction in the Immigration Court and the alleged defect in the NTA did not deprive the Immigration Court of jurisdiction).
 
We concur with the Immigration Judge that the NTA was not defective for lacking the date, time, and place of the hearing (I.J. at 2). The respondent asserts that the NTA contravenes section 239(a)(1)(G) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1129(a)(1)(G) because the form itself does not state the date, time and place for the commencement of proceedings (Resp. Br. at 12-14). The respondent maintains that the NTA is thus defective. Contrary to the respondent’s appellate contentions, the absence of a specific date, time, and place for the initial removal hearing in the NTA does not render it “defective” (Resp. Br. at 12-14). See Matter of Camarillo, 25 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 2011). As noted in Matter of Camarillo, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.18(b) permits the NTA to include such scheduling information “where practicable.” The Immigration Judge reasonably determined that the respondent’s arguments are also undercut by the subsequent notice of hearing sent to the respondent’s counsel of record (I.J. at 2). Popa v. Holder, 571 F.3d 890 (9th Cir. 2009) (holding that a NTA that failed to include a date or time of alien’s deportation hearing but states that a date and time will be set later, is not defective as long as the notice of hearing is in fact later sent to the alien). In view of the circumstances presented, we are not persuaded by the arguments on appeal that the NTA was defective and jurisdiction was not conferred to the court.
 
*3 Finally, even if the NTA was defective, the violation of a regulation does not necessarily invalidate a removal proceeding. See Matter of Hernandez, 21 I&N Dec. 224, 226 (BIA 1996). Rather, we have held that the violation of a regulatory requirement invalidates a proceeding only where the regulation provides a benefit to the alien and the violation prejudiced the interest of the alien who was to be protected by the regulation. See Matter of Garcia-Flores, 17 I&N Dec. 325 (BIA 1996). The respondent does not contend that he is in this country pursuant to a lawful admission and is not subject to removal from the United States, nor does he allege that he is currently eligible for any form of relief from removal. Thus, he has not demonstrated that the regulation provides a benefit to him or that he was prejudiced by the individual signing the NTA.
 
The respondent moves that the proceedings be administratively closed so that he may apply for prosecutorial discretion before the DHS (Resp. Br. at 15-18). See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012) (setting forth the factors to consider in determining whether administrative closure of a proceeding is appropriate even when it is opposed by one of the parties). The exercise of prosecutorial discretion is a matter within the authority of the DHS, not of the Immigration Judge or the Board.3 See Matter of Bahta, 22 I&N Dec. 1381, 1391 (BIA 2000) (observing that the DHS has prosecutorial discretion, which includes the discretion to address the equities of individual cases in a manner that the rigid application of a broadly drawn statute often will not allow); see also Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998); Matter of Singh, 21 I&N Dec. 427, 435 (BIA 1996); Matter of Yazdani, 17 I&N Dec. 626, 630 (BIA 1981). As a general matter, the DHS is in the best position to assess its own priorities and whether its prosecutorial discretion should be exercised in a particular case. The potential exercise of discretion does not represent an “immigration benefit” over which the Immigration Court or this Board has jurisdiction or to which a respondent may claim entitlement.4 See generally, section 240 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a; 8 C.F.R. § § 1003.1(b), (d)(1)(i). The DHS has not joined in the respondent’s motion. Neither the Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction to compel the DHS to exercise prosecutorial discretion which may be granted at any stage of the proceedings. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2009) (holding that the Board lacks the authority to reopen a proceeding to allow an alien an opportunity to seek relief from removal if the Board and the Immigration Judge have no authority to grant the requested form of relief, especially where reopening is sought simply as a mechanism to stay the final order while the collateral matter is resolved by the agency or court having jurisdiction to do so).
 
*4 The Immigration Judge did not err in denying the respondent’s motion to terminate proceedings. The Immigration Judge only has the authority delegated to him or her by the Attorney General. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.10(b). It is the function of the Immigration Judge to adjudicate the case and not to determine when or whether charges are brought. See 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(a) and (b); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.1(a) (authority of the Immigration Judge). The initiation of removal proceedings through the issuance of a Notice to Appear is covered by section 239 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229; see 8 C.F.R. §§ 2.1, 239.2(a) issuance of Notice to Appear). The DHS, not an Immigration Judge, has prosecutorial discretion to commence proceedings. Matter of Bahta, 22 I&N Dec. 1381, 1391 (BIA 2000) (INS has unreviewable prosecutorial discretion); Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998); Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 350 (BIA 1982). Further, section 240 of the Act confers prosecutorial discretion upon the DHS to commence proceedings, and once proceedings have been commenced, it is the responsibility of the Immigration Judge to adjudicate them. See Matter of Lujan-Quintana, 25 I&N Dec. 53, 56 (BIA 2009) (“DHS has inherent flexibility, in the exercise of prosecutorial discretion, to pursue removal of an alien in either expedited removal proceedings or proceedings under section 240 of the Act.”); see also Matter of Bahta, supra; Matter of G-N-C-, supra; Matter of Quintero, supra.
 
The respondent’s motion does not present “exceptional circumstances” that would warrant the exercise of our discretionary authority to reopen his proceedings sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Having considered the respondent’s appellate contentions (Resp. Br. at 15-18), reopening and administrative closure is also not warranted because we do not have the authority to grant relief from removal solely on equitable or humanitarian grounds. See Matter of Silva-Rodriguez, 20 I&N Dec. 448 (BIA 1992); Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734, 742 (BIA 1988). Under these circumstances, administrative closure of the proceedings is not warranted under Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012).
 
*5 We acknowledge that enforcement of the removal order may result in hardship to the respondent and family members. However, the Immigration Judge and the Board do not have jurisdiction to fashion equitable remedies or grant general humanitarian relief. See Matter of Medina, supra.
 
In general, motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days from the date “on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceedings sought to be reopened,” see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); see also section 240(c)(7)(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C), although this time limit is subject to equitable tolling. Alzaarir v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 639 F.3d 86, 90 (3d Cir. 2011). The respondent does not dispute that his motion to reopen was filed more than 90 days after the administratively final decision. Rather, he argues that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion by refusing to equitably toll the 90-day time limitation for filing a motion to reopen on the basis of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel (Resp. Br. at 14-15).
 
Ineffective assistance of counsel can serve as a basis for equitable tolling, but it must be substantiated and accompanied by a showing of due diligence. See Mahmood v. Gonzales, All F.3d 248, 252 (3d Cir. 2005). An alien seeking to equitably toll the limitation period based on ineffective assistance of counsel must demonstrate that he has exercised due diligence in raising the claim, and that prejudice resulted from counsel’s alleged errors. Mahmood v. Gonzales, supra, at 251-52; Fadiga v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 488 F.3d 142, 159 (3d Cir. 2007). Prejudice requires an alien to show that there was a “reasonable likelihood that the result would have been different if the error[s] ... had not occurred.” Fadiga v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 159. “Periods of unaccounted-for delay reveal a lack of diligence.” Mahmood v. Gonzales, supra, at 253; see also Alzaarir v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 90 (“Due diligence must be exercised over the entire period for which tolling is desired.”). “This includes both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed.” Alzaarir v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra. The failure to exercise due diligence in asserting an ineffective assistance of counsel undermines any claim for equitable tolling. “Equitable tolling is an extraordinary remedy which should be extended only sparingly.” Mahmood v. Gonzales, supra, at 253 (quoting Hedges v. U.S., 404 F.3d 744, 751 (3d Cir. 2005)).
 
*6 We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s “““assertions of counsel ineffectiveness are without merit” (I.J. at 2). On this record, the respondent has not demonstrated compliance with the procedural requirements for establishing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988).5 The Third Circuit has adopted Lozada’s procedural requirements, but strict compliance is not always required. Pajow v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 515 F.App’x 119, 121 (3d Cir. 2013) (unpublished); Rranci v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 540 F.3d 165, 172-74 (3d Cir. 2008). Having considered the respondent’s appellate contentions, we conclude that he has not demonstrated substantial compliance with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra (Resp. Br. at 7, 14-15). Moreover, the respondent has not accounted for the period of delay in pursuing his ineffective assistance of counsel claim.
 
To the extent the respondent asserts a due process violation, we are unpersuaded. On this record, the respondent has not shown that the Immigration Judge denied the respondent due process of law by denying his motion to reopen. The respondent has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for a form of relief that either the Immigration Judge or the Board has the authority to grant. Moreover, although the Immigration Judge did not discuss every piece of evidence referred to by the respondent, he did consider the relevant evidence of record. Huang v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., 620 F.3d 372, 388 (3d Cir. 2010) (finding that Immigration Judges need not discuss every piece of evidence mentioned by an asylum applicant). We do not conclude that the respondent was in any way prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s handling of her case, or that the hearing was unfair and violated due process. See Delgado-Sobalvarro v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 625 F.3d 782, 787 (3d Cir. 2010); see also Khan v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., supra, at 236; Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984); Matter of Exame, 18 I&N Dec. 303 (BIA 1982).
 
Further, the respondent has not demonstrated that the Immigration Judge abandoned his impartial role. An Immigration Judge has the authority to “““interrogate, examine, and cross-examine the alien” in removal proceedings. See section 240(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(1). See generally Matter of Rhee, 16 I&N Dec. 607 (BIA 1978). Due process requires the alien be afforded a full and fair hearing; however, an Immigration Judge has broad discretion in how he conducts that hearing. See generally Romanishyn v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., 455 F.3d 175, 185 (3d Cir. 2006); Khan v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., 448 F.3d 226, 236 (3d Cir. 2006); Abdulrahman v. Ashcroft, 330 F.3d 587, 596 (3d Cir. 2003). We have reviewed the record and find no basis to conclude that the Immigration Judge was biased or that the Immigration Judge’s questioning or comments in any way exceeded judicial bounds. See Liteky v. United States, 510 U.S. 540, 556 (1994); Abdulrahman v. Ashcroft, supra, at 597; cf. Offutt v. United States, 348 U.S. 11 (1954) (finding that the record reveals an attitude inconsistent with the restraints of conventional judicial demeanor).
 
*7 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD

Matter of Lozada, supra.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LESY ERENIO SANCHEZ-MATUTE
File: AXXX XX8 848 - San Antonio, TX
May 14, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paula J. Ferreira, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 7, 2014, decision denying her motion to reopen. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on July 8, 2013. The appeal will be sustained, the proceedings reopened and the record remanded.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent claimed in her motion to reopen that exceptional circumstances prevented her from attending her July 8, 2013, removal hearing (Resp. Mot. to Reopen at 3-6). Specifically, respondent, through counsel, asserted that her attorney advised her that her pending motion to change venue would be granted and she was not required to attend the hearing; and also that her counsel suffered a computer virus that made his calendar inaccessible (Resp. Mot. to Reopen at 3-6). The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding that the mere existence of a pending motion to change venue did not relieve the respondent of her duty to attend the hearing, and that the computer virus did not amount to exceptional circumstances (I.J. at 2). On appeal, the respondent, through the same counsel, maintains her claim of exceptional circumstances and further asserts that she is not required to comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), because she is not claiming ineffective assistance of counsel, only that counsel’s incorrect advice constitutes an exceptional circumstance (Resp. Br. at 7).
 
The respondent is correct that both this Board and the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this matter arises, have found that the erroneous advice of counsel may constitute an exceptional circumstance excusing the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing. See Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (1996); see also Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzales, 484 F.3d 798 (5th Cir. 2007). However, in such a scenario, substantial compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, is still required.1 See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496-98 (5th Cir. 2000); see also Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 648 (5th Cir. 2014); Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948, 953 (5th Cir. 2012).
 
*2 Based on the totality of the circumstances in this case, we will grant the respondent’s motion to reopen to rescind her in absentia order pursuant to our sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, supra, and Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzales, supra, each of the respondents were represented by new counsel and had already complied with Matter of Lozada, supra. See Galvez-Vergara, supra, at 800, 800 n.2; see also Grijalva-Barrera, supra, at 473-74. Here, the respondent is represented by the same counsel who incorrectly advised her on her need to appear for the hearing and has not filed any complaint regarding this conduct. Further, it appears counsel still has not advised the respondent regarding the need to comply with Matter of Lozada, supra.

2015 WL 3932324 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDWARD PSZCZOLA
File: AXXX XX5 199 - Chicago, IL
May 8, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Edwin T Gania, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Geoffrey P. Gilpin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; adjustment of status
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 17, 2013, ordering him removed after denying his request for a continuance. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) opposes the respondent’s appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
The Immigration Judge granted the respondent 2 continuances prior to his final hearing (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 9-11, 21-24). In denying the respondent’s request for yet another continuance, the Immigration Judge considered the factors outlined in Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009), and Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009) (I.J. at 3). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6; Matter of Villarreal-Zuniga, 23 I&N Dec. 886, 891 (BIA 2006). The Immigration Judge specifically considered the respondent’s lengthy residence and family ties in this country, and other positive factors such as his business and ownership of rental properties (I.J. at 3-4). The Immigration Judge also considered the DHS’s opposition to any further continuances based on the respondent’s criminal record and his undesirability as a permanent resident (I.J. at 3). The Immigration Judge determined that even if the underlying visa petition were approved, the respondent did not warrant adjustment of status as a matter of discretion (I.J. at 4-5). Pawlowska v. Holder, 623 F.3d 1138, 1140-41 (7th Cir. 2010) (where Immigration Judge denied a continuance based on the conclusion that he would ultimately deny adjustment of status as a discretionary matter).
 
Although the underlying visa petition has since been approved (Respondent’s Brief, Exh. A), we find no basis to remand this matter, or to otherwise disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision, as we agree that the respondent has not demonstrated that he warrants adjustment of status as a matter of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
*2 The Immigration Judge properly weighed and balanced the factors relevant to the exercise of discretion in this case. Although the respondent has many equities and his removal will result in some hardship, as described by the Immigration Judge and the. respondent on appeal (Respondent’s Brief at 5-9), he also has an extensive history of drinking and driving, which the Immigration Judge properly considered as a significant negative factor. Matter of Teixeira, 21 I&N Dec. 316, 321 (BIA 1996) (evidence of criminal activity should be considered in discretionary determinations). The Immigration Judge did not clearly err in finding the respondent’s repeated drank driving violations were serious and that the respondent was a danger to society (I.J. at 5). Portillo-Rendon v. Holder, 662 F.3d 815, 816 (7th Cir. 2011) (finding that the alien exhibited a “disdain for the rules that govern the use of automobiles. Driving while intoxicated or without a license reflects both indifference to the welfare of other drivers and pedestrians and defiance of known legal obligations.”). The respondent’s repeated disregard for the welfare of others demonstrates a pattern of knowingly placing others at risk of harm. For the reasons discussed above and by the Immigration Judge we agree that the respondent’s positive equities are outweighed by the negative factors, and that the respondent has failed to carry his burden of proving that he merits adjustment of status as a matter of discretion. Id; Matter of Blas, 15 I&N Dec. 626, 628-30 (BIA 1974; A.G. 1976); Matter of Arai, 13 I&N Dec. 494 (BIA 1970).
 
While we sympathize with the respondent’s family members, who will undoubtedly experience some hardship in connection with his removal, our overriding obligation is to safeguard the interests of the United States, and not just those of the respondent and those close to him.
 
Although the respondent argues that his former counsel’s inadequate preparation negatively impacted his case (Respondent’s Brief at 3-5), he has failed to meet the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). For example, the respondent has not provided an affidavit setting forth his agreement with former counsel, has not shown that former counsel was given the opportunity to respond to the allegations, and has not filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority. Matter of Lozada, supra. Further, he has not established prejudice. Apouviepseakoda v. Gonzales, 475 F.3d 881, 885 (7th Cir. 2007) (alien must establish prejudice before court would vacate the agency’s decision); Boci v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 762, 768 (7th Cir. 2007) (alien cannot prevail without showing that granting a continuance likely would have affected the result of the hearing). Additionally, the respondent’s disagreement with the Immigration Judge’s decision does not render his conduct a violation of due process (Respondent’s Brief at 3-5). See Khan v. Filip, 554 F.3d 681, 689 (7th Cir. 2009); Zamora-Mallari v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 679, 694 (7th Cir. 2008). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 3932324 (BIA)
End of Document

2015 WL 3896273 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ESSAM N. SHARAFI
File: AXXX XX6 518 - Chicago, IL
May 6, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jesus Ruiz Velasco, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michelle M. Venci
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; adjustment of status
 
The respondent, a native of Saudi Arabia and a citizen of Pakistan, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s November 22, 2013, decision denying his request for a continuance, finding he had abandoned his application for adjustment of status, and ordering him deported from the United States to Pakistan. During the pendency of this appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The record will be remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
The respondent seeks adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), based on an approved immediate relative visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife. At the scheduled merits hearing on November 22, 2013, the respondent requested a continuance for several reasons. He had just retained current counsel who had only recently reviewed the record of proceedings. The respondent’s prior counsel had provided current counsel with the respondent’s file just days before the merits hearing. The respondent’s United States citizen wife was taking exams in Pakistan and could not be present at the hearing. And, the respondent had just been indicted for a controlled substance crime and the criminal proceedings were pending. See Respondent’s Motion to Continue.
 
The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent did not establish good cause for a continuance and denied his motion (Tr. at 33-34). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29 (a motion to continue may be granted for good cause). She found the continuance motion untimely filed as the written motion was submitted in Court the day of the merits hearing. She determined that the respondent’s current counsel had reviewed the record 2 weeks prior to the hearing and had done nothing to prepare the case, including filing required documents (medical report and affidavit of support) and ensuring the biometrics were complete. She determined that there was no reason to continue based on the respondent’s pending criminal case because the outcome was speculative. She observed that the respondent had been in proceedings since 2009. The Immigration Judge also concluded that because the respondent did not complete the required biometrics, he abandoned his applications for relief (Tr. at 34-35). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.47(d).
 
*2 During the pendency of this appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand premised on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his prior counsel. We find a remand of the record is warranted in this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992) (the requirements for a motion to remand are essentially the same as the requirements for a motion to reopen); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4). The documentation filed in support of the respondent’s motion to remand is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent’s motion is supported by documentation that satisfies the procedural requirements articulated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (holding that an alien making an ineffective assistance claim must: (1) present an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement with former counsel and representations by counsel in this regard; (2) inform counsel of allegations of ineffective conduct and afford counsel the opportunity to respond, and (3) file a bar complaint or explain the reasons for not doing so).
 
The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) agrees that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Lozada, but asserts that the respondent has failed to establish that prior counsel prejudiced his case. See DHS Brief at 9-10. The DHS relies on the finding of the Attorney Registration and Disciplinary Commission of the Supreme Court of Illinois, that the respondent did not establish by clear and convincing evidence that prior counsel violated the Rules of Professional Conduct. See Respondent’s Brief, Exhibit D.
 
However, according to the Attorney Registration and Disciplinary Commission of the Supreme Court of Illinois, prior counsel stated that he filed a motion to terminate on February 2, 2012, he filed an adjustment application on July 3, 2012, and he prepared and filed all paperwork for which the respondent was eligible. See Respondent’s Brief, Exhibit D. Yet, the record does not contain a motion to terminate. The adjustment application appears to have been incorrectly filed with the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). And, the respondent had not updated his biometric information. Although prior counsel represented the respondent for nearly 2 years, the record reveals that the only document he filed was a motion for substitution of counsel.
 
Because the respondent has submitted evidence that has not yet been considered by the Immigration Judge, which potentially bears on her rulings, we will remand the record for further factual findings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (stating that the Board may not engage in fact finding in the course of deciding appeals except for taking administrative notice of commonly known facts); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002) (in light of Board’s limited fact-finding ability on appeal, a remand is appropriate when the record is inadequate for review). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 3896273 (BIA)

2015 WL 3483401 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JULIO ALVARO CARTAGENA-AVELAR
File: AXXX XX8 174 - Los Angeles, CA
April 30, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Roger H. Ponce, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s December 11, 2014, decision denying his motion to reopen, which he filed on October 6, 2014. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia on October 23, 2003, for his failure to appear for a scheduled hearing. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
In his Motion to Reopen, the respondent challenged the in absentia order of removal, arguing that he did not receive timely notice of the October 23, 2003, hearing because (1) he was not living at the address of record due to a dispute with his aunt, with whom he was living, and (2) he did not receive notice of the hearing from his attorney, who had appeared at a prior hearing on his behalf. The respondent argues both that he did not receive notice of the hearing, and that his failure to appear should be excused based on exceptional circumstances as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). The motion to reopen did not contain evidence to support the contentions about a lack of notice or exceptional circumstances, such as an affidavit from the respondent or his aunt, or evidence that the respondent was pursuing any claims against his former attorney, who he alleges failed to provide him with adequate representation and/or a copy of the notice of the hearing. The respondent did provide with his motion copies of the birth certificates of his children and step-daughter, and evidence supporting the medical needs of his step-daughter.
 
In general, only one motion to reopen may be filed to reopen an administratively final order of removal. This must be filed within 90 days of the entry of such order, state new facts that will be proven, and be supported by evidence of those facts. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii), an order of removal entered in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Act may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal, if based on exceptional circumstances as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). A motion to reopen based on a lack of notice under section 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act may be filed at any time. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
*2 In her decision denying the motion, the Immigration Judge made the following factual findings, without clear error. First, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent entered as a juvenile1 and was released into the custody of his aunt (I.J. at 1). He appeared at a hearing on February 27, 2003, and admitted the charge of inadmissibility (Id.). A notice resetting the respondent’s hearing until September 18, 2003, was mailed to the respondent in June 2003 to his address of record (his aunt’s home) and to his attorney (I.J. at 1-2). On September 18, 2003, the respondent did not appear but his attorney who was present indicated that he had made attempts to contact the respondent (I.J. at 2). At the attorney’s request, proceedings were continued (I.J. at 2). A notice was personally served on the attorney indicating that the next hearing would occur on October 23, 2003 (Id.). On that date, the attorney appeared and indicated that he had made multiple attempts to contact the respondent, but the respondent again failed to appear and was ordered removed in absentia (Id.). The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed some 11 years later, on October 6, 2014 (Id.).
 
Based on the above, we find no cause to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision. To the extent that the respondent argues that exceptional circumstances prevented him from attending the September and October 2003 hearings in his immigration proceedings--specifically that he lacked maturity and was in a conflict with his aunt, with whom he was living2--we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the argument was not raised within the requisite 180 days, as set by regulation (I.J. at 2-3). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Indeed, the respondent did not file his motion for over 10 years after the entry of the in absentia order of removal. Moreover, the respondent bears the burden to establish that exceptional circumstances existed, and he has not met this burden inasmuch as he has not proffered evidence (such as a sworn affidavit from him or his aunt) to show that he was not living at his address of record, and was prevented from accessing his mail. Statements of counsel in a brief or other filing are not evidence. Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).3
 
To the extent that the respondent argues that they delay in filing his motion is immaterial because he never received notice of the October 2003 hearing, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that this argument is unavailing, in light of the fact that the notice was served on his attorney of record. See I.J. at 2. Service of the notice of hearing on the respondent’s attorney was adequate to reflect that notice was provided under applicable precedent. Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181, 185 (2001). Although the respondent argues in the motion and on appeal that the attorney did not provide adequate representation and did not provide him with the notice, this claim lacks support. The contention that the attorney only “made one appearance” on the respondent’s behalf, on February 27, 2003, is belied by the fact that the attorney actually appeared twice thereafter when the respondent failed to do so. The respondent has not argued that this finding is clearly erroneous. No affidavit from the respondent avers that the attorney failed to give him notice of the hearing. Indeed, the respondent has not supported any claim of ineffective assistance of counsel with, e.g., notice of such a claim to the attorney or applicable state bar office, or by filing an affidavit detailing the agreement between himself and the attorney. Cf. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*3 We find no cause to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen these proceedings. As the Immigration Judge noted, the motion lacks evidence to support it. Furthermore, the respondent’s claim that he is eligible to apply for adjustment of status lacks support in the form of evidence that he is the beneficiary of an approved, immediately available visa (I.J. at 4).
 
For the foregoing reasons, the appeal will be dismissed and the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent states on appeal that he was 17 years old when he entered the United States in June 2001 (Respondent’s Brief at 2).

2

The respondent also claims that tension arose with his aunt when his then-girlfriend got pregnant and he had to move out to get a full-time job to support his girlfriend and expected child. See Respondent’s Brief at 3. No evidence regarding the birth of this child is part of the record.

3

We note that even the statements in the motion are vague in this regard, since it is unclear exactly when the respondent stopped residing at his aunt’s home, given his failure to appear for the September 2003 hearing, and the fact that the notice of this hearing was mailed to him in June 2003, just a few months after he provided notice of this address to immigration officials. Therefore, even if they were taken as true, the assertions would not establish exceptional circumstances.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEONARDO CAHUEQUE-SANTOS A.K.A. LEONARDO CAHUEQUE-SANTOS
File: AXXX XX8 745 - Denver, Colorado
April 28, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew W. Buck, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 By a decision of an Immigration Judge of November 7, 2013, the motion to reopen filed by the respondent on September 4, 2013, was denied. The respondent appealed.1 The appeal will be dismissed based upon the respondent’s failure to file an appeal brief after stating in his Notice of Appeal that such a brief would be filed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(E). Moreover, the motion asserted a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel however the respondent has failed to comply with the requirements necessary to make such a claim. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988). Although, the respondent has submitted an affidavit, as well as one prepared by a former counsel, no bar complaint has been filed. Therefore, we agree with the Immigration Judge that there is insufficient basis to grant the respondent’s motion to reopen. To the extent that the respondent seeks humanitarian relief to enable him to remain in the United States, such relief is to be pursued with the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). Likewise, any request for relief from removal in the exercise of prosecutorial discretion must also be made to the DHS. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

A notice of withdrawal was filed on February 19, 2014. However, that notice, bearing the name and alien number relating to the respondent, references another person in the body of the notice, and, thus, absent certainty that it relates to the respondent, we disallow withdrawal of the appeal on the basis of the aforenoted notice.

2015 WL 3488723 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DANIEL RAZO-NICOLAS A.K.A. HECTOR SANCHEZ-ARAUZA
File: AXXX XX1 136 - Denver, CO
April 15, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alexander D. McShiras, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kalin Ivany
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s July 22, 2013, denial of his motion to reopen. The respondent’s request for oral argument will be denied and his appeal will be dismissed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2015).
 
As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent--through counsel--admitted the allegations contained in the Notice to Appear (Exh. 1) and conceded the above charge of removability (I.J. at 1 (citing Exh. 2 (Respondent’s Written Pleadings)). After granting the respondent a continuance on August 23, 2012-- for the adjudication of an I-130 visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife--the Immigration Judge granted his ensuing request for pre-conclusion voluntary departure on January 10, 2013 (i.e., based on his counsel’s statement that the I-130 petition was approved and that the respondent sought voluntary departure so he could adjust status through consular processing) (I.J. at 1-2). See I.J.’s January 10, 2013, Order; see also I.J. at 2-3 (noting that the respondent, despite being informed in Spanish of the consequences of failing to comply with his voluntary departure order, stayed in the country beyond April 10, 2013, the last day in which he could depart while still complying with the Immigration Judge’s Order). The respondent claims in his motion to reopen--which was filed on May 16, 2013-- that he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel because his prior attorney denied him a reasonable opportunity to present his applications for adjustment of status and Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (“DACA”). See Respondent’s Motion at 7-16.
 
We find that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion. On appeal, the respondent takes issue with the Immigration Judge’s decision that he did not comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), in filing a motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 3). Despite the respondent’s varied contentions on appeal (Respondent’s Br. at 9-14), he has shown no error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that he did not meet Lozada’s requirements because he “submitted no independent evidence of having informed [his prior counsel] Mr. De Pedro of the allegations against him” (I.J. at 3).1 See Tang v. Ashcroft, 354 F.3d 1192, 1196 (10th Cir. 2003) (in order to assert an ineffective assistance claim, an alien must comply with the Board’s Lozada requirements). Nonetheless, even assuming that the respondent satisfied Lozada’s requirements as he contends, he has not demonstrated that reopening is warranted.
 
*2 The Immigration Judge correctly held that the respondent’s motion was filed beyond the 90-day filing period and is therefore untimely (I.J. at 3). See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) (a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the final order of removal, which in this case was January 10, 2013). In this regard, while equitable tolling may permit reopening beyond the above time limit when an alien shows that he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel--and was prejudiced as a result--we see no error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent “““does not merit equitable tolling because he . . . has not presented evidence that he acted with due diligence in seeking to reopen his case” (I.J. at 3).
 
As discussed by the Immigration Judge, none of the respondent’s materials in his motion to reopen “indicate[d] that he took any steps to discover whether he had any alternate options [regarding his immigration status] until after the expiration of the voluntary departure and reopening periods” (I.J. at 3) (emphasis added). See Department of Homeland Security’s (“DHS”) Br. at 4 (noting that the respondent did not file his motion to reopen “until over one month after he failed to depart”). Indeed, the respondent’s own affidavit-- which was attached to his motion to reopen--is dated April 10, 2013 (i.e., the last day in which he could timely file a motion to reopen following the Immigration Judge’s January 10, 2013, order) and fails to even discuss his prior attorney’s alleged ineffective assistance. We are not persuaded by the respondent’s account that his purported confusion regarding the voluntary departure order somehow justifies his “mistaken impression that he could appeal the decision at any time” (Respondent’s Br. at 6). The respondent articulates no plausible basis that would reasonably explain his belief in this regard. See, e.g., Riley v. INS, 310 F.3d 1253 (10th Cir. 2002); Galvez Pineda v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 833, 838-39 (10th Cir. 2005) (discussing the petitioners’ lack of sufficient diligence to justify equitable tolling of the 90-day period for seeking reopening and concluding that “any absence of evidence favorable to equitable tolling was the [petitioners’] fault, as they had the burden of persuasion on the motion to reopen”).
 
Moreover, the fact that the respondent’s complaint against Mr. De Pedro was dismissed on April 23, 2013, by the Colorado Office of Attorney Regulation Counsel belies his claim that he detrimentally relied on “Mr. De Pedro’s bad advice” (Respondent’s Br. at 6, 12). To the extent that he cites Dada v. Mukasey, 554 U.S. 1, 18-23 (2008), for the proposition that filing a motion to reopen will nullify the consequence of failing to depart within the voluntary departure period (Respondent’s Br. at 7), the respondent does not recognize that the United States Supreme Court held that filing a motion to reopen during the voluntary departure period and requesting a withdrawal of voluntary departure will nullify the consequence of failing to timely depart. See id.; see also DHS’s Br. at 5-6. As the respondent filed his motion well after the expiration of the period of voluntary departure, his reliance on Dada v. Mukasey, supra, is misplaced. Based on the foregoing, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the motion was untimely, and equitable tolling of the time limit was unwarranted as the respondent’s claims of due diligence are not supported by the record (I.J. at 3). See generally Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (allowing equitable tolling of deadlines on motions to reopen “when a petitioner is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error, as long as the petitioner acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error”).
 
*3 Furthermore, because the respondent has remained beyond the period of voluntary departure, the voluntary departure order has terminated and converted to an order of removal to Mexico (I.J. at 4). Also, given his failure to timely depart and decision to file a motion to reopen outside of the voluntary departure period, he is subject to the consequences of failing to depart under the Act, including being barred from adjustment of status for a period of 10 years (I.J. at 4). See section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(a), (e)(2). Despite the respondent’s arguments on appeal (Respondent’s Br. at 7-9), he has not cited to any binding case law to show that he is not subject to the penalty provisions for failure to depart within the time period afforded for voluntary departure under section 240B(d)(1) of the Act. See DHS’s Br. at 4 (noting that the respondent (1) was granted voluntary departure until April 10, 2013; (2) accepted the Court’s decision in this regard; and (3) did not show that his non-compliance with the Court’s order was the result of his prior attorney’s representation as he opted to remain in the country after receiving the necessary warnings and consequences regarding failing to comply with the voluntary departure order). Moreover, the Board lacks authority to apply an “exceptional circumstances” or any other equitable exception to the penalty provisions for failure to depart within the time period afforded for voluntary departure under section 240B(d)(1) of the Act. See Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87, 92 (BIA 2007) (noting that the voluntary departure agreement comes with warnings of the consequences of failing to depart within the time granted while citing the penalties listed under section 240B(d) of the Act).2
 
In addition, upon our de novo review under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii), we conclude that the respondent did not establish any prejudice as a result of his prior attorney’s representation. See Matter of Assaad, supra, at 556. Despite the respondent’s contentions on appeal (Respondent’s Br. at 13-17), he has not met his burden of proving prejudice or fundamental unfairness. See U.S. v. Sandoval, 390 F.3d 1294, 1299 (10th Cir. 2004). Specifically, he has not shown that but for the ineffective assistance of counsel, there was a reasonable likelihood that he would have been entitled to a different outcome, which in this context would be continued residence in the United States. See U.S. v. Aguirre-Tello, 353 F.3d 1199, 1208 (en banc) (10th Cir. 2004) (A showing of prejudice means that there is a reasonable likelihood that, but for the errors complained of, the applicant would not have been deported) (internal citations omitted). In particular, the respondent cites to no objectively reliable evidence in the record to support his contention that his prior attorney erred by conceding that he “was not inspected and admitted to the United States,” and as such, he has not shown that he is in this country pursuant to a lawful admission and is not subject to removal (Respondent’s Br. at 14-16).
 
*4 Also, despite stating that his former attorney failed to apply for DACA and adjustment of status (Respondent’s Br. at 14), the respondent has not established eligibility for adjustment of status given his inadmissibility under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act, and he has not shown a reasonable likelihood of obtaining DACA relief. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988) (an applicant seeking reopening must establish prima facie eligibility for the underlying relief sought). Further, as to the latter, neither the Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction over the DHS regarding issues of prosecutorial discretion. See, e.g., Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-50 (BIA 1982) (deferred action status is a function of DHS’s prosecutorial grace). To the extent that he states that “former counsel failed to seek administrative closure” under Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012) (Respondent’s Br. at 14), he has not provided any meaningfully discussion of the relevant factors we announced in Matter of Avetisyan, supra, in examining weather administrative closure is warranted. We likewise find no prejudice by his former counsel’s “fail[ure] to preserve an appeal of the voluntary departure decision” given that a grant of voluntary departure prior to completion of proceedings under section 240B(a) of the Act requires an express waiver of the alien’s right to appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1).
 
In sum, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent has not met his burden of establishing that the filing deadline for his motion to reopen should be equitably tolled and we are unable to find that he was prejudiced due to his prior attorney’s representation as he has not shown that there was a reasonable likelihood of qualifying for the relief sought but for such representation. See Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413, 420 (1996). While the respondent has offered several documents on appeal, this Board may not accept such materials into the record as it is precluded from fact-finding in the course of deciding appeals. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). Further, as the respondent has not established any legal or clear factual error in the Immigration Judge’s decision, we find no basis to reopen or to remand this case for any reason. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Under Matter of Lozada, supra, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must be supported by (1) an affidavit setting out the agreement entered into with counsel, (2) proof that notice of the allegations and an opportunity to respond has been provided to counsel whose representation was allegedly ineffective, and (3) the filing of a complaint with appropriate disciplinary authorities or an explanation for the failure to file a complaint. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 561-62 (BIA 2003) (requiring prejudice stemming from prior counsel’s actions in addition to compliance with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra).

2

We have held that, absent egregious circumstances, a tactical decision by an attorney is binding on his or her client. See Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938, 942 (BIA 1995); Matter of Valasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986). We do not find that the instant case demonstrates egregious circumstances (Respondent’s Br. at 14-15). See generally Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 934 (9th Cir. 1986) (finding that the decision to forego contesting deportability in favor of a generous grant of voluntary departure is a tactical choice, while stating that the attorney “did so based on his belief that pending amnesty legislation would allow petitioners to become lawful permanent residents,” and “[a]lthough looking at this now [in hindsight] we might find this tactical choice to have been unwise, we nevertheless find that it did not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel”).

2015 WL 1978624 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUEBEN ARCHIBALD NELSON
File: AXXX XX5 243 - Hartford, CT
March 27, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jack Andreas Krumbein, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leigh Mapplebeck
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision of November 20, 2013, denying his untimely motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) requests that the Immigration Judge’s decision be upheld. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews findings of fact by an Immigration Judge only to determine whether they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(I); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 464-65 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Motions to reopen are generally disfavored, and the limitations upon them are strictly enforced. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 107-08 (1988) (observing that granting such motions too freely will permit endless delay of deportation by aliens creative and fertile enough to continuously produce new and material facts sufficient to establish a prima facie case); Wang v. Board of Immigration Appeals, 437 F.3d 270, 274 (2d Cir. 2006) (endorsing a strict limitation on motions to reopen and observing that it would be ironic if aliens like the respondent who have remained in the United States illegally following an order of deportation were allowed to have “a second and third bite at the apple” simply because he managed to marry and have children while evading authorities); Cao v. U.S. Dept. of Justice, 421 F.3d 149, 156 (2d Cir. 2005) (citing Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992)). Thus, with certain limited exceptions, an alien is permitted to file one motion to reopen a final administrative order of deportation, not later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent filed his motion to reopen on November 4, 2013, more than 6 years after the entry of the final administrative order on June 8, 2007. Therefore, the motion was untimely.
 
The respondent was last in removal proceedings in 2007. He was represented by former counsel, Ms. Moidel, and found to be removable as charged and statutorily ineligible for relief under former section 212(c) or section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(c), (h). He reserved appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision, but never ultimately perfected an appeal. The respondent was subsequently removed from the United States. On November 4, 2013, he filed to reopen proceedings, alleging that he had received ineffective assistance of counsel from his former attorney. The government opposed reopening. The Immigration Judge rejected the grounds put forth for reopening and denied the motion.
 
*2 For an alien raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the untimeliness of the motion may be excused under the doctrine of equitable tolling. In addition to complying with the procedural requirements for such a claim, the alien must show prejudice as a result of his counsel’s ineffective assistance and that he exercised due diligence during the period he seeks to toll. See Jian Hua Wang v. BIA, 508 F.3d 710, 715 (2d Cir. 2007); Celtic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
The respondent contends on appeal that the Immigration Judge should have found ineffective assistance of counsel prejudiced him. The respondent asserts that he never met his prior counsel, that she was ineffective for appearing telephonically and for not filing a brief on his eligibility for relief or for insisting on a merits hearing.
 
Upon review, we affirm the result below, denying reopening. We find first no clear error in any of the Immigration Judge’s findings regarding the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). A motion to reopen claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must substantially comply with the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, supra. In Lozada, we held that (1) the motion must be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) the motion must show that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) the motion must reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640.
 
The respondent has not filed a bar complaint against his former attorney. The requirement that an ineffective assistance of counsel claim demonstrate the filing of a bar complaint serves to deter meritless claims and highlights the standards that should be expected of attorneys who represent aliens in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. It also serves to protect against collusion between alien and counsel in which “ineffective assistance” is tolerated, and goes unchallenged by an alien before disciplinary authorities, because it results in a benefit to the alien in that delay can be a desired end, in itself, in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 604 (BIA 1996).
 
*3 Some explanations for not filing a bar complaint may excuse the failure. See e.g. Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding reasonable explanation where employee in attorney’s office who was responsible for recording hearing dates had been suffering from illnesses about which the attorney was unaware, and calendared the hearing date, and the attorney took full responsibility for the error in front of the Immigration Judge the next business day). In this case, the respondent alleges that he lacked the resources to necessary to file a bar complaint. The respondent statement is not, however, adequate to excuse the failure to file a bar complaint, particularly given that the respondent was able to file his motion to reopen despite the same lack of resources in Jamaica. The respondent’s lack of compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, was a proper reason to deny the motion to reopen.
 
More fundamentally, the respondent was not prejudiced by Ms. Moidel’s representation of him because at the time of his June 8, 2007, hearing, he was removable as charged and not eligible for relief. While the case law has changed since his hearing, Ms. Moidel relied on the law at the time and the respondent’s desire to be released from detention. Since the respondent cannot prove prejudice, we dismiss his other due process claims as well. See Matter of Assaad, supra; Matter of D-, 20 I&N Dec. 827, 831 (BIA 1994); Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356-7 (BIA 1983). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 1978624 (BIA)

2015 WL 1978568 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAVIER AUGUSTO ARANA-MEJIA A.K.A. LUIS ELRIQUE GONZALEZ BACENET
File: AXXX XX2 636 - Buffalo, NY
March 24, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose Enrique Perez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated November 4, 2013. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s applications for voluntary departure under sections 240B(a) and (b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229c(a) and (b). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, judgment, and all other issues in appeals are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent does not appeal from the Immigration Judge’s determination that he did not establish his eligibility for voluntary departure (I.J. at 3). See sections 240(c)(4)(A)(i), (ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A)(i), (ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). Therefore, this issue is waived. See, e.g., Matter of Cervantes, 22 I&N Dec. 560, 561 n.1 (BIA 1999) (expressly declining to address an issue not raised by party on appeal); Matter of Gutierrez, 19 I&N Dec. 562, 565 n.3 (BIA 1988) (same).
 
Rather, on appeal, the respondent objects to the admissibility of the Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien, Form I-213. However, the I-213 is a record made by public officials in the ordinary course of their duties and is accorded strong indicia of reliability. See Felzceek v. INS, 75 F.3d 112, 116 (2d Cir. 1996). Moreover, neither before the Immigration Judge nor on appeal has the respondent articulated any basis for his objection to the I-213 other than generally alleging error. On this record, the respondent did not make a prima facie case for suppression. See generally Matter of Barcenas, 19 I&N Dec. 609 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for a further continuance to retain a new attorney and explore his potential eligibility for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture. A party seeking a continuance has the burden to establish good cause for the continuance. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. In this regard, the Immigration Judge acknowledged the respondent’s general statement that he feared returning to Guatemala (I.J. at 1). However, the record reflects that the respondent was represented at prior proceedings and did not request any other relief from removal other than voluntary departure (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 11). The record further indicates that the proceedings were continued for the sole purpose of allowing the respondent to establish his eligibility for voluntary departure (Tr. at 11-12). In this regard, we have held that, absent egregious circumstances, a tactical decision by an attorney (in this case deciding to pursue only voluntary departure) is binding on his client. See Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938, 942 (BIA 1995); Matter of Valasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986). The respondent has not alleged any egregious circumstances; nor do we find any such circumstances on this record. Indeed, the respondent has not alleged that his prior attorney was ineffective or complied with the procedural requirements, as set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Prior to the reconvened hearing on the respondent’s application for voluntary departure, the respondent fired his attorney and did not secure a new attorney prior to his next scheduled hearing. In addition, the respondent did not file any motion for a continuance prior to the hearing as required by the Immigration Court Practice Manual.
 
*2 Considering it was the respondent’s choice to fire his prior attorney and the absence of any viable legal theory of relief from removal, the respondent did not show good cause for an additional continuance. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 422, 424 (BIA 1987) (Immigration Judge’s denial of a motion for a continuance will not be overturned on appeal unless the respondent demonstrates that the denial deprived her of a full and fair hearing). Indeed, the viability of the respondent’s proposed asylum application is doubtful considering that the respondent appears time-barred pursuant to section 208(a)(2)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1158(a)(2)(B) from pursuing relief under that section. In addition, the respondent has not proffered any testimony or theory of relief for withholding of removal under the Act, or for protection under the Convention Against Torture that might have been presented had he retained new counsel. Nor has respondent submitted an I-589 or any other application, even now on appeal, to establish prima facie eligibility for any relief from removal. On this record, the respondent has not presented sufficient evidence to indicate that if proceedings were continued and had the respondent retained a new attorney that the ultimate result in the disposition of the case would be different. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992) (explaining that a party who seeks a remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case).
 
In sum, we find nothing in the Immigration Judge’s order or in the manner in which the proceedings were conducted to indicate that the respondent’s due process rights were violated or that he was denied a full and fair hearing. See Matter of D-, 20 I&N Dec. 827, 831 (BIA 1994); Matter of Tomas, 19 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1987).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 1978568 (BIA)

2015 WL 1978546 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALFREDO PIMENTEL-ARAGON
File: AXXX XX2 906 - Atlanta, GA
March 17, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elliott Ozment, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Wylly Jordan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Remand; voluntary departure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 6, 2013, finding him removable from the United States, and granting his application for voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(1). The appeal will be dismissed in part and sustained in part.
 
The Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
In his appellate brief, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge incorrectly denied his motion to withdraw his concession of removability. In addition, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge set an unreasonably high voluntary departure bond.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has not established egregious circumstances to withdraw his former attorney’s concession of removability. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (holding that absent egregious circumstances, a party is bound by his/her attorney’s conduct, including admissions). The respondent argues that his current attorney has discovered that the government has engaged in egregious conduct by requiring his detention in Alabama for an unreasonable period, and thus evidence could be suppressed impacting his removability (Resp. Brief at 9, 15; Motion to Withdraw Plea). However, the respondent’s allegation is insufficient to withdraw his concession of removability.
 
In addition, the respondent argues that his former counsel’s written concession of removability, filed on March 8, 2012, in connection with a motion to change venue, constituted ineffective assistance of counsel (Resp. Brief at 11-18). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel). See also Ali v. U.S. Atty. Gen, 643 F.3d 1324 (11th Cir. 2011) (requiring that an alien establish prejudice from an attorney’s concession of removability). The respondent has not satisfied any of the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel. Moreover, the respondent has not submitted any evidence establishing that he may not be removable from the United States, and thus has not shown that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s concession. In fact, on May 4, 2011 the respondent admitted in court the factual bases for the finding of inadmissibility (Tr. at 8). In light of the foregoing, the respondent has not established an egregious circumstance to withdraw his concession of removability.
 
*2 However, we agree with the respondent’s appellate argument that the Immigration Judge required that he post an unduly high voluntary departure bond (I.J. at 2-3). Section 240B(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(3), states that an alien permitted to depart voluntarily under that subsection shall be required to post a voluntary departure bond “in an amount necessary to ensure that the alien will depart.” The Immigration Judge determined that a $20,000 bond was necessary to ensure compliance with the voluntary departure grant (I.J. at 2-3). Based on the totality of the evidence, we find that a bond of $1,000 is sufficient to ensure the respondent’s departure within the time period specified. Accordingly, we will vacate the Immigration Judge’s grant of voluntary departure and alternatively grant the respondent a 60-day period of voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(b) of the Act upon the payment of a bond in the amount of $1,000.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and sustained in part.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s grant of voluntary departure is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Conditioned upon compliance with the provisions of the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent must post a voluntary departure bond in the amount of $1,000 with the DHS within 5 business days of the date of this order. If the bond is not posted within 5 business days, the order of voluntary departure is automatically vacated on the following business day, and the respondent is ordered removed as provided by the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
*3 WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 1978546 (BIA)
