** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IN SIL KIM-CHOI A.K.A. IN SIL CHOI
File: AXXX XX7 821 - Los Angeles, CA
March 15, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Claire H. Kim, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of South Korea, was ordered removed by an Immigration Judge on May 8, 2012. At that time, the respondent agreed to pre-conclusion voluntary departure. On August 29, 2014, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, and now appeals the February 9, 2015, decision denying the motion. The respondent seeks reopening so that the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) can exercise its prosecutorial discretion not to pursue her removal; or in the alternative, for these proceedings to be administratively closed. The DHS has submitted nothing in favor of the respondent’s motion or appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed for the reasons stated therein. The respondent does not dispute the Immigration Judge’s determination that the motion is untimely, and that an exception to 90-day time limit for filing such a motion has not been demonstrated. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). Although the respondent alludes to prior ineffective assistance of counsel, she has not attempted to set forth a claim to reopen proceedings on that basis. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of reopening proceedings sua sponte. Although the respondent asserts that she cooperated with authorities to aid a prosecution, and apparently received a temporary stay of removal from the DHS, she did not depart voluntarily as agreed, and--in any event--has no further basis to remain in the United States. The respondent notes that the DHS has not filed an opposition to her motion, but we note that there is nothing to indicate that the DHS has consented to reopening for any purpose, including to exercise its prosecutorial discretion in these concluded proceedings. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). As the respondent has no prospective relief potentially available, there was no basis for the Immigration Judge to reopen and administratively close proceedings. See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012).
 
In light of the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 10582167 (BIA)

2015 WL 2090722 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JASON CHUNG YU
File: AXXX XX5 545 - Los Angeles, CA
March 12, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elif Keles, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 This case is before us on certification by the Immigration Judge following a remand for forwarding of a reconstructed record of proceedings to the Board. The respondent, a native and citizen of Taiwan, filed a timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 14, 2013, which denied his request for a continuance and pretermitted his application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1225(i).1 The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a brief or other response. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The factual findings of the Immigration Judge are reviewed to determine whether they are “clearly erroneous.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). All other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges, including legal and discretionary determinations and applications of law to fact, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent failed to establish good cause for a continuance (I.J. at 7-8). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 355 (BIA 1983); see also Ahmed v. Holder, 569 F.3d 1009 (9th Cir. 2009). On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred by denying him a continuance to pursue post-conviction relief of his 1999 controlled substance offense (Resp. Br. at 4-5). The mere pendency of a collateral attack on a conviction does not mean that the conviction is not final for immigration purposes (I.J. at 8). See Matter of Cardenas Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996) (stating that collateral attacks upon a respondent’s conviction do not operate to negate the finality of the conviction unless and until the conviction is overturned); Matter of Ozkok, 19 I&N Dec. 546, 551-53 (BIA 1988). The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent was warned of the immigration consequences of a guilty plea (I.J. at 8; Exh. 2, at 5). While the respondent asserts on appeal that he was not given any immigration advisals regarding the deferred entry of judgment, he has not shown that he is likely to succeed in having the deferral vacated (I.J. at 8; Respondent’s Appeal Brief; Respondent’s Notice of Appeal). Moreover, to date he has not shown that the deferral has been vacated.
 
*2 Furthermore, the respondent has failed to show prejudice (Respondent’s Appeal Brief; Respondent’s Notice of Appeal). To date, the respondent has not shown that his deferral has been vacated.
 
The respondent also argues that he was denied counsel (Resp. Br. at 6-7). We are not persuaded by his arguments in this regard. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent was given approximately a year with his first attorney to prepare his case and present legal arguments, which was a sufficient amount of time (I.J. at 8).2 Furthermore, when he appeared with his second attorney, whom he hired at the eleventh hour merely to request a continuance, she withdrew as counsel due to lack of cooperation by the respondent (Resp. Br. at 6-7; I.J. at 8; Tr. at 41-42; Motion to Withdraw as Attorney of Record).
 
The denial of a continuance will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing, and here the respondent has not established such (Respondent’s Notice of Appeal; Respondent’s Appeal Brief). See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusions denying the respondent’s request for a continuance. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has not contested the Immigration Judge’s determination that he was convicted of an offense relating to a controlled substance which renders him permanently ineligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) (I.J. at 2-7).

2

The respondent states that prior counsel has been disciplined by the Massachusetts bar for poor representation, but he has not established the Mr. Byrd provided inadequate representation in the respondent’s immigration proceedings, and we see no evidence of per se ineffective assistance of counsel (Resp. Br. at 6).). See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (dispensing with the Lozada requirements where ineffective assistance of counsel was obvious and undisputed on the face of the record).

2015 WL 2090737 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ENZO OLIVOS CAMPOS
File: AXXX XX7 885 - Baltimore, MD
March 11, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Faize Rasul, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Billy Sapp
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before us on November 13, 2014, when we reopened the respondent’s removal proceedings for the limited purpose of reinstating the 60-day period of voluntary departure. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen to allow him to pursue a U visa as well as prosecutorial discretion under the newly announced deferred action plan. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The agency lacks the authority to grant or review both forms of relief sought by the respondent. The United States Citizenship and Immigration Service (USCIS) has exclusive jurisdiction over U visa petitions. See Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 811-12 (BIA 2012); see also 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c). An exercise of prosecutorial discretion is granted exclusively by the DHS. Accordingly, reopening of these proceedings is not warranted for the respondent to pursue either form of relief. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2009) (holding that the Board generally lacks the authority to reopen a proceeding to allow an alien the opportunity to seek relief from removal where the agency lacks jurisdiction over the form of relief sought).1
 
We are also unpersuaded by the respondent’s claim that reopening is warranted on account of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has not complied with the procedural safeguards set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) or otherwise provided sufficient evidence or explanation to establish a valid ineffective assistance claim an explanation of his agreement with prior counsel. See Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741, 746 (4th Cir. 2006) (explaining that, while strict compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, is not required in every case, the procedural safeguards set forth therein “provide[] a useful framework for assessing ineffective assistance claims”). Furthermore, the respondent has not established that his proceedings were so fundamentally unfair that he was prevented from establishing his eligibility for a U visa, as he still may pursue that form of relief before USCIS. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638.
 
Finally, we decline to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen these proceedings as the respondent has not established an exceptional circumstance warranting reopening. 8 C.F.R. 1003.2(a); Matter of Yauri, supra; see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

While the pendency of an application for a U visa has no effect on the government’s authority to execute a removal order, an applicant for a U visa is permitted to file a request for a stay of removal with the DHS. 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1)(ii). If USCIS approves the respondent’s application, the regulation allows him to file a motion to reopen and terminate proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. §214.14(c)(5)(i).

2015 WL 1605481 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MANUEL PEREZ-RUBIO
File: AXXX XX1 711 - Denver, CO
March 4, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Veronica Reyes, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Geoffrey D. Rieman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 4, 2013. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s December 10, 2012, motion to reopen proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on October 23, 2012, pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5). See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and 240(e)(1) of the Act (defining “exceptional circumstances”). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent has not specifically contested any of the Immigration Judge’s legal or factual findings (DHS’s Br. at 1). The respondent’s Notice of Appeal (“NOA”) merely states that he “believes that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen.” See NOA at 2. The DHS also correctly notes that the respondent’s brief “fails to specify [any] alleged error in the Immigration Judge’s decision.” See DHS’s Br. at 1 (stating that the respondent “simply argues that he was prejudiced by [his former counsel’s] ineffective assistance” but that he has failed to comply with the procedural requirements to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988)). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Tang v. Ashcroft, 354 F.3d 1192, 1196 (10th Cir. 2003) (holding that in order to assert an ineffective assistance claim, an alien must comply with the Boards Lozada requirements).
 
In sum, the respondent provides no substantive discussion of whether the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen. See Respondent’s Br. at 1-3. Without a meaningful challenge to any of the Immigration Judge’s findings, we see no basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s order. See Matter of Cervantes-Gonzalez, 22 I&N Dec. 560, 561 n.1 (BIA 1999) (declining to address an issue not raised by a party on appeal); see also Becker v. Kroll, 494 F.3d 904, 913 n.6 (10th Cir. 2007) (holding that an issue “““insufficiently raised in the opening brief is deemed waived”).
 
*2 Based on the foregoing, we will affirm the Immigration Judge’s January 4, 2013, decision for the reasons stated therein. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EBER SALGADO-GUTIERREZ A.K.A. RODOLFO GONZALEZ-LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX4 421 - Chicago, IL
February 27, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria Baldini-Potermin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patrick M. McKenna
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)] - Controlled substance violation
 
Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated June 9, 2014, ordering him removed from the United States to Mexico.1 The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand the proceedings to the Immigration Court.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including any determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that his prior counsel provided him with ineffective assistance by not seeking asylum, withholding of removal, protection under the Convention Against Torture (“CAT”), or voluntary departure on his behalf. The respondent’s motion is accompanied by an affidavit setting forth his agreement with counsel and asserting that he had asked his former attorney of the possibility of seeking asylum or voluntary departure; an affidavit from the respondent’s girlfriend detailing her interactions with the respondent’s former counsel; copies of payment receipts from the respondent’s former counsel; a completed asylum application; and a copy of a January 16, 2015, letter to the respondent’s former counsel informing him of the allegations of ineffective assistance. The respondent also indicated that he will file a bar complaint no later than February 3, 2015, in order to first give former counsel an opportunity to respond to the allegations of ineffective assistance, and he will supplement the record with a copy of the bar complaint and with his former counsel’s response, if any. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
The Board has limited ability to engage in fact-finding in the course of deciding appeals. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). The determination whether the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel, and, if so, whether he was prejudiced, involves findings of fact. Therefore, we will remand the record to the Immigration Judge to address the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim and to decide whether further proceedings are warranted to allow the respondent an opportunity to apply for asylum or other forms of relief from removal. By remanding the record, we are not suggesting a desired outcome.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

The issue regarding the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request for a continuance has been waived on appeal. See, e.g., Capric v. Ashcroft, 355 F.3d 1075, 1089 (7th Cir. 2004) (finding alien did not preserve for appeal due process challenge to admission of evidence where notice of appeal stated hearing did not comport with requirements of due process but appeal brief made no allegations regarding admission of such evidence).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IHAB GAMAL EL DIN EL ZOHAIRY
File: AXXX XX6 561 - Las Vegas, NV
February 25, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John D. Friedman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
An Mai Nguyen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; continuance; voluntary departure
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 27, 2013, pretermitting his application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), and denying his motion to continue to file a new I-130 visa petition. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for a continuance and pretermitted his application for adjustment of status because he did not have a visa currently available (I.J. at 2-3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that a continuance was not warranted. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6 (placing the decision to grant or deny a continuance within the discretion of the Immigration Judge if good cause is shown); see also Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987). As the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent did not establish good cause for a continuance to apply for this relief (I.J. at 2-3).
 
While this Board has announced numerous factors that should be considered when considering whether removal proceedings should be continued to await a final decision regarding the merits of a pending immigrant visa petition, “the focus of the inquiry is the apparent ultimate likelihood of success on the adjustment application.” Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785, 790 (BIA 2009). At the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent did not have a pending visa petition, and the Department of Homeland Security opposed the motion (I.J. at 2). The respondent already had two unsuccessful visa petitions from the same spouse petitioner (I.J. at 2). We conclude that, in light of the highly speculative nature of the outcome of a third visa petition from the same spouse, the respondent had not sufficiently demonstrated an apparently ultimate likelihood of success on the adjustment application to warrant a continuance. See Matter of Hashmi, supra, at 790.
 
*2 The respondent’s arguments on appeal are not persuasive, and do not address the complete lack of any basis to grant a continuance to apply for adjustment of status. See Respondent’s Brief. The respondent’s main argument is that the Immigration Judge should not have denied his motion to continue because he received ineffective assistance from the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge. The respondent alleges that his prior counsel failed to adequately prepare for the hearing and show good cause for a continuance. See Respondent’s Brief at *unnumbered* p. 2-3. However, the respondent has yet to demonstrate compliance with the criteria we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to establish a claim for ineffective assistance of prior counsel. Specifically, the respondent has submitted no proof that former counsel has been made aware of the allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel before the Board and that he has filed a complaint with the state bar. The respondent’s photocopies of letters to former counsel and the state bar are not proof of mailing or filing.
 
Further, even if the respondent had complied procedurally with Lozada, he has not established that former counsel’s action or inaction caused him prejudice in these proceedings. See Matter of Assad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2002) (alien did not establish that this former counsel’s failure to file a timely appeal constituted sufficient prejudice to warrant consideration of his late appeal on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640 (successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim requires a showing that the respondent was prejudiced by counsel’s deficient performance). Specifically, since the Immigration Judge’s decision nearly two years ago and during the pendency of this appeal, the respondent has not submitted an application for relief, or shown that he has an approved or pending visa petition.1 As the respondent has not presently established prima facie eligibility for any relief, the alleged ineffective assistance has not been shown to have potentially affected the outcome of the respondent’s case. Thus, the respondent has not established a basis to remand his case on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Consequently, the respondent has further failed to show prejudice that resulted from the continuance denial. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). Thus, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance and pretermission of the respondent’s application for adjustment of status.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
*3 FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*4 Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent attached a Form I-130 visa petition to his brief; however, he submitted no proof that this application has been submitted to United States Citizenship and Immigration Services.

2015 WL 1208094 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BALKAR SINGH
File: AXXX XX7 641 - Buffalo, New York
February 11, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elizabeth J. Dobosiewicz, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michele Henriques
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of India, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s October 3, 2014, written decision which denied his motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has requested summary affirmance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgment and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of her own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). In determining whether exceptional circumstances exist to excuse an alien’s failure to appear, the “totality of circumstances” pertaining to the alien’s case must be considered. Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, 509 (BIA 1996).
 
The respondent received personal service of the Notice to Appear on September 14, 2011, which bears his signature (Exh. 1). On December 8, 2011, the Immigration Judge informed him of the consequences for failure to appear through an interpreter, and he indicated that he understood the consequences (I.J. at 5). On October 26, 2012, the respondent, through counsel, requested voluntary departure and a hearing on voluntary departure was scheduled for May 5, 2014 (I.J. at 2). The respondent was present at the hearing and indicated through an interpreter that he understood that his next hearing was scheduled for May 5, 2014 (Id.).
 
*2 Following a hearing conducted in absentia on May 5, 2014, at which the respondent failed to appear and the DHS presented evidence, an Immigration Judge found him subject to removal as charged, and ordered him removed from the United States. See generally section 240 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a. His counsel appeared and was unable to explain the respondent’s absence (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge granted her request to withdraw as counsel (I.J. at 2-3).
 
On September 12, 2014, the respondent filed a pro se motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. He alleged without elaboration that he did not appear on May 5, 2014, because his attorney allegedly informed him that his hearing was on May 6, 2014. The respondent did not specify when his attorney told him the incorrect hearing date. He claimed that his attorney’s allegedly informing him of the incorrect hearing date, without more, constituted ineffective assistance of counsel amounting to exceptional circumstances, which should excuse his failure to appear. The respondent conceded that he indicated at the October 26, 2012, hearing that he understood that his next hearing date was on May 5, 2014. Furthermore, he alleged without elaboration that he was eligible for asylum although he did not attach an asylum application. Finally, the respondent attached a copy of an Immigration Practitioner Complaint Form (Form EOIR-44) dated September 12, 2014, which he claimed to have filed with the Executive Office for Immigration Review (EOIR). The DHS filed a response in opposition to the motion to reopen.
 
On October 3, 2014, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to rescind the in absentia removal order because the respondent had been personally informed on October 26, 2012, that his next hearing was on May 5, 2014, he indicated that he understood the next hearing date, and he had not established exceptional circumstances to excuse his failure to appear (I.J. at 5). In addition, the Immigration Judge held that the respondent had not established that he merited reopening for the purpose of applying for asylum inasmuch as the record indicated that he had entered the United States prior to 2000, and he had not alleged or established the existence of any changed country conditions to excuse his applying for asylum more than 1 year after his entry date (Exh. 2) (I.J. at 5-6). Finally, he had not attached an asylum application to his motion (I.J. at 6).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision that the respondent did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel to excuse his failure to appear. He has not established that his absence at the scheduled hearing was the result of lack of notice because, in addition to the fact that the attorney received notice, he conceded that he understood that his hearing was scheduled for May 5, 2014, when he personally appeared for the hearing on October 26, 2012. See also Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996) (alien seeking to reopen in absentia proceedings based upon her unsuccessful communications with attorney about hearing date did not establish exceptional circumstances where she failed to satisfy all of requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
*3 To the extent that the respondent has claimed that his attorney purportedly told him an incorrect hearing date, this allegation, without more, does not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel, especially in light of the respondent’s concession that he knew his hearing was scheduled for May 5, 2014. In addition, he has not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) to establish ineffective assistance of counsel because he has not provided any proof that he informed his prior attorney about her alleged prior misconduct, and he has not filed a state bar complaint. His claim that he is unable to comply with Matter of Lozada, supra, because he is detained, without additional explanation or elaboration, is unavailing.1
 
Finally, the respondent alleges for the first time on appeal that he is eligible for reopening because he purportedly did not receive oral warnings of the consequences for failure to appear at the October 26, 2012, hearing. However, he did not raise this allegation of error in his motion, and the Board does not review issues raised for the first time on appeal. See, e.g., Matter of Jimenez, 21 I&N Dec. 567, 570 n.2 (BIA 1996) (issues not raised before Immigration Judge are not properly before Board on appeal); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629, 638 (BIA 2003). In any event, his reliance on Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998), and section 242B of the Act, in support of this argument is misplaced in part because he is removal proceedings and not deportation proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

As part of a Motion To Stay Removal filed on February 6, 2015, the respondent claims he has filed a state bar complaint. However, the Board is an appellate body and we generally do not consider evidence filed on appeal. Also, the respondent has not shown that this complaint could not have been submitted earlier in the proceedings. Thus, we do not consider the additional evidence. In any event, the attorney apparently has not had the opportunity to respond given the late submission. The Motion To Stay Removal is moot given our disposition on the merits.

2015 WL 1208207 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ADRIAN DE ALBA CASTREJON
File: AXXX XX9 607 - Seattle, WA
February 11, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stephen Robbins, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated August 29, 2014, denying his motion to reopen. While we accept the respondent’s appeal brief, we will dismiss his appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by any alleged deficiencies in his prior attorney’s handling of this case. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988) (holding that, in order to warrant reopening on account of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance); see also Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999) (holding that prejudice results when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings).
 
Here, the respondent contends that his former counsel did not advise him of the ability to pursue an Application for Asylum and for Withholding of Removal (Form I-589). However, as recognized by the Immigration Judge, the Form I-589 which the respondent offered in support of his motion to reopen provided no substantive information about his claim (I.J. at 2). While the respondent did check the box on page 5 of his proposed Form I-589, to indicate that he is seeking asylum and withholding of removal on account of his membership in a particular social group, he has not provided the exact delineation of any particular social group to which he claims to belong. See Matter of A-T-, 25 I&N Dec. 4 (BIA 2009). On appeal, the respondent has referred to being handicapped as a result of a workplace injury (Respondent’s Br. at 3). However, he not meaningfully argued that such status amount to membership in a particular social group cognizable under the Act. See Matter of M-E-V-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 227 (BIA 2014). He has also not offered any evidence that handicapped individuals are persecuted in Mexico. While the respondent does refer to a cousin that was killed in Mexico, he does not claim that his cousin was also handicapped or otherwise similarly situated. A generalized fear of crime and violence in Mexico does not amount to a basis to grant the respondent asylum or withholding of removal as he must demonstrate that a protected ground is a “central reason” for the claimed harm. See Matter of C-T-L-, 25 I&N Dec. 341, 347 (BIA 2010); Matter of J-B-N- & S-M-, 24 I&N Dec. 208 (BIA 2007).
 
*2 The respondent has also not meaningfully articulated a basis upon which he would warrant protection under the Convention Against Torture. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16(c)(2), 1208.18(a); Matter of J-F-F-, 23 I&N Dec. 912 (A.G. 2006).
 
For the reasons set forth above, we conclude that the respondent has not demonstrated the requisite prejudice needed to reopen these proceedings on account of ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 1208207 (BIA)

2015 WL 1208104 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ESTELA IBONNE ARREDONDO
File: AXXX XX0 314 - Dallas, TX
February 9, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Omar Salas, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled (withdrawn)
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)]-Crime involving moral turpitude (withdrawn)
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law (conceded)
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; stay
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration December 20, 2012, decision denying her timely motion to reopen.1 The respondent’s motion was filed on December 6, 2012, after the Immigration Judge granted her pre-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), in a decision dated September 7, 2012. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b) (generally setting a 90-day deadline for filing motions to reopen). The respondent then filed this timely appeal. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). Her appeal will be sustained, proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The following facts and procedural history are not in dispute. The respondent entered the United States on March 26, 1985, as a non-immigrant visitor but remained longer than authorized (Exhs. 1A, 4:1). On June 14, 2001, she pled guilty to and was convicted of injury to a child, a third degree felony (Exh. 3). She is the beneficiary of an approved Alien Relative Petition (Form I-130) filed on her behalf by her United States citizen son and approved by United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) on June 1, 2011 (Exh. 4:3). In light of the respondent’s conviction and her approved visa petition as an immediate relative, she sought adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), in conjunction with a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), before the Immigration Judge (Exh. 4).2
 
*2 At her final hearing, scheduled before the Immigration Judge on September 7, 2012, the respondent was advised that her adjustment application would be denied (I.J. at 1).3 Thereafter, she consulted with her then-counsel off the record before withdrawing her applications for adjustment and a 212(h) waiver and accepting pre-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Act (I.J. at 1).
 
On December 6, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen through new counsel in which she maintains that her previous attorney provided ineffective assistance (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 3-7; Tabs E, I-J). Specifically, she maintains that her prior attorney performed deficiently because he did not advise her that she needed to obtain an updated medical examination prior to her final hearing (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 6; Tab E). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.102(o), (q), (r) (requiring a practitioner to provide competent representation, to act with reasonable promptness and diligence in representing the respondent before the Immigration Court, and to maintain appropriate communication with the respondent during the course of such representation). In addition, the respondent maintains that when issues regarding the medical examination were raised at her final hearing, her former attorney did not advise her about the problem but instead told her that the Immigration Judge was denying her applications because of her criminal conviction (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 3, 6; Tab E). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.102(r)(1)-(2) (requiring counsel to promptly inform the respondent about any decision or circumstance requiring the respondent’s informed consent, consult with the respondent regarding such consent, and consult with the respondent about the means by which the respondent’s objectives may be accomplished). The respondent also maintains that her attorney performed deficiently because he did not request a continuance in allowing her to remedy the alleged defect in her application (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 6; Tab E). Instead, she asserts that her attorney misled her when advising her about her options by telling her the Immigration Judge could immediately arrest her if she did not agree to withdraw her applications for relief and accept voluntary departure and indicating to her that she did not have other options (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 3, 7; Tab E). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.102(p) (requiring the practitioner to abide by the respondent’s decisions concerning the respondent’s objectives after reasonably consulting with the respondent in advising her of such options); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.102(r)(2).
 
*3 On December 20, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding the record does not contain sufficient evidence establishing that the respondent’s former attorney performed deficiently when counseling the respondent with respect to her options in these proceedings (I.J. at 1). Specifically, he concluded that the respondent’s decision to accept voluntary departure was knowing and voluntary because (1) her counsel confirmed on the record that he spoke with the respondent regarding her options, (2) the Immigration Judge confirmed this in questioning the respondent on the record, and (3) the respondent signed the Immigration Judge’s order granting pre-conclusion voluntary departure and advising the respondent of her rights and responsibilities thereunder (I.J. at 1). In addition, the Immigration Judge concluded that, notwithstanding any deficiencies in prior counsel’s performance, the respondent did not establish that she would have prevailed on her adjustment and waiver applications if she had a hearing on the merits (I.J. at 1-2).
 
At the outset, we note that the Immigration Judge did not conclude that the respondent’s motion to reopen does not comply with the procedural requirements for motions based on ineffective assistance as outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (Resp. Motion to Reopen at Tabs E, I-J). See id. at 639 (explaining that generally a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance should generally be supported by (1) an affidavit from the aggrieved party, (2) evidence that the former attorney has been advised of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond, along with any response offered, and (3) evidence that appropriate disciplinary authorities have been notified or, if not, the reason such notification is not being pursued); see also Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647 (5th Cir. 2014) (confirming the applicability of these procedural requirements within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, wherein this case arises).
 
Although the Immigration Judge concluded that the record contains insufficient evidence of misconduct by the respondent’s former attorney, in part, because the Immigration Judge spoke to the respondent on the record and confirmed her willingness to withdraw her applications for relief and accept voluntary departure, we note that the Immigration Judge’s decision does not consider all of the allegations of misconduct made by the respondent (I.J. at 1; Resp. Motion to Reopen at 3, 6, Tabs E, I-J; Resp. Brief at 3-10). Specifically, the respondent maintains that her attorney told her that (1) she did not have any other options, including an opportunity to appeal; and (2) the Immigration Judge could immediately arrest her if she declined to accept voluntary departure, such that she did not knowingly and voluntarily accept this grant of relief (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 3, 6, Tabs E, I-J; Resp. Brief at 3-4). While we agree with the Immigration Judge that we will generally bind a respondent to the tactical decisions she makes with her counsel, she is not bound by these decisions to the extent she makes a showing of egregious circumstances (I.J. at 1). See Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938, 942 (BIA 1995); Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986); see also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 166-67 (5th Cir. 2006).
 
*4 Here, we conclude that such egregious circumstances have been demonstrated where the respondent claims in her unrebutted affidavit that her attorney (1) did not tell her an updated medical was required, (2) did not advise her at her hearing that her adjustment application was incomplete, and thus subject to denial, based on the date of the medical examination, (3) did not request a continuance to obtain an updated medical examination, (4) did not advise the respondent that she could appeal any adverse decision issued by the Immigration Judge, and (5) advised the respondent that her only option was to accept voluntary departure because her failure to do so would result in her arrest by the Immigration Judge (Resp. Motion to Reopen at 3, 6, Tabs E, I-J; Resp. Brief at 3-10).
 
Moreover, we agree with the respondent that her proceedings were rendered fundamentally unfair by her attorney’s conduct, as his failure to advise her of the need for an updated medical examination and failure to request a continuance to remedy the defect led directly to a determination that she could not prevail on the adjustment application at her final hearing before the Immigration Judge (Resp. Brief at 9-10; I.J. at 1). See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998) (requiring ineffective assistant to be so egregious as to render the hearing unfair); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640; see also Ogbemudia v. INS, 988 F.2d 595, 598 (5th Cir. 1993) (requiring an alien to demonstrate “substantial prejudice” in order to establish that a hearing is fundamentally unfair). While we express no opinion as to the respondent’s ultimate statutory and discretionary eligibility for adjustment of status with a waiver of inadmissibility, we conclude that the record does not contain sufficient evidence to establish that the respondent knowingly and voluntarily withdrew her applications for relief and we have identified no apparent, statutory impediments to her ability to proceed with these previously filed applications. See Anwar v. INS, 116 F.3d 140, 144 (5th Cir. 1997); Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994) (requiring an alien to establish prima facie eligibility for relief from removal to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
Accordingly, in light of the respondent’s timely motion, supported by her unrebutted affidavit asserting that her prior attorney performed deficiently and deceptively in securing her consent to withdraw her applications for relief and to accept pre-conclusion voluntary departure, we conclude that reopening of this matter is appropriate. Therefore, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be granted and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge to allow the respondent to pursue her previously withdrawn applications.
 
*5 In light of our disposition of this matter, we decline to address the respondent’s motion for a stay as moot. Upon remand, the parties will have an opportunity to supplement the record with any documentation or testimony they may choose to present regarding the respondent’s statutory and discretionary eligibility for adjustment of status sought in conjunction with her waiver of inadmissibility.
 
Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained and her motion to reopen, granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion for a stay is denied as moot.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion, including, but not limited to, proceedings allowing the respondent to pursue her applications for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act and a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

Because the Immigration Judge served his decision on the parties by mail on December 28, 2012, the respondent’s January 25, 2013, Notice of Appeal is timely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(c) (setting a 30-day deadline for filing a Notice of Appeal beginning from the date that the Immigration Judge’s decision is served by mail).

2

Some of the facts recited in this order were not formally found by the Immigration Judge but may be administratively noticed based on the record evidence. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (limiting our appellate fact-finding authority to “taking administrative notice of commonly known facts such as the contents of official documents”); Matter of S-H-, supra, at 465-66.

3

Specifically, the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) moved to pretermit the respondent’s adjustment application due to a purportedly out-of-date medical examination, at which point the respondent’s former counsel agreed to discuss pre-conclusion voluntary departure with the respondent (I.J. at 1). We need not ultimately address whether the medical exam was expired, thus forming a valid basis for pretermitting the respondent’s adjustment application. However, we note that 8 C.F.R. §§ 245.5 and 1245.5 indicate that the exam must be completed no more than a year prior to the filing of the adjustment application, and the relevant statute and regulations do not further define whether the medical expires during the pendency of an adjustment application if the application remains under review for a period of more than a year from the date that the examination was completed. However, at the time that the Immigration Judge found the respondent statutorily ineligible to adjust her status due to the expiration of her medical examination USCIS maintained a policy in which the agency considered a medical examination to remain unexpired during the pendency of such application in the absence of any noted Class A or B medical condition. See, e.g., Policy Memorandum, Extension of Validity of Medical Certifications on Form I-693 (December 29, 2011) (valid until January 1, 2013).

2015 WL 1208077 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KHURRAM KHAN
File: AXXX XX5 814 - Houston, TX
February 2, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Courtney A. Chua, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patrick E. McClintock
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The motion to reopen is untimely. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on February 26, 2003, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. With certain exceptions, a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent filed his motion to reopen and request for a stay of removal on November 14, 2014, over 11 years after the Board’s final order. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The motion and stay will be denied.
 
To the extent that the respondent is continuing to raise a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not complied with any of the Lozada1 procedural requirements. See Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87, 94-95 (BIA 2007) (finding that the alien satisfied the concerns underlying the Lozada requirements by complying with the first two steps). Further, the respondent has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s actions or inactions. See Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605, 609 (5th Cir. 2006) (rejecting claim of ineffective assistance of counsel based on failure to pursue a discretionary waiver of relief from removal); Assaad v. Ashcroft, 378 F.3d 471, 476 (5th Cir. 2004) (“because Assaad’s attorney’s allegedly deficient performance merely restricted his chances of receiving a discretionary waiver of his removability, we conclude that he has not alleged a violation of his due process rights”); see also Altamiro-Lopez v. Gonzales, 435 F.3d 547, 550 (5th Cir. 2006) (the denial for discretionary relief does not amount to a deprivation of a liberty interest). Here, the respondent has not sufficiently established prima facie eligibility for relief. Thus, the respondent has not shown due diligence or prejudice to warrant equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel
 
To the extent that the respondent is also seeking to apply for asylum or withholding of removal based on changed circumstances or conditions arising in Pakistan, he has not submitted any evidence in support of his claim nor has he articulated a basis for relief. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). Moreover, the respondent has not submitted a new asylum application along with his motion as required by regulation. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). As such, we are not persuaded that the respondent’s alleged fear of harm in Pakistan is adequately supported. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104 (1988) (a motion to reopen may be denied if alien does not establish prima facie case for the relief sought); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (to prevail on a motion to reopen an alien must establish that “the new evidence offered would likely change the result in the case”).
 
*2 Finally, the respondent’s motion does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretionary authority to reopen his proceedings sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “the power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). While the respondent alleges that he will seek adjustment of status based on a yet to be filed immediate relative visa petition, he is an arriving alien and thus, USCIS has sole jurisdiction over any future application for adjustment of status. Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 107 (BIA 2009). Accordingly, the motion and stay will be denied.2
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Matter of Lozada, 19 I & N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), requires a successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim be accompanied by: “(1) an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement with former counsel concerning what action would be taken and what counsel did or did not represent in this regard; (2) proof that the alien notified former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance and allowed counsel an opportunity to respond; and (3) if a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities is claimed, a statement as to whether the alien filed a complaint with any disciplinary authority regarding counsel’s conduct and, if a complaint was not filed, an explanation for not doing so.”).

2

Any request for prosecutorial discretion must be addressed to the DHS. The Board does not have the authority to grant relief from removal solely on equitable or humanitarian grounds. See Matter of Silva-Rodriguez, 20 I&N Dec. 448 (BIA 1992); Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734, 742 (BIA 1988).

2015 WL 1208087 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE WILMER MEMBRENO-DELAO A.K.A WILBER DELGO MEMBRENO A.K.A. JOSE MEMBRENO A.K.A. JOSE WUILMER MEMBRENO
File: AXXX XX3 797 - Los Angeles, CA
January 29, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lorena Marquez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before us on October 31, 2014, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision, affirming the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent had not demonstrated good cause for a continuance to pursue prosecutorial discretion from the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and that the respondent did not warrant preconclusion voluntary departure as a matter of discretion. The DHS has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent requests reconsideration of our prior decision because he argues that the decision is based on the mistaken conclusion that he was convicted of two counts of driving under the influence of alcohol, one in 2010 and another in 2011, when, in fact, he was only charged with driving under the influence in 2010, but never convicted (Resp. Mot. at 2-3; Exhs. A-B). The respondent argues that his prior counsel was ineffective in that he did not inform the court that the respondent was only convicted one time (Resp. Mot. at 2). In support of this argument, the respondent has submitted evidence for the first time which demonstrates that his 2010 charge for driving under the influence was dismissed (Resp. Exh. A at 5). Accordingly, the motion is also in the nature of a motion to reopen.
 
We see no reason to reconsider our prior decision or reopen proceedings in light of the new evidence presented. First, the respondent has not established that the evidence, which was available at the time of the previous hearing, was not submitted as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel. He has neither substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), nor is ineffective assistance plain from the administrative record. See Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999) (explaining that “[t]o establish ineffective assistance of counsel in a motion to reopen, an alien must” meet Lozada’s criteria); see Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 596-99 (9th Cir. 2004) (explaining that strict compliance with Matter of Lozada may not be required where ineffective assistance of counsel is plain from the administrative record).
 
Even if the respondent was to establish that his counsel was neglectful in failing to file evidence of his prior convictions, we do not conclude that “the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999). While it does appear that the respondent was in fact not convicted of driving under the influence in 2010, the evidence indicates that he was arrested for such an offense, made to complete alcohol awareness classes, and that his alcohol use was limited as a condition of his probation (Exh. A). That arrest, and subsequent probation, are still relevant to the discretionary determination on his application for relief, even if it did not result in a conviction. See e.g. Matter of Grijalva, 19 I&N Dec. 713, 722 (BIA 1988) (advising that in cases involving discretionary relief, all relevant factors concerning criminal history, including arrests not resulting in a conviction, and information contained in police reports should be considered). The particularly troubling aspect of this respondent’s misconduct was his 2011 driving under the influence conviction shortly after he had attended alcohol awareness classes in 2010. As emphasized by the Immigration Judge, the respondent apparently “learned nothing from those classes that he took in 2010” because he was “well aware of the consequences of driving under the influence of alcohol, but chose to get in a car again and put the lives of other people at risk by driving under the influence of alcohol.”
 
*2 As such, we conclude that the respondent was not prejudiced by counsel’s actions and that reopening for further consideration of the respondent’s application for pre-conclusion voluntary departure is not warranted. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (explaining that the party who seeks to reopen proceedings to pursue a discretionary grant of relief from removal bears a “heavy burden” of demonstrating the new evidence presented would likely change the result in the case).
 
Finally, we are unpersuaded by the respondent’s argument that his proceedings should be reopened to allow him to pursue his Form I-589, submitted for the first time with this motion, because of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel (Resp. Br. at 2). As explained above, the respondent has not substantially complied with Matter of Lozada in making his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and nothing in the record suggests that respondent’s counsel was ineffective in not pursuing this form of relief on the respondent’s behalf previously. See Ortiz v. INS, supra, at 1153; Reyes v. Ashcroft, supra, at 596-99. The respondent’s right to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture was fully explained to him at his hearing and he was given two continuances for the purpose of completing those applications (Resp. Mot. at 3; Exh. C; Tr. at 9-10, 14, 17). Accordingly, we see no reason to remand proceedings to allow the respondent to pursue that form of relief. Therefore, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 1208087 (BIA)
End of Document

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Gomez v. Lynch, 5th Cir., August 5, 2016
2015 WL 799939 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMUEL GOMEZ A.K.A. SAMUEL MARTINEZ GOMEZ A.K.A. SAMUEL MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX1 930 - Houston, Texas
January 26, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The proceedings in this matter were last before the Board on August 22, 2014, at which time the respondent’s appeal of the decision of the Immigration Judge of May 17, 2013, was dismissed. That decision pretermitted the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). On November 14, 2014, the respondent filed the timely motion to reopen now before the Board alleging ineffective assistance of prior counsel. The Board may in some cases grant a motion to reopen in order to remedy an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See generally Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-640 (BIA 1988). Nonetheless, in this matter, the respondent has failed to show that but for the assistance rendered, he would have been entitled to relief from removal. In particular, the respondent contends that former counsel erred in conceding that he, the respondent, was removable as charged. However, the respondent has failed to provide to former counsel, and has continued to fail to provide to the Board, appropriate evidence to establish lawful entry. Absent such evidence, the respondent has failed to show the prejudice requisite to establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 (5th Cir. 2001). To the extent that the respondent seeks humanitarian relief to enable him to remain in the United States, such relief is to be pursued with the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). Likewise, any request for relief from removal in the exercise of prosecutorial discretion must also be made to the DHS. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is, hereby, denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 799939 (BIA)
End of Document

2015 WL 799924 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KULWINDER SINGH DHALIWAL
File: AXXX XX9 685 - Detroit, MI
January 20, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul W. Shonk, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathan Goulding
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of India who entered the United States as a conditional permanent resident based on a marriage to a United States citizen. His status was terminated by USCIS, the Immigration Judge denied his good faith waiver, sustained the charge of marriage fraud, and the Board dismissed his appeal. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent also seeks a remand based on his marriage to another United States citizen and a pending I-130 visa petition. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the granting of the motion. The motion is denied.
 
A motion to reopen “shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing . . . .” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The movant must also establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought, INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 319 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988), and satisfy the “heavy burden” of establishing that if the proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
The respondent does not address the requirement that the evidence must not be previously available. Rather, the respondent contends that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel because he did not also pursue an extreme hardship waiver based on his alleged persecution in India, which continued against his family even after he moved to the United States, or adequately prepare him, or his witnesses, for trial (Motion, Exh. A). Many years after the fact, the respondent also argues that his mother should not have been called as a witness because she had dementia. We find no basis to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Contrary to the respondent’s assertions, the record indicates that Mr. Thomas’s representation was thorough and that he was an effective advocate on behalf of the respondent during this proceeding. Mr. Thomas filed a response to the complaint which confirms this view (Notice of Filing Counsel’s Response to Bar Complaint, filed 1/7/15). A review of the record indicates that the respondent had at least three different attorneys in this proceeding before Mr. Thomas entered his appearance (EOIR-28 filed 3/6/08 for Karen Denise Bradley; I.J. Order issued 3/27/08 substituting Candie Clement; I.J. Order issued 4/1/08 substituting David Wenger; I.J. Order issued 2/8/12 substituting Mark Jacob Thomas).
 
*2 An attorney prior to Mr. Thomas prepared and pursued the I-751 waiver with USCIS. It is well-settled that where USCIS has denied a waiver, the respondent may not pursue an alternative waiver under section 216(c)(4) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1186a(c)(4) during proceedings. See Matter of Anderson, 20 I&N Dec. 888 (BIA 1994). Further, the respondent has not presented sufficient evidence of changed circumstances occurring after the filing of the original waiver so that Mr. Thomas would have had notice and been able to seek a continuance. Id.
 
As to the question of extreme hardship, even if Mr. Thomas had pursued an untimely waiver and argued that the alleged extreme hardship arose during the requisite two year period, we do not find that the respondent’s current claims are credible (Motion, Exhs. F and G). The respondent and his father allege that due to the persecution, the respondent was in hiding at a relative’s home prior to leaving India, and his father asserts that he was forced to immigrate soon after due to the continued persecution (Motion, Exh. F at p. 045; Exh. G at p. 048). However, the documents and affidavits filed with USCIS in 2001 within several years after the last incidents of alleged harm make no mention of persecution, harm, hiding, or fear of residing in India.
 
Rather, in his 2001 filings with USCIS (long before Mr. Thomas represented him), the respondent submitted an affidavit under oath that described his courtship in India. He attested to their initial meeting in his family home in India, their visits with her children, their visits and “shopping all over the city” together, looking around for a wedding hall, their ceremony and honeymoon, visits with relatives, his playing with her children, residing at his parents’ house with his new wife, driving to the airport and waiting there until her plane took off, and obtaining his visa and coming to the United States (I.J. Dec. at 3; Exh. 2, Tab B at pp. 61-63). The respondent’s father also submitted a prior affidavit and did not mention the alleged fears or the fact that his son was in hiding (Exh. 2, Tab B at pp. 64-66), which they now claim over 13 years later.
 
Given the inconsistencies between the affidavits filed with the motion long after the alleged events occurred and the affidavits already contained in the record, we find that the respondent’s current claims are not credible. Further, given the representations made by the respondent and his father under penalty of perjury in the 2001 filing with USCIS, there was no reason for Mr. Thomas to inquire further or explore an untimely extreme hardship waiver. Accordingly, we do not find that Mr. Thomas provided ineffective assistance of counsel to the respondent such that proceedings should be reopened and remanded. Nor has the respondent shown the requisite prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
*3 The respondent has a pending I-130 based on his marriage to another United States citizen, but he is not prima face eligible to adjust his status (I.J. Dec. at pp. 27-28 sustaining marriage fraud charge of removability). Section 204(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(c). Further, the respondent filed a document indicating that his conditional permanent resident status has somehow been reinstated. See Notice of Filing dated 11/10/14, Tab A). Therefore, the respondent is not eligible to adjust his status under section 245. Section 245(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(d). The following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 799924 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAJINDER SINGH
File: AXXX XX5 843 - Eloy, Arizona
January 20, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hardeep Rai, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The proceedings in this matter were last before the Board on July 28, 2014. On November 25, 2014, the respondent filed the motion to reopen now before the Board alleging ineffective assistance of prior counsels. The respondent has complied, in part, with the process requisite to asserting such a claim. See generally Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-640 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent has provided detailed affidavits setting forth the bases for his assertion of ineffective assistance of counsel; evidence of fee arrangements and payments both in the United States and abroad to former counsels; evidence of representation by former counsels; bar complaints filed against one of the attorneys in two different jurisdictions, California and Texas; adverse information from the press relating to an individual associated with former counsel who also played a part in the representation of the respondent; and, a press report naming the individual who the respondent met at the detention center who referred him to former counsel. The respondent paid substantial fees to an attorney, referred to him by an individual at the Eloy detention facility, who, with the assistance of other attorneys, appeared in proceedings on behalf of the respondent, but ultimately failed to file an application for relief by the deadline set resulting in the respondent’s inability to seek the relief of asylum in the United States.
 
The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. We accept the respondent’s detailed affidavit, which we accept as true for purposes of this motion (see Najmabadi v. Holder, 597 F.3d 983, 994 (9th Cir. 2010)). Given the circumstances detailed in the respondent’s affidavit, we find that an adequate showing of due diligence has been made, that equitable tolling is appropriate and that reopening should be granted. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672 (9th Cir. 2011). On remand, the respondent shall have the opportunity to apply for any relief for which he may be eligible.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen is granted and the record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
